Declaring Property Haram as an Oath
Hanafi ruling that declaring halal things haram is an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that declaring one's own property haram is an oath requiring kaffarah, and apply it to scenarios of speech, food, and conditional declarations.

Core Idea
Declaring your property haram does not make it haram — it creates an oath requiring expiation.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the commentator expands on the author's statement that declaring one's property haram does not render it forbidden. Instead, such a declaration is legally treated as an oath (yamin). If the person then uses the property in any way, he must pay kaffarah (expiation). The Quranic basis is from Surah al-Tahrim, where Allah rebukes the Prophet for forbidding something lawful and prescribes expiation for oaths. The passage then delves into numerous applications: speech, eating, conditional clauses, and even wine and pork. It also highlights an intra-Hanafi difference on whether eating a morsel of a 'haram' loaf breaks the oath. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to navigate these nuanced rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage begins by stating that declaring one's own property haram does not change its essence; the property remains halal. The declaration is an oath, so if the person uses the property, kaffarat al-yamin becomes wajib.
This is the foundational principle.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
The commentator cites Surah al-Tahrim (66:1-2) to prove that forbidding the lawful is an oath requiring expiation. Note that the verse mentions 'tahillat aymanikum', which the Hanafi school interprets as kaffarah for the oath.
Dalil naqli — the proof is from revelation.
3
Understand the scope of the rule
The passage extends the ruling to various situations: 'your speech is haram upon me', 'entering this house is haram', 'this loaf is haram', etc. It also covers declarations by women and declarations about others' property.
The author notes that 'man' includes males and females.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on partial consumption
Read the discussion on whether saying 'this loaf is haram upon me' is broken by eating a morsel. According to al-Khulasa, any consumption breaks the oath; according to Fatawa Qadikhan, it is akin to swearing not to eat the whole loaf, so only eating the whole loaf breaks it.
The mufta bihi often follows custom and istihsan.
5
Apply the rule to conditional and other special cases
If the declaration is conditional ('if I do X, it is haram'), no immediate oath is created. Also, if one declares wine or pork haram, it is still an oath; intention (niyyah) matters: if intended as news, no kaffarah; if as an oath, kaffarah.
The passage notes that declaring someone else's property haram also creates an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن حرم ملكه لم يحرم) أي لا يصير حراماً عليه لذاته لأنه قلب المشروع وتغييره ولا قدرة له على ذلك بل الله تعالى هو المتصرف في ذلك بالتبديل، وغيره إن استباحه كفر أي عامله معاملة المباح بأن فعل ما . رمه الله فإنه يلزمه كفارة اليمين لقوله تعالى يا أيها النبي لم تحرم ما أحل الله لك ﴾ [التحريم: ١] الآيتين. فبين الله تعالى أن نبيه عليه السلام حرم شيئاً مما هو حلال وأنه فرض له تحلته فعبر عن ذلك بقوله «تحلة أيمانكم» فعلم أن تحريم الحلال يمين موجب للكفارة وما في بعض الروايات من أنه يحلف صريحاً فليس هو في الآية ولا في الحديث الصحيح إلى آخر ما في فتح القدير . ولو ذكر المصنف بدل الملك الشيء بأن قال ومن حرم شيئاً ثم فعله كفر لكان أولى ليشمل الأعيان والأفعال وملكه وملك غيره وما كان حلالاً وما كان حراماً فيدخل فيه ما إذا قال كلامك علي حرام أو معي أو الكلام معك حرام كما في المبتغى . كذا إذا قال دخول هذا المنزل علي حرام ونحوه كما في المجتبى. ولو قال لقوم كلامكم علي حرام أيهم كلم حنث . وفي مجموع النوازل: وكذا كلام فلان وفلان علي حرام يحنث بكلام أحدهما، وكذا كلام أهل ،بغداد وكذا أكل هذا الرغيف علي حرام يحنث بأكل لقمة بخلاف ما لو قال والله لا أكلمهم لا يحنث حتى يكلمهم. وفي الخلاصة : لو قال هذا الرغيف علي حرام حنث بأكل لقمة. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان قال مشايخنا: الصحيح أنه لا يكون حانثاً لأن قوله هذا الرغيف علي حرام بمنزلة قوله والله لا أكل هذا الرغيف، ولو قال هكذا لم يحنث بأكل البعض ا هـ . مع أن حرمة العين والمراد منها تحريم الفعل فإذا قال هذا الطعام علي حرام فالمراد أكله، وكذا إذا قال هذا الثوب علي حرام فالمراد لبسه إلا إذا نوى غيره كما في الخلاصة : ولو قال لدراهم في يده هذ الدراهم علي حرام إن اشترى بها حنث وإن تصدق بها أو وهبها لم يحنث بحكم العرف كما في المحيط وغيره، ولا خصوصية للدراهم بل لو وهب ما جعله حراماً أو تصدق به لم يحنث لأن المراد بالتحريم حرمة الاستمتاع. وفي المحيط : لو قال مالي علي حرام فأنفق منه شيئاً حنث، وكذا مال فلان علي حرام فأكل منه أو أنفق حنث، ويدخل فيه ما إذا قال هذا الطعام علي حرام لطعام لا عليه حرام على الطعام والشراب والفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته من غير نية ومن نذر نذراً مطلقاً أو معلقاً بشرط ووجد وفى به ولو وصل بحلفه إن شاء الله برّ . يملكه فيصير به حالفاً حتى لو أكله حلالاً أو حراماً لزمته الكفارة، إلا إذا قصد به الإخبار عنها وهو لا يدخل تحت عبارة المصنف أيضاً، ويدخل فيه أيضاً ما إذا قال هذه الخمر علي حرام فإذا شربه كفر ففي فتاوى قاضيخان من فصل الأكل الصحيح أنه إذا قال الخمر علي حرام أو الخنزير علي حرام كان يميناً حتى إذا فعله كفر . وذكر في فصل تحريم الحلال : إذا قال هذه الخمر علي حرام فيه قولان والفتوى على أنه ينوّي في ذلك، فإن أراد به الخبر لا تلزمه الكفارة، وإن أراد به اليمين تلزمه الكفارة، وعند عدم النية لا تلزمه الكفارة ا هـ . المصنف بـ«من المفيدة للعموم ليشمل الذكر والأنثى فلذا قال في المجتبى والخلاصة : وعبر قالت لزوجها أنت علي حرام أو قالت حرمتك علي نفسي فيمين حتى لو طاوعته في الجماع أو أكرهها لزمتها الكفارة بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار فأدخل فإنه لا يحنث ا هـ . وقيد بكونه حرمه على نفسه لأنه لو جعل حرمته معلقة على فعله فإنه لا يلزمه الكفارة لما في الخلاصة : لو قال إن أكلت هذا الطعام فهو علي حرام فأكله لا حنث عليه. وفي المحيط وفي المنتقى : إذا قال لغيره كل طعام أكله في منزلك فهو علي حرام ففي القياس لا يحنث إذا أكله ؛ هكذا روى ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف وفي الاستحسان يحنث والناس يريدون بهذا أن أكله حرام. وفي الحيل : إن أكلت عندك طعاماً أبداً فهو حرام فأكله لم يحنث ا هـ . وفي القنية : إن دخلت عليك فما أخذت بيميني فحرام فإن دخل عليه صار يميناً، فإن ملك شيئاً ولو شربة ماء تلزمه كفارة اليمين ا هـ .
His statement: 'Whoever declares his property haram, it does not become haram' — i.e., it does not become forbidden upon him in its essence, because that would be altering divine legislation, and he has no power over that; rather, Allah is the one who changes things. If someone else treats it as permissible, he disbelieves — i.e., treats it as mubah. If he does what... then kaffarat al-yamin is required due to His statement: 'O Prophet, why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful to you?' (Quran 66:1) and the following verses. So Allah informed that His Prophet forbade something lawful, and He prescribed its expiation, expressing that as 'the expiation of your oaths.' Thus it is known that declaring the lawful haram is an oath necessitating kaffarah. What is in some narrations that he swears explicitly is not in the verse or authentic hadith, as in Fath al-Qadir. If the author had said 'whoever declares something haram' instead of 'property,' it would have been better to include objects and actions, his property and others', lawful and unlawful. Thus it includes when he says 'your speech is haram upon me' or 'speaking with me' or 'speaking with you is haram' as in al-Mubtagha. Similarly, saying 'entering this house is haram upon me' and the like as in al-Mujtaba. If he says to a group 'your speech is haram upon me,' he breaks the oath by speaking to any of them. In Majmu' al-Nawazil: similarly 'the speech of so-and-so is haram upon me' — he breaks it by talking to one of them; similarly 'the speech of the people of Baghdad.' Similarly, 'eating this loaf is haram upon me' — he breaks it by eating a morsel, unlike if he says 'by Allah I will not speak to them' — he does not break it until he actually speaks. In al-Khulasa: if he says 'this loaf is haram upon me' he breaks it by eating a morsel. In Fatawa Qadikhan: our scholars said: the correct view is that he does not break it, because his statement 'this loaf is haram upon me' is equivalent to 'by Allah I will not eat this loaf.' If he said that, he does not break it by eating some of it. End quote. Yet the prohibition of the object is intended to prohibit the action. So if he says 'this food is haram upon me,' the intention is eating it. Similarly if he says 'this garment is haram upon me,' the intention is wearing it, unless he intended something else as in al-Khulasa. If he says of dirhams in his hand 'these dirhams are haram upon me if I buy with them,' he breaks the oath if he buys; but if he gives charity with them or gifts them, he does not break by custom as in al-Muhit and others. There is no specificity for dirhams; rather, if he gives away what he declared haram or gives charity with it, he does not break because the intended prohibition is of enjoyment. In al-Muhit: if he says 'my property is haram upon me' and spends from it, he breaks; similarly 'so-and-so's property is haram upon me' — if he eats from it or spends, he breaks. It includes if he says 'this food is haram upon me' for food... not haram upon food and drink, and the fatwa is that his wife becomes... without intention... and whoever makes a vow unconditionally or conditionally and it occurs, he fulfills it, even if he connects it to his oath 'if Allah wills.' He owns it and becomes an oath by it. So if he eats it, whether lawful or unlawful, kaffarah is required, unless he intended to inform about it, and that does not fall under the author's wording. It also includes if he says 'this wine is haram upon me' and then drinks it: he disbelieves. In Fatawa Qadikhan, from the chapter on eating: the correct view is that if he says 'wine is haram upon me' or 'pork is haram upon me,' it is an oath, so if he does it, he disbelieves. And it is mentioned in the chapter on declaring lawful things haram: if he says 'this wine is haram upon me,' there are two opinions, and the fatwa is that he intends (niyyah) in that. If he intended it as information, no kaffarah; if he intended it as an oath, kaffarah; and if he had no intention, no kaffarah. End quote. The author used 'man' because it is general to include male and female. Hence they said in al-Mujtaba and al-Khulasa: if a wife said to her husband 'you are haram upon me' or said 'I have made you haram upon myself,' it is an oath, such that if she voluntarily or coerced submits to intercourse, kaffarah is upon her. Unlike if he swore not to enter this house and then was carried in, he does not break the oath. End quote. And he restricted it to declaring his own property haram, because if he made its prohibition contingent on his action, no kaffarah is required, as in al-Khulasa: if he says 'if I eat this food, it is haram upon me' and then eats, there is no break. In al-Muhit and al-Muntaqa: if he says to someone 'every food I eat in your house is haram upon me,' by qiyas he does not break if he eats; thus Ibn Sama'a narrated from Abu Yusuf; by istihsan he breaks, and people mean by this that his eating is haram. In al-Hiyal: 'if I ever eat food with you, it is haram' — he eats and does not break. End quote. In al-Qunyah: 'if I enter upon you, then whatever I take with my right hand is haram' — if he enters, it becomes an oath. If he owns something, even a sip of water, kaffarat al-yamin is required. End quote.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the author's statement 'wa man harrama milkahu lam yahrum' (whoever declares his property haram, it does not become haram). The commentator explains that such a declaration does not actually change the nature of the property; it remains legally permissible because only Allah has the power to make things haram or halal. The act of declaring one's property haram is treated as an oath (yamin) for which kaffarah (expiation) is required if the property is used. The Quranic proof is from Surah al-Tahrim (66:1-2), where Allah rebukes the Prophet for forbidding something lawful and prescribes expiation for oaths. Thus, declaring the lawful haram is a form of oath. The passage then explores several sub-rulings: (1) the general principle covers speech, entering houses, eating, etc.; (2) if one says 'your speech is haram upon me,' breaking it requires kaffarah per each instance of speech; (3) there is a difference of opinion regarding whether saying 'this loaf is haram upon me' is an oath that breaks by eating any part; the preferred view in Fatawa Qadikhan is that it is analogous to swearing not to eat it, so only eating the whole loaf breaks it, but the earlier sources (Khulasa) hold that any morsel suffices; (4) the intended prohibition is of the action (eating, wearing) not the object itself; (5) if one declares a specific property haram and then gives it away or gives charity, he does not break the oath because the prohibition was on personal enjoyment; (6) the ruling applies equally to declaring wine or pork haram — it is an oath, and if one consumes them, he disbelieves (actually 'kafara' here means he commits an act that incurs kaffarah, not literal disbelief); (7) the author's use of the word 'man' is comprehensive, including women, so a wife's statement 'you are haram upon me' is an oath requiring kaffarah if she submits to intercourse, even under coercion; (8) the ruling is restricted to declaring one's own property haram; if one makes a conditional declaration ('if I do X, it becomes haram'), it does not create an oath until the condition occurs; (9) regarding food eaten in another's house: by qiyas (Abu Yusuf's view) no oath is broken, but by istihsan it is broken because common usage indicates an intention to prohibit that act. (10) Finally, if one says 'if I enter upon you, then whatever I take with my right hand is haram,' and then enters, it becomes an oath; if he subsequently acquires anything, even water, he must pay kaffarah. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that custom and intention play a key role in determining the scope of these oaths.

Key Terms
تحريم الحلال (taḥrīm al-ḥalāl): Declaring something permissible as forbidden, which constitutes an oath (yamin) requiring kaffarah.
يمين (yamīn): An oath that, if broken, requires kaffarah (feeding ten poor people, clothing them, or freeing a slave).
كفارة اليمين (kaffārat al-yamīn): Expiation for breaking an oath: feeding ten poor people, clothing them, or freeing a slave; if unable, three days of fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person says 'this loaf is haram upon me' and then eats a small piece, according to the view in al-Khulasa?
A) No kaffarah because he did not eat the whole loaf.
B) He breaks the oath and must pay kaffarah.
C) The loaf becomes haram.
D) He must fast as expiation.

Key Takeaway
Declaring one's own property haram does not change its legal status but constitutes an oath requiring kaffarah upon any use, with details varying by intention and custom.

Inclusivity of 'Man' in Oath Formulations
Expansion on 'man' generality and oath consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife declares her husband haram upon herself and apply the distinction between voluntary and coerced acts in oath violations.

Core Idea
The word 'man' is general, including both sexes—a wife's 'haram' declaration is an oath, not divorce.

This lesson focuses on a subtle linguistic point with major legal consequences: the word 'man' in oath formulations includes both males and females. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq illustrates how a wife's statement 'anta 'alayya haram' is treated as an oath (yamin) requiring kaffarah if she subsequently has intercourse, even under coercion. This contrasts with cases where the oath-breaker has no control, like being carried into a house.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic tool
The author uses 'man' (من) to express generality. Recognise that in Hanafi usul, 'man' is a general term covering all persons, male and female.
This is an expansion probe—understanding the basis for the ruling.
2
Locate the applied ruling
The passage then applies this generality: a wife's statement 'anta 'alayya haram' is treated as an oath. Determine the hukm: it is a yamin, not talaq or dhihar.
The ruling is from al-Mujtaba and al-Khulasa.
3
Determine the consequence
If the wife submits to intercourse (willingly or coerced), kaffarah is wajib upon her. This shows that the oath is binding and the act of intercourse triggers expiation.
Contrast with being carried into a house—no hanth because no voluntary action.
4
Distinguish the exception
The passage adds an exception: if a person swears not to enter a house and is carried in, he does not break the oath. Understand the illa: lack of volition.
This highlights that the wife's submission, even under coercion, is still an act she can control—unlike being carried.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعبر المصنف بمن الخ) قال في النهر : أنت خبير بأن في شمول كلامه لذلك نظيراً بيناً المصنف بـ«من المفيدة للعموم ليشمل الذكر والأنثى فلذا قال في المجتبى والخلاصة : وعبر قالت لزوجها أنت علي حرام أو قالت حرمتك علي نفسي فيمين حتى لو طاوعته في الجماع أو أكرهها لزمتها الكفارة بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار فأدخل فإنه لا يحنث ا هـ .
His statement: (And the author expressed with 'man' etc.) He said in al-Nahr: You are aware that the inclusiveness of his speech in that has an evident parallel. The author used 'man' because it is general, to include male and female. Hence they said in al-Mujtaba and al-Khulasa: If a wife said to her husband, 'You are haram upon me,' or said, 'I have made you haram upon myself,' it is an oath, such that if she willingly or under coercion submits to intercourse, the kaffarah is incumbent upon her. Unlike if he swore not to enter this house and then was carried in, he does not break the oath. End quote.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This short passage comments on the author's choice of the word 'man' in the base text phrase 'man harrama milkahu' (whoever declares his property haram). The commentator from al-Nahr notes that 'man' is general and includes both males and females. Therefore, the rulings apply equally to women. The implication is that if a wife says to her husband, 'You are haram upon me,' it constitutes an oath (yamin). Consequently, if she then has intercourse with him, whether willingly or under coercion, she must pay kaffarah. This contrasts with a case where a person swears not to enter a house and then is carried in: he does not break the oath because he did not enter voluntarily. The distinction underscores that the wife's submission to intercourse is treated differently because she has control over her compliance.

Key Terms
من (man): A relative pronoun used generally to include all persons, male and female.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a wife says to her husband 'anta 'alayya haram' and then voluntarily has intercourse with him?
A) She commits sin but no kaffarah is required.
B) She is divorced.
C) Kaffarah is required from her.
D) The husband must pay kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
A wife's declaration 'anta 'alayya haram' is an oath, and kaffarah becomes wajib upon her if she subsequently has intercourse.

The Scope of ‘Everything is Haram’ as an Oath: Zufar vs. Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of the oath 'kullu shay'in 'alayya haram'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'kullu shay'in 'alayya haram' as an oath, distinguishing between the qiyas position of Imam Zufar and the istihsan position, and apply the role of custom and intention.

Core Idea
The phrase 'everything is haram' is an oath on food and drink by custom, not on all acts, unless intention includes more.

When someone declares 'everything is haram upon me,' is it a literal oath binding on every action? The Hanafi school engages in an important intra-madhhab dispute on this question. This lesson unpacks the positions of Imam Zufar and the majority, showing how custom (urf) and the principle of istihsan restrict the literal scope to food and drink, while intention can expand it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The phrase 'kullu shay'in 'alayya haram' constitutes an oath (yamin) in Hanafi law. The ruling: breaking it by eating or drinking triggers wajib kaffarah.
The phrase is not a literal declaration of prohibition but a legally binding oath.
2
State the strict qiyas position (Zufar)
Imam Zufar holds that by qiyas, any permissible act directly performed (e.g., breathing) contradicts the apparent generality, thus breaking the oath.
Zufar's qiyas treats all direct actions as analogous to eating/drinking.
3
State the istihsan position (majority)
The majority rules that the oath is confined to eating and drinking by custom (urf), because the intended purpose (birr) of restraint would be defeated by literal universality.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the school.
4
Analyze the role of custom and intention
Custom determines the default scope (food/drink). However, if the person intends women or other matters, the oath extends accordingly. Without intention, wife is not included.
Intention (niyyah) can override custom but must be explicit.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The istihsan position is adopted in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and is the operative ruling for fatwa. The practical consequence: only eating/drinking (or intended acts) break the oath.
Zufar's view is marjuh (weak) but recognized as a valid scholarly opinion.

Source Text

قوله : (كل) حل علي حرام فهو على الطعام والشراب والقياس أن يحنث كما فرع لأنه باشر فعلاً مباحاً . التنفس ونحوه وهذا قول زفر وجه الاستحسان أن المقصود وهو البر لا وهو يحصل مع اعتبار العموم وإذا سقط اعتباره ينصرف إلى الطعام والشراب للعرف فإنه يستعمل فيما يتناول عادة فيحنث إذا أكل أو شرب ولا يتناول المرأة إلا بالنية فلا يحنث بجماع . قوله : (وفي الاستحسان يحنث قال في النهر : وعلى هذا فيجب أن يحنث في قوله إن أكلت طعاماً بأكله ا هـ . ومثله في الفتح قوله : (تقتضي أن الأمر موقوف على النية الخ) الضمير في «تقتضي» راجع إلى عبارة ،الحاكم وفي كونها تقتضي ذلك نظر فإن قوله وإن لم تكن له نية فهو يمين يكفرها الخ» معناه أنه يمين على الطعام والشراب كما أفاده ما قبله من قوله فإن نوى يميناً الخ» فصار حاصله أنه إن نوى اليمين أو لم ينو شيئاً فهو يمين يكفرها ولا تدخل امرأته إلا أن ينويها فإن أكل أو شرب حنث، وإن كان نوى المرأة وقربها سقط الإيلاء لأنه حنث وهذا كله مستفاد من عبارة الهداية أيضاً. نعم في عبارة الحاكم زيادة وهي: لو نوى به الطلاق أو نوى به الكذب فهو كما نوى. وليس في الهداية ما ينافي ذلك فلا مخالفة بين العبارتين إلا في زيادة حكم لم تصرح به عبارة الهداية كما لا
His statement: (Everything) is haram upon me, so it applies to food and drink. By qiyas, he would break the oath even by doing a permissible act like breathing. This is the statement of Zufar. The basis of istihsan is that the intended purpose is abstinence (birr), and it cannot be achieved if the literal generality is considered. So when considering it, it refers to food and drink by custom, because it is commonly used for what is normally consumed. So he breaks the oath if he eats or drinks, but it does not include women except by intention, so he does not break by intercourse. His statement: (And in istihsan, he breaks). He said in al-Nahr: Based on this, it is necessary that he breaks the oath in his statement 'If I eat food' by eating it. End quote. And similar is in al-Fath: his statement: (It requires that the matter is dependent on intention, etc.) The pronoun in 'tastadhi' refers to the statement of al-Hakim. And there is in this requiring that it is dependent on intention a consideration. Indeed, his statement: 'And if he had no intention, it is an oath for which he pays kaffarah' means it is an oath regarding food and drink as indicated by what preceded: 'If he intends an oath, etc.' So the conclusion is that if he intends an oath or does not intend anything, it is an oath for which he pays kaffarah, and his wife is not included unless he intends her. So if he eats or drinks, he breaks; and if he intended his wife and approached her, the oath of ila' is waived because he broke it. All this is derived from the expression of al-Hidayah as well. Yes, in the expression of al-Hakim there is an addition: if he intended divorce or falsehood, it is as he intended. There is nothing in al-Hidayah contradicting that, so there is no conflict between the two expressions except an additional ruling not stated in al-Hidayah, as... incomplete.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines the legal effect of the phrase 'kullu shay'in 'alayya haram' (everything is haram upon me). The commentary opens by stating that this phrase is an oath that primarily applies to eating and drinking. It then presents a point of intra-Hanafi dispute. By strict qiyas (analogical reasoning), a person would break the oath by any permissible act, including breathing, since he is directly engaging in an action. This is the position of Imam Zufar (d. 158 AH), a major student of Abu Hanifa. However, the relied-upon Hanafi position (istihsan) is different: the intended purpose of such an oath is to impose a restriction (birr), and if taken literally universally, the oath would be so burdensome that the purpose would be defeated. Therefore, by istihsan (juristic preference), the scope is limited to food and drink, based on custom ('urf). Thus, the oath is only broken by eating or drinking. It does not include intercourse unless that was specifically intended. The passage then cites al-Nahr, which elaborates that under istihsan, if one says 'if I eat food' and then eats it, he indeed breaks the oath. This is equivalent in al-Fath. Then attention turns to the role of intention (niyyah). The passage quotes al-Hakim's statement: 'It requires that the matter is dependent on intention.' The commentator notes that this is a point of scrutiny. Al-Hakim's wording suggests that if the person has no specific intention, the oath is still binding on food and drink, so kaffarah is due. The wife is not included unless specifically intended. If he intended the wife and approached her, the oath of ila' (abstinence from intercourse) is lifted because he broke it. This is consistent with al-Hidayah. Al-Hakim adds a detail: if he intended by his statement divorce or falsehood, it is as he intended. This additional ruling is not contradictory to al-Hidayah; it simply supplements it. The passage ends abruptly, but the key point is that intention and custom play a defining role in interpreting such sweeping oath formulations.

Key Terms
القول بزفر (al-qawl bi-Zufar): The position attributed to Imam Zufar that general oaths are broken by any action, including permissible ones.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): A juristic preference that sets aside strict analogy in favor of custom or ease, used here to restrict the oath's scope.
العرف (al-ʿurf): Customary practice that determines the intended meaning of general phrases in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (istihsan), what action will break an oath when someone says 'kullu shay'in 'alayya haram'?
A) Any action, including breathing.
B) Only eating and drinking.
C) Only intercourse.
D) Any action except breathing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets the sweeping oath 'everything is haram' as limited to food and drink by custom, and intention can expand its scope.

Rulings on Absolute and Conditional Vows
Explains Hanafi ruling on vows: absolute vs conditional and effect of intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on absolute and conditional vows and distinguish when expiation (kaffarah) is an alternative to fulfilling the vowed act.

Core Idea
A conditional vow with an undesired condition is like an oath — choose to fulfill or expiate.

This passage from a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq explores the nuances of vows (nadhr) in Hanafi fiqh. While the general rule requires fulfillment of any vowed act, a detailed view distinguishes based on intention behind the condition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the texts correctly and avoiding unnecessary hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of vows
Distinguish between an absolute vow (munjaz, immediate) and a conditional vow (mu'allaq, suspended on a condition).
The hadith mentioned applies to both types.
2
Recognize the general ruling
The hadith 'Whoever makes a vow and specifies something must fulfill what he specified' makes fulfillment wajib for all vows.
This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Note the retraction and detail
Abu Hanifa reportedly retracted and agreed with Muhammad that for conditional vows with an undesired condition, expiation suffices.
This is the sahih (correct) view according to later authorities.
4
Apply the criterion of intention
If the condition is desired (e.g., cure of patient), the vow is devotional and must be fulfilled. If undesired (e.g., speaking to Zayd as a deterrent), it is oath-like.
The presence of 'ma'na al-yamin' (meaning of an oath) changes the ruling.
5
Reconcile the two hadiths
The command to fulfill applies to absolute/desired-condition vows; the hadith 'kaffarat al-nadhr kaffarat al-yamin' applies to undesired-condition vows.
This reconciliation harmonizes the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومن نذر نذراً مطلقاً أو معلقاً بشرط ووجد وفى به أي وفي بالمنذور لقوله عليه السلام من نذر وسمى فعليه الوفاء بما سمى وهو بإطلاقه يشمل المنجز والمعلق ولأن المعلق بالشرط كالمنجز عنده أطلقه فشمل ما إذا علقه بشرط يريد كونه أو لا. وعن أبي حنيفة أنه رجع عنه فقال : إن فعلت كذا فعلي حجة أو صوم سنة أو صدقة ما أملكه أجزأه عن كفارة يمين. وهو قول محمد ويخرج عن العهدة بالوفاء بما سمى أيضاً إذا كان شرطاً لا يريد كونه لأن فيه معنى اليمين وهو المنع وهو بظاهره نذر فيتخير ويميل إلى أي الجهتين شاء بخلاف ما إذا كان شرطاً يريد كونه كقوله إن شفى الله مريضي لانعدام معنى اليمين فيه. قال في الهداية : وهذا التفصيل هو الصحيح وبه كان يفتي اسماعيل الزاهد كما في الظهيرية . وقال الولوالجي : مشايخ بلخ وبخارى يفتون بهذا وهو اختيار شمس الأئمة، ولكثرة البلوى في هذا الزمان وظاهر الرواية كما في المختصر للحديث المتقدم. ووجه الصحيح حديث مسلم «كفارة النذر كفارة اليمين» وهو يقتضي السقوط بالكفارة مطلقاً فتعارضا فيحمل مقتضى الإيفاء بعينه على المنجز أو المعلق بشرط يريد كون وحديث مسلم على المعلق بشرط يريد كونه لأنه إذا علقه بشرط لا يريده يعلم منه أنه لم يرد كونه المنذور حيث جعله مانعاً من فعل ذلك الشرط مثل دخول الدار وكلام زيد لأن تعليقه حينئذ لمنع نفسه عنه بخلاف الشرط الذي يريد كونه إذا وجد الشرط فإنه في معنى المنجز ابتداء فيندرج في حكمه
His statement: 'Whoever makes a vow, absolute or conditional upon a condition, and it occurs, he fulfills it' – i.e., he fulfills what was vowed, because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever makes a vow and specifies something must fulfill what he specified.' This includes, by its generality, the immediate and the conditional, and because the conditional is like the immediate in his view. He made it general, so it includes when he attaches it to a condition that he intends to occur or not. And from Abu Hanifa that he retracted and said: 'If I do such-and-such, then I owe Hajj, or fasting for a year, or charity of what I own, it suffices for expiation of an oath.' This is the saying of Muhammad. And one is released from liability by fulfilling what was vowed as well, if it is a condition that he does not intend to occur because in it there is the meaning of an oath, which is prevention. It is, in its outward form, a vow, so he has a choice and may incline to whichever side he wishes, unlike the case where it is a condition that he intends to occur, such as saying 'If Allah cures my patient', because the meaning of an oath is absent. In al-Hidayah it is said: 'This detail is correct, and Ismail al-Zahid used to give fatwa according to it, as in al-Zahiriyyah.' Al-Walwaliji said: 'The scholars of Balkh and Bukhara give fatwa by this, and it is the choice of Shams al-A'imma.' Due to the frequency of incidents in this age, and the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) as in al-Mukhtasar according to the hadith mentioned previously. The evidence for the correct view is the hadith of Muslim: 'The expiation for a vow is the expiation of an oath.' This would necessitate falling (of the obligation) by expiation absolutely, but they conflict (with the general command to fulfill). So the requirement of fulfillment of the specific thing is applied to the immediate vow or the conditional with a condition he intends to occur, and the hadith of Muslim is applied to the conditional with a condition he does not intend to occur, because if he attaches it to a condition he does not intend, it is known that he did not intend the vowed act itself; rather he made it a deterrent from doing that condition, like entering a house or speaking to Zayd, because his attachment then is to prevent himself from it, unlike the condition he intends to occur; when the condition occurs, it is like an immediate vow from the beginning, so it falls under its ruling.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling on vows (nadhr), specifically the difference between absolute (munjaz) and conditional (mu'allaq) vows, and the effect of the intention behind the condition. The text begins by citing the matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq: whoever makes a vow, whether absolute or conditional, must fulfill it when the condition occurs. The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever makes a vow and specifies something must fulfill what he specified.' This general ruling covers both immediate and conditional vows. However, the text reports a retraction from Abu Hanifa (and endorsed by Muhammad al-Shaybani) that if a conditional vow is attached to a condition that the person does not intend to occur (e.g., 'If I speak to Zayd, I owe Hajj'), it is treated like an oath by Allah: the person may choose either to fulfill the vow or to pay expiation (kaffarah yamin). This is because such a vow carries the meaning of an oath (prevention of the condition). The condition itself is unwanted, so the vow is essentially a deterrent oath. In contrast, if the condition is something the person wants to happen (e.g., 'If Allah cures my patient'), there is no oath-like meaning, and the vow must be fulfilled as specified; expiation is not an option. The text notes that this detailed view is considered correct (sahih) by later Hanafi authorities, including the author of al-Hidayah, Ismail al-Zahid, and the scholars of Balkh and Bukhara. It is the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani. The apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) from Imam Muhammad's works supports the general obligation of fulfillment without the option of expiation, based on the general hadith. However, the correct view (sahih) harmonizes the two hadiths: the command to fulfill applies to absolute vows or conditional vows with desired conditions, while the hadith allowing expiation applies to conditional vows with undesired conditions. This reconciliation is based on the intent of the vow-maker: if the condition is a deterrent, the vow is oath-like; if the condition is desired, the vow is a true devotional act.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship (like Hajj, fasting, charity) that is not already obligatory. In this text, it includes both absolute (immediate) and conditional (suspended on a condition) vows.
معلق بشرط (mu'allaq bi-shart): A vow that is conditioned upon the occurrence of a future event, e.g., 'If Allah cures my patient, I will fast for a month.'
كفارة اليمين (kaffarat al-yamin): Expiation for breaking an oath: feeding or clothing ten poor people, or freeing a slave, or fasting three days. In this context, a conditional vow with an undesired condition may be expiated like an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the detailed (sahih) Hanafi view on conditional vows, if a person says 'If I speak to Zayd, I owe Hajj' intending to prevent himself from speaking to Zayd, what is the ruling when he subsequently speaks to Zayd?
A) He must perform Hajj exactly as vowed.
B) He may either perform Hajj or pay expiation (kaffarah) for breaking an oath.
C) The vow is void because he did not intend the condition to occur.
D) He must perform Hajj and also pay expiation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows expiation (kaffarah) for a conditional vow when the condition is not intended, treating it as an oath.

The Final Fatwa on Vows: Retraction Overrides Zahir al-Riwayah
Critique of the detailed distinction's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi mufta bihi on conditional vows is absolute takhyeer, and evaluate the role of the Imam's retraction in overriding Zahir al-Riwayah.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's retraction seven days before his death makes the Nawadir position the final madhhab.

In the previous lesson, you learned the detailed distinction: the view that a conditional vow with an intended condition requires fulfillment, while an unintended condition gives a choice. But is that truly the final madhhab? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq critiques that distinction and argues that the actual mufta bihi is absolute takhyeer, because Imam Abu Hanifa himself retracted his earlier position. This lesson will guide you through the evidence and the reasoning that settled the fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the two competing positions
Identify the detailed distinction (fulfillment for intended conditions, choice for unintended) and the absolute takhyeer (choice for all conditional vows). Know which is in Zahir al-Riwayah and which in Nawadir.
Zahir al-Riwayah: fulfillment always. Nawadir: choice always.
2
Identify the authority conflict
Zahir al-Riwayah is normally more authoritative than Nawadir. Here, scholars face a conflict: which do we follow?
3
Weigh the retraction (ruju')
The key is that Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier position to the Nawadir view seven days before death. That retraction elevates the Nawadir position to the final madhhab, making the earlier position like abrogated.
Al-Shurunbulali, al-Sarakhsi, and others rely on this.
4
Analyze the counter-arguments
Some maintain the detailed distinction as the view of the verifying scholars. The passage refutes this by noting the retraction and quoting many later muftis who adopted absolute takhyeer.
See the list: Ismail al-Zahid, Shams al-Aimma, al-Qadi al-Marwazi, etc.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The passage concludes that the correct fatwa is on absolute takhyeer, with the retraction being the decisive factor. Note the nuance: some limit it to conditions not intended, but the preponderant view is absolute.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب الإيفاء . ثم اعلم أن هذا التفصيل وإن كان قول المحققين فليس له أصل في الرواية لأن المذكور في ظاهر الرواية لزوم الوفاء بالمنذور عيناً منجزاً كان أو معلقاً. وفي رواية النوادر : وهو مخير فيهما بين الوفاء وبين كفارة اليمين قال في الخلاصة : وبه يفتي. فتحصل أن الفتوى على التخيير مطلقاً ولذا اعترض في العناية على تصحيح الهداية ا هـ. وأراد بقوله وفي إنه يلزمه الوفاء بأصل القربة التي التزمها لا بكل وصف التزمه لما تأمل قوله : (وفي رواية النوادر وهو بخير فيهما ظاهر سياق كلام المؤلف أن ضمير «فيهما» عائد على المنذور المنجز والمعلق مطلقاً وبذلك يظهر قوله أن هذا «التفصيل أي الذي صححه في الهداية لا أصل له في الرواية، ويحتمل أن يعود الضمير على قسمي المعلق أعني المعلق بشرط يريد كونه أو لا يريد كما حمله عليه في النهر. وعلى كل فهو مخالف لما في الفتح فإنه بعد ما ذكر القول الأول والقول الثاني الذي صححه في الهداية قال : والأول وهو لزوم الوفاء به عيناً هو المذكور في ظاهر الرواية والتخيير عن أبي حنيفة في النوادر وكذا ذكر في العناية فإنه بعد ما ذكر رجوع أبي حنيفة إلى التخيير فيما لا يريد كونه وأنه قول محمد قال : وهذا مروي عن أبي حنيفة في النوادر. في النهر بعد سوقه كلام الخلاصة قال في البحر فتحصل أن الفتوى على التخيير مطلقاً. وأقول : وضع في الخلاصة في التعليق بالشرط الذي لا يراد كونه فالإطلاق ممنوع أعني سواء أريد كونه أو لا والله تعالى الموفق ا هـ كلام النهر. وبه ظهر أن قوله إن هذا التفصيل ليس له أصل في الرواية غير مسلم. وقوله ولذا اعترض في العناية على تصحيح الهداية أي حيث قال وفيه نظر لأنه إن أراد حصر الصحة فيه من حيث الرواية فليس بصحيح . لأنه غير ظاهر الرواية، وإن أراد من حيث الدراية لدفع التعارض فالدفع ممكن من حيث حمل أحدهما على المرسل والآخر المعلق من غير تفرقة بين ما يريد كونه وما لا يريد كونه. وأجاب الشرنبلالي منتصراً لما في الهداية بأن . الصحة رجوع الإمام إليه لأنه رجع إليه قبل موته بسبعة أيام فصار هو الصحيح لأن المرجوع عنه لا يقاوم المرجوع إليه في الصحة لأن الذي استقر أمر المجتهد ورأيه عليه صار هو المذهب للإمام فيصير المسطر عنه في ظاهر الرواية كالمنسوخ بما بعده، ولا يكون ما أراده الأكمل إلا إذا تقابل ظاهر الرواية والنوارد وتعارضا غير رجوع عن إحدى الروايتين، أما معه كما بينا فلا ولهذا أفتى بما في النوادر اسماعيل الزاهد ومشايخ بلخ وبعض مشايخ بخاری و اختاره شمس الأئمة والقاضي المروزي. وقال في البزازية وعليه الفتوى وقال في الفيض والمفتى به ما رويناه عن أبي حنيفة من رجوعه. وكذا اختاره الصدر الشهيد وفي الخلاصة : واختاره السرخسي والصدر الشهيد وبه يفتي . وقد جعله متناً في مجمع البحرين وصححه، وكذا صححه الزيلعي وتمامه في رسالته المسماة بتحفة التحرير، وبين فيها أيضاً أن ما رجع إليه الإمام هو التخيير في صورة التعليق بما لا يراد كونه، وإن قول الهداية «وهذا إذا كان شرطاً لا يريد كونه وكذا قول ابن الهمام واختار المصنف والمحققون أن المراد بالشرط الشرط الذي لا يريد كونه ليس معناه أن ما رجع إليه الإمام شامل لذلك وللشرط البحر الرائق / ج ٤/ م ٣٢
The obligation of fulfillment. Then know that this detailed distinction, even if it is the saying of the verifying scholars, has no basis in the transmission, because what is mentioned in the apparent narration is the necessity of fulfilling the vowed act itself, whether unconditional or conditional. And in the Nawadir narration: He is given a choice between them (fulfillment and expiation). Al-Khulasa said: And with it fatwa is given. So it becomes clear that fatwa is on absolute choice. Therefore, al-Inayah objected to the correction of al-Hidayah. And he meant by his saying 'and fulfill' that he is obliged to fulfill the essence of the act of worship he committed to, not every description he committed to. Consider his words: (And in the Nawadir narration: he is given a choice between them). The apparent context of the author's speech is that the pronoun 'them' refers to the vowed act, unconditional and conditional absolutely. And thereby his statement appears that this 'detailed distinction' which al-Hidayah corrected has no basis in the transmission. And it is possible that the pronoun refers to the two types of conditional: the conditional on a condition he intends to occur or not, as al-Nahr interpreted. And in any case, it is contrary to what is in al-Fath, for after he mentions the first saying and the second saying which al-Hidayah corrected, he said: The first, which is the necessity of fulfilling it itself, is what is mentioned in the apparent narration, and the choice is from Abu Hanifa in al-Nawadir. And likewise mentioned in al-Inayah: after mentioning Abu Hanifa's retraction to choice regarding what he does not intend to occur, and that it is the saying of Muhammad, he said: And this is narrated from Abu Hanifa in al-Nawadir. In al-Nahr after presenting the speech of al-Khulasa, he said in al-Bahr: So it becomes clear that fatwa is on absolute choice. And I say: In al-Khulasa it is placed regarding the attachment to a condition that is not intended to occur, so absolute statement is not allowed, meaning whether he intends it to occur or not. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And from this it appears that his statement that this detailed distinction has no basis in transmission is not acceptable. And his statement: Therefore, al-Inayah objected to the correction of al-Hidayah. That is, where he said: There is consideration because if he intended to restrict correctness to it in terms of transmission, it is not correct because it is not from the apparent narration, and if he intended in terms of knowledge to resolve the conflict, the resolution is possible by carrying one on the unconditional and the other on the conditional without distinction between what he intends to occur and what he does not. And al-Shurunbulali responded, supporting what is in al-Hidayah, that correctness is due to the Imam's retraction to it because he retracted to it seven days before his death, so it became correct because the retracted view cannot compete with the one retracted to in terms of correctness, because what the mujtahid's opinion settled on became the madhhab of the Imam, so what is recorded from him in the apparent narration becomes like abrogated by what came after. And it is not what al-Akmal meant except when the apparent narration and nawadir meet and conflict without retraction from one of the two narrations. But with retraction as we have shown, it is not. For this reason, Ismail al-Zahid, the scholars of Balkh and some scholars of Bukhara gave fatwa with what is in al-Nawadir, and Shams al-Aimma and al-Qadi al-Marwazi chose it. And it is said in al-Bazzaziyyah: Fatwa is upon it. And it is said in al-Fayd: The mufta bihi is what we narrated from Abu Hanifa of his retraction. Likewise al-Sadr al-Shahid chose it, and in al-Khulasa: al-Sarakhsi and al-Sadr al-Shahid chose it, and with it fatwa is given. And he made it a text in Majma' al-Bahrayn and corrected it, and likewise al-Zayla'i corrected it, and its completion is in his treatise called Tuhfat al-Tahrir, and he also explained therein that what the Imam retracted to is choice in the case of attachment to what is not intended to occur, and that the saying of al-Hidayah 'This is if the condition is not intended to occur' and likewise the saying of Ibn al-Humam and the author and the verifying scholars that what is meant by the condition is the condition which is not intended to occur, does not mean that what the Imam retracted to includes that and the condition. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, volume 4, page 32.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the detailed distinction presented in the previous lesson. The author, al-Bahr, first notes that even though the detailed distinction is the view of the verifying scholars, it has no basis in the established transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah), which requires fulfillment of the vow in all cases. However, in the Nawadir narration, Abu Hanifa is reported to have given a choice (takhyeer) between fulfillment and kaffarah. Al-Khulasa states that fatwa is given according to this. Therefore, the fatwa is on absolute choice for any conditional vow. Al-Inayah objected to al-Hidayah's correction of the detailed distinction. The author then presents the defense by al-Shurunbulali: because Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier position seven days before his death, that retraction becomes the correct madhhab, and the recorded positions in Zahir al-Riwayah are like abrogated. Numerous scholars, including Ismail al-Zahid, Shams al-Aimma, and others, gave fatwa with the Nawadir narration. The passage concludes by clarifying that the retraction specifically applies to the case where the condition is not intended to occur, not to all conditional vows, and this is the correct understanding of al-Hidayah's statement.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The most authoritative transmitted positions in the Hanafi school, found in the six books of Imam Muhammad. Here, it requires fulfillment of all vows.
نوادر (nawadir): Rare transmissions from Abu Hanifa, not part of the six core books. Here, it contains the ruling of takhyeer.
رجوع (ruju'): Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction from his earlier position on vows to the position of takhyeer, reported to have occurred seven days before his death.
تخيير (takhyeer): The choice given to the one who made a conditional vow to either fulfill the vowed act or pay kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason the fatwa on vows shifted to absolute takhyeer (choice) rather than the detailed distinction?
A) The hadith from Muslim was stronger than the earlier hadith.
B) Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier position seven days before his death.
C) The scholars of Balkh and Bukhara had a local custom.
D) The detailed distinction was found to be logically inconsistent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa on conditional vows is absolute takhyeer (choice) because Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction to that view makes it the final madhhab, overriding both Zahir al-Riwayah and the detailed distinction.

Dispute on Fulfilling Vows vs. Option
Establishes the fatwa on option despite Zahir al-Riwayah's obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on vows, explain the transmission basis for each, and identify the operative fatwa with its tarjih mechanism.

Core Idea
The Hanafi fatwa gives the vow-maker a choice even though the core texts require fulfillment.

Vows (nudhur) are serious commitments in Islamic law, but what happens when circumstances change or the vow becomes burdensome? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq navigates a classic Hanafi conflict between the transmitted ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah—obligation of fulfillment—and a rare narration giving an option to pay expiation. The resolution hinges on the authority of Abu Hanifa's retraction and the practice of later scholars, making this a model case in Hanafi usul al-iftaa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position (obligation to fulfill the vow specifically) and the Nawadir position (option between fulfillment and kaffarah yamin).
Note that both positions are attributed to Abu Hanifa, but at different stages of his ijtihad.
2
Analyze the transmission sources
Distinguish Zahir al-Riwayah (core six books) from Nawadir (rare transmissions). Understand that the former carries higher authority unless the latter is supported by the Imam's retraction.
3
Examine the tarjih mechanism
See how later scholars weighed the two: Abu Hanifa retracted the obligation ruling seven days before his death, making the option his final view. Scholars like al-Shurunbulali argued this retraction abrogates the earlier transmission.
Also note the support from numerous later authorities (Ismail al-Zahid, Shams al-A'immah, etc.).
4
Compare with opposing school
Contrast the Hanafi option with the Shafi'i view that vows are binding without expiation. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in fatwa.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Given a conditional vow (e.g., 'If I recover, I will fast a month'), determine the Hanafi ruling: the person may fulfill or pay kaffarah.
Remember that some later scholars restricted the option to cases where the condition was not intended to occur.

Source Text

فيهما بين الوفاء وبين كفارة اليمين قال في الخلاصة : وبه يفتي. فتحصل أن الفتوى على التخيير مطلقاً ولذا اعترض في العناية على تصحيح الهداية ا هـ. وأراد بقوله وفي إنه يلزمه الوفاء بأصل القربة التي التزمها The chapter of oaths. The obligation of fulfillment. Then know that this detail, even if it is the statement of the verifiers, has no basis in transmission because what is mentioned in the apparent transmission is the necessity of fulfilling the vowed thing specifically, whether immediate or conditional. And in the narration of al-Nawadir: he has a choice between fulfillment and the expiation of an oath. He said in al-Khulasa: and this is issued as fatwa. So it is concluded that the fatwa is on option absolutely. For this reason there was an objection in al-Inayah to the correction in al-Hidayah. End. Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage addresses a central dispute in the Hanafi school regarding the nature of vows (nudhur). The author begins by stating that although the detailed position (distinguishing between types of vows) is the view of the verifiers, it has no basis in the transmission (riwaya). The Zahir al-Riwayah (the core transmitted texts) states that one must fulfill the vowed act specifically, whether the vow is immediate or conditional. In contrast, a narration from al-Nawadir (rare transmissions) reports that Abu Hanifa gave a choice between fulfillment and expiation (kaffarah yamin). Al-Khulasa states that the fatwa is given on the latter. The commentary then dissects the pronoun 'fihima' and discusses whether the option applies to all vows or only to conditional ones where the condition is not intended to occur. Various authorities are cited: al-Inayah objects to the ruling in al-Hidayah, al-Nahr argues that al-Khulasa restricted the option to vows with a condition not intended to occur, while al-Fath maintains that the first position (obligation) is the Zahir al-Riwayah. The response by al-Shurunbulali defends al-Hidayah by noting that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier view seven days before his death, making the retracted position the relied-upon one. Therefore, the ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah is effectively abrogated. Numerous later scholars, including Ismail al-Zahid and the authorities of Balkh and Bukhara, adopted the option view. The passage concludes that the correct position is that the option applies to conditional vows where the condition is not intended to occur, but the fatwa is on option absolutely according to many.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat).
النوادر (al-Nawadir): Rare transmissions beyond the six core books, often attributed to Hasan ibn Ziyad or other narrators.
التخيير (al-takhyeer): The option given to the vow-maker between fulfilling the vow or paying the expiation of an oath (kaffarah yamin).
المنذور (al-mandhur): The vowed act or thing that a person commits to as a religious obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because Zahir al-Riwayah is inherently unreliable.
B) Because Abu Hanifa retracted the obligation position before his death.
C) Because all Hanafis today only follow Nawadir.
D) Because the Quran explicitly allows expiation for vows.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a vow-maker has the choice between fulfilling the vow or paying kaffarah, based on Abu Hanifa's retraction.

Conditions for the Validity of Vows (al-Nadhr)
List and explanation of conditions for valid vow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four (or five) conditions for a valid vow in the Hanafi school and apply them to classify given vows as valid or invalid.

Core Idea
A valid vow must not be sin, have a wajib counterpart, be purposeful worship, and not already wajib.

Vows (nadhr) are serious commitments that become binding under specific conditions. The Hanafi school sets clear criteria to ensure the vow is a valid act of worship and not a means of sin or futility. This lesson examines the four conditions from the classical texts, along with an added fifth condition concerning ownership, and illustrates them with examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four core conditions
From the passage, extract the four conditions: (1) not a sin in essence, (2) has a wajib counterpart in the same type, (3) that wajib is a purposeful worship (ibadah maqsudah), (4) not already wajib upon the person before the vow.
Memorize these four as the foundation.
2
Apply condition 1: sin in essence vs. sin incidental
If the vowed act is sinful in itself (e.g., drinking alcohol), the vow is invalid. However, if the sin is due to an external factor (e.g., fasting on Eid al-Adha), the vow is valid because the act of fasting is not sinful in essence; the sin is in breaking the fast, not the vow itself.
The passage uses 'yawm al-nahr' (Eid al-Adha) as an example.
3
Apply condition 2: must have a wajib counterpart
The vowed act must be of a type that has a corresponding wajib act in Shariah, such as prayer (wajib: five daily prayers), fasting (wajib: Ramadan), charity (wajib: zakat). For example, vowing to give charity is valid because zakat is wajib.
Acts that are purely voluntary (mandub) have no wajib counterpart; vowing them is invalid.
4
Apply condition 3: must be purposeful worship (ibadah maqsudah)
The vowed act must be worship intended for its own sake, like prayer, fasting, Hajj, zakat. Acts like wudu, visiting the sick, walking to mosque are not purposeful worship, even though they are good deeds. Therefore, vowing to perform wudu before every prayer is not valid according to the preferred Hanafi view.
There is an internal Hanafi debate whether this condition applies to the vowed act itself or its type; the passage clarifies it applies to the vowed act itself.
5
Apply condition 4: not already wajib before the vow
If the vowed act is already obligatory (e.g., the obligatory Hajj, Ramadan fast), the vow does not create an additional obligation. Vowing to perform the obligatory Hajj (Hajjat al-Islam) only obliges the original Hajj, not an extra one.
The passage warns that vowing something already wajib is fruitless.
6
Consider the fifth condition: vowed item must be owned
A vow involving property of another is invalid because it entails sin against that person's right. However, the passage notes this sin is not in the essence of the vow but due to another's right. If someone vows 'I give this sheep' that belongs to another, the vow is not valid; but if he says 'I will give it' with an oath intention, it becomes an oath, not a vow.
This condition is added from the discussion to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

We have presented that if he specifies a dirham, a poor person, or a place for charity or prayer, then the specification is not binding. We have presented the branches of vows regarding prayer and at the end of fasting, and that its conditions are four: that it not be a sin in itself, thus the vow to fast on the day of Nahr is valid because it is for another reason; that there be an obligatory (act) of its type; that that obligatory act be a purposeful worship; and that it not be obligatory upon him before the vow, so if he vows the Hajj of Islam (the obligatory Hajj), nothing else is binding upon him. Through this it is known that the absolute statement of the author in the place of restriction in al-Khulasa: if he commits by vow more than he owns, he is obliged to what he owns is the chosen view, as when he says 'If I do such and such, then a thousand dirhams of my wealth are charity' and he does it while he owns only one hundred, he is only obliged to the one hundred because what he does not own is not owned. Similarly, it is not valid if added to its cause, so it is not valid like his saying 'my wealth is charity for the poor' and he has no wealth, so likewise this is in al-Wilwaliya. Also in al-Khulasa: if he says 'by Allah, I owe to give this sheep as a gift' and it is the property of another, the vow is not valid unlike his saying 'I will give it as a gift' and if he intends an oath, it is an oath. Therefore, a fifth condition must be added: that what he commits to not be the property of another, unless it is said that vowing it is a sin but not a sin in itself but because of the right of another. And in al-Khulasa: if he says 'by Allah, I owe feeding the poor' it is upon what he intends to happen, and that in al-Hidayah he chose to specify it to what he does not intend to happen... Commentary
This passage details the conditions (shurut) for the validity of a vow (nadhr) in the Hanafi school. The author first reminds of a previous ruling that specifying a particular item (dirham, poor person, place) in a vow is not binding – the vow is still valid but the specific designation is not required. Then he presents the four conditions from earlier treatments (in prayer and fasting): (1) The vow must not be a sin in itself; e.g., vowing to fast on Eid al-Adha (yawm al-nahr) is valid because the sin is due to the day, not the act itself. (2) The vowed act must be of a type that has an obligatory counterpart in the Shariah; e.g., prayer, fasting, charity – because the vow makes it mandatory. (3) That obligatory counterpart must be a purposeful (maqsudah) act of worship, not a mere means like wudu. (4) The act must not already be obligatory upon the person before the vow; e.g., vowing to perform the obligatory Hajj (Hajjat al-Islam) does not create an additional obligation. The author then discusses additional rulings: if someone vows to give more wealth than he owns, he is only obliged to what he owns at the time of the vow, because a vow of something not owned is invalid. Similarly, if he vows to give a specific item that belongs to another person, the vow is not valid because it involves another's property. A fifth condition is then proposed: that the vowed item not be the property of another. The text also addresses the dispute about whether the condition 'purposeful worship' applies to the vowed act itself or to its type; the preferred view is that the vowed act itself must be a purposeful worship. Examples include that vowing to perform wudu, visit the sick, or build a mosque is not valid because these are not purposeful acts of worship, even though they are good deeds. The passage concludes with a discussion of the difference between continuous and non-continuous forms of the verb in vows.

Key Terms
عبادة مقصودة (ibadah maqsudah): An act of worship that is intended for its own sake, such as prayer, fasting, Hajj, zakat, not merely a means (like wudu) or a preparatory act.
النذر (al-nadhr): A solemn promise to perform an act of worship for Allah, which becomes binding when made.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specifying a particular item or person in a vow; such specification is not binding according to this passage, the vow is fulfilled by any substitute of similar nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid vow according to the Hanafi conditions discussed?
A) 'I vow to fast on the first day of Ramadan every year.'
B) 'I vow to perform wudu before every prayer.'
C) 'I vow to give $500 from my savings to the mosque.'
D) 'I vow to build a school if I become rich.'

Key Takeaway
A vow is valid only if it fulfills four conditions: not sin, of a type with a wajib counterpart, that wajib is purposeful worship, and not already wajib; additionally, the vowed item must be owned.

Conditions for a Valid Vow (Nadhr)
Listing conditions for a valid nadhr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for a valid nadhr according to the Hanafi school and explain the additional fifth condition regarding ownership of another's property.

Core Idea
A valid vow must be upon an act that is not inherently sinful, of an obligatory kind, independent worship, not already obligatory, and the vowed item must not be another's property.

Vows (nadhr) are serious commitments in Islam. The Hanafi school establishes clear conditions to ensure that a vow is valid and binding. This lesson extracts those conditions from a passage of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a major Hanafi commentary. Understanding these conditions helps you avoid making invalid vows and know what obligations are truly binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conditions
Locate the four conditions listed at the beginning of the passage: (1) not a sin by essence, (2) there exists an obligatory act of its kind, (3) that obligatory act is an independent worship, (4) not already obligatory before the vow.
These are the core conditions from the matn.
2
Understand the rationale for each
For each condition, note why it is necessary. For example, condition 1 excludes inherently sinful acts, condition 2 ensures the vowed act has a basis in Shari'ah obligation, condition 3 excludes auxiliary acts like wudu, condition 4 prevents adding a second obligation to an already obligatory act.
The passage gives examples for condition 1 and 4.
3
Analyze the addendum on property
Read the discussion from al-Khulasa: if someone vows more than he owns, only what he owns is binding. Also, vowing a specific item that belongs to another is invalid because it involves the right of another.
This leads to the fifth condition: the vowed property must not be owned by another.
4
Derive the fifth condition
From the invalidity of vowing another's property, deduce that a valid vow requires that the committed item be owned by the vower (or fully in his control).
The text explicitly says 'a fifth condition must be added.'
5
Apply the conditions to examples
Test your understanding by applying the conditions to scenarios: vowing to fast on Eid, vowing to give zakat, vowing to give a friend's car in charity.
Use the conditions to decide validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرائطه أربعة : أن لا يكون معصية لذاته فخرج النذر بصوم يوم النحر لصحة النذر به لأنه لغيره، وأن يكون من جنسه واجب، وأن يكون ذلك الواجب عبادة مقصودة، وأن لا يكون واجباً عليه قبل النذر، فلو نذر حجة الإسلام لم يلزمه شيء غيرها. وبه عرف أن إطلاق المصنف في محل التقييد في الخلاصة : لو التزم بالنذر أكثر مما يملكه لزمه ما يملكه هو المختار كما إذا قال إن فعلت كذا فألف درهم من مالي صدقة ففعل وهو لا يملك إلا مائة لا يلزمه إلا المائة لأنه فيما لم يملك لم يوجد في الملك. كذا لا يصح ولا مضافاً إلى سببه فلم يصح كقوله مالي في المساكين صدقة ولا مال له لا فكذا هذا ؛ في الولوالجية. وفي الخلاصة أيضاً : لو قال الله علي أن أهدي هذه الشاة وهي ملك الغير النذر بخلاف قوله لأهدين ولو نوى اليمين كان يميناً .اهـ. فعلى هذا لا بد أن يزاد شرط خامس وهو أن لا يكون ما التزمه ملكاً للغير إلا أن يقال إن النذر به معصية لكن ليس معصية لذاته وإنما هو لحق الغير
And its conditions are four: that it not be a sin by its essence; thus the vow of fasting on the day of Nahr is valid because it is for something else; and that there be an obligatory act of its kind; and that that obligatory act be an intended act of worship; and that it not be already obligatory upon him before the vow. So if he vows Hajj al-Islam, nothing other than it is binding upon him. And by this it is known that the author's absolute statement in the place of restriction in al-Khulasa: if he commits by vow more than he owns, what he owns is binding upon him, that is the chosen view, just as if he said 'If I do such-and-such, then one thousand dirhams of my wealth is charity' and he does it while he owns only one hundred, only the one hundred is binding upon him because what he does not own does not exist in ownership. Similarly, it is not valid if it is attached to its cause, so it is not valid, like his saying 'My wealth is charity for the poor' while he has no wealth; so likewise this; in al-Walwaljiyya. And in al-Khulasa also: if he says 'By Allah, I must gift this sheep' while it is the property of another, the vow is invalid, unlike his saying 'I will gift it' and if he intends an oath, it is an oath. So upon this, a fifth condition must be added: that what he commits not be the property of another, unless it is said that the vow of it is a sin but not a sin in its essence, rather due to the right of another.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out the conditions for a valid vow (nadhr) according to the Hanafi school. The author states four conditions: (1) The vowed act must not be inherently sinful (e.g., vowing to fast on the day of Nahr—the text claims this is valid because the sin is due to something else, but this is problematic; likely a scribal error). (2) There must exist an obligatory act of the same category (e.g., vowing a voluntary fast is valid because fasting has an obligatory variant like Ramadan). (3) That obligatory act must be an independent act of worship (e.g., vowing to feed the poor is valid, but vowing to perform wudu is not because wudu is not an independent worship). (4) The act must not already be obligatory upon him before the vow (e.g., vowing to perform Hajj al-Islam is meaningless because it is already obligatory). The passage then adds a ruling from al-Khulasa: if someone vows more than he owns, only what he owns is binding. For example, vowing 1000 dirhams while only owning 100 dirhams means only 100 dirhams are owed. The text then discusses vowing something owned by another person: if he says 'By Allah, I must gift this sheep' and the sheep belongs to someone else, the vow is invalid because he cannot dispose of another's property. However, if he says 'I will gift' without a vow formula, it is considered an ordinary promise. From this, a fifth condition is derived: the vowed item must not be owned by someone else, unless the act itself is sinful due to the right of the other (but not inherently sinful).

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow whereby one commits to perform a devotional act for the sake of Allah, making it binding upon fulfillment of a condition or absolutely.
معصية (ma'siyah): An act of disobedience to Allah. The condition requires that the vowed act not be inherently sinful.
عبادة مقصودة (ibadah maqsudah): A standalone act of worship intended for its own sake, e.g., prayer, fasting, charity. Excludes acts that are means to an end, like wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone vows 'I must give this sheep as charity' but the sheep belongs to his neighbor, what is the ruling?
A) The vow is valid and he must buy the sheep from the neighbor.
B) The vow is invalid because the sheep is owned by another.
C) The vow is valid if the neighbor agrees.
D) He must give an equivalent sheep from his own property.

Key Takeaway
A valid nadhr requires five conditions: the act must not be inherently sinful, must have an obligatory counterpart, be an independent worship, not already obligatory, and any vowed property must be owned by the vower.

Imam Abu Hanifa's Retraction on Vows of Ambiguous Wording
Resolving the dispute about whether Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his position on ambiguous vows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the scope of Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction on ambiguous vows and state the operative ruling in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction on ambiguous vows was limited, not general – a critical precision for reliable fatwa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common misunderstanding about Imam Abu Hanifa's reported retraction. Students often assume the retraction applies universally, but the text shows it was confined to a specific type of vow. Understanding the precise scope is essential for muftis and advanced students.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on ambiguous vows
According to zahir al-riwayah, an ambiguous vow is interpreted according to the person's intention. If the intention is unclear, it is not binding.
2
Identify the report of retraction
Al-Walid ibn Aban reported that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted seven days before his death, saying 'One may choose between the author of al-Hidayah and those who agree with him.' This could be misinterpreted.
3
Analyze the limitation
The retraction is specific to the type of vow mentioned in his example (acts he did not intend to occur). It does not give free choice for any vowed act.
4
Contrast general vs. detailed interpretation
Some scholars understood the retraction as general; but the detailed view limits it to that one type. The passage shows only two positions: zahir al-riwayah and the detailed view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling is the detailed view: the retraction does not apply broadly; the ambiguous vow is to be interpreted by intention or remains as per the specific wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة : لو قال الله علي إطعام المساكين فهو على الذي يريد كونه وأنه في الهداية اختار تخصيصه بما لا يريد كونه لأن كلام الإمام خاص بالثاني كما اقتضاه التمثيل بقوله إن فعلت كذا فعلى حجة أو صوم سنة الخ لكن لما كان ظاهر قول حاكي الرجوع شمول المنذور بقوله أخبرني الوليد بن أبان أن الإمام رجع قبل موته بسبعة أيام وقال يتخير بين صاحب الهداية ومن وافقه حكم النوع الذي رجع . الإمام لئلا يفهم أحد شمول الرجوع فيجري التخيير عموماً في كل منذور ا.هـ. وبه ظهر أنه ليس في المسألة سوى القولين ظاهر الرواية والقول بالتفصيل
And in al-Khulasa: if he says 'By Allah, I must feed the poor,' it is according to what he intends it to be. And that in al-Hidayah he chose to restrict it to what he does not intend to occur because the statement of the Imam is specific to the second, as indicated by his example: 'If I do such-and-such, then I owe a hajj or a year of fasting' etc. But since the apparent wording of the narrator of the retraction encompasses the vowed act, by his saying: 'Al-Walid ibn Aban informed me that the Imam retracted seven days before his death and said: One may choose between the author of al-Hidayah and those who agree with him regarding the ruling on the type from which he retracted.' This so that no one understands that the retraction is general, thus the choice would apply universally to every vowed act. (End quote.) And by this it appears that there are only two views in the matter: the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) and the view of detail.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute about the correct ruling when someone utters an ambiguous vow, such as 'By Allah, I must feed the poor' without specifying what exactly. According to al-Khulasa, it is interpreted according to his intention. Al-Hidayah (by al-Marghinani) reports that Imam Abu Hanifa restricted the ambiguous vow to what he did not intend to occur, meaning that if he intended a specific act, it is that act; if he had no intention, it is not binding. There is a report from Al-Walid ibn Aban that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier position seven days before his death and said that one may choose between the view of al-Hidayah and its proponents regarding the type from which he retracted. However, this retraction is limited to that specific type (ambiguous vows which he did not intend to happen) and does not generalize to all vowed acts. Therefore, there are only two positions: the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) and the detailed view that distinguishes based on intention. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies this to prevent misunderstanding that the retraction applies universally.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted ruling from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered the most authoritative basis for fatwa.
رجوع (ruju'): The act of an Imam retracting a previous legal opinion. Here, it refers to Imam Abu Hanifa's reported retraction on ambiguous vows.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the scope of Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction regarding ambiguous vows?
A) It applies to all vowed acts
B) It applies only to vows that the person intended to occur
C) It applies only to the type of vow mentioned in his example
D) It was a complete rejection of his earlier rulings

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction on ambiguous vows is limited to the specific type he had earlier not limited; the operative ruling remains that ambiguous vows are interpreted by intention.
