Rulings on Specific Vow Formulations (Feeding, Freeing, and Sick Person's Vows)
Fragments of juristic rulings on vow wordings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for specific vow wordings (feeding a poor person, freeing a slave, conditional slaughter by a sick person) and identify the condition that makes the vow binding.

Core Idea
The precise wording of a vow determines its obligation; even subtle changes affect enforceability.

The Hanafi school provides detailed rulings on various formulations of vows (nadhr). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents several specific cases, illustrating how wording differences—such as including charity or not—affect whether a vow is binding. By examining these examples, you will learn to distinguish between vows that are legally enforceable and those that are only morally binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act vowed and the wording used
For each case, note the object of the vow (e.g., feeding, freeing, slaughtering) and the exact phrasing (e.g., 'Allah upon me to feed', 'if I recover I will slaughter').
The same act can be vowed with different expressions, leading to different rulings.
2
Determine if the wording indicates a binding obligation
Check if the expression includes 'Allah upon me' (الله علي) or equivalent. Without such binding language, the vow may be a mere promise or not binding.
Istihsan may override qiyas in some cases, as with the feeding vow.
3
Assess if the vowed act is a valid act of worship (qurbah)
The vow must be on a deed that is itself a praiseworthy act (e.g., charity, sacrifice, freeing slave). Acts not inherently worship (e.g., shrouding a deceased) invalidate the vow.
An act that is already obligatory (like sacrifice upon oneself) cannot be vowed again.
4
Check for conditions and substitution
Conditional vows (e.g., 'if I recover') require both the condition and proper wording. Substitution is permitted in some cases, like slaughtering seven sheep instead of a camel.
Substitution is valid only if the intended charity is fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عشرة عند أبي حنيفة . الله علي إطعام مسكين يلزمه نصف صاع من حنطة استحساناً. ولو قال إن فعلت كذا فألف درهم من مالي صدقة لكل مسكين درهم واحد فحنث وتصدق بالكل على مسكين واحد جاز. ولو قال الله علي أن أعتق هذه الرقبة وهو يملكها فعليه أن يفي بذلك ولو لم يف يأثم ولكن لا يجبره القاضي. وفي مجموع النوازل لو قال وهو مريض إن برئت من مرضي هذا ذبحت شاة أو علي شاة أذبحها فبرىء لا يلزمه شيء، ولو قال علي شاة أذبحها وأتصدق بلحمها لزمه، ولو قال الله علي أن أذبح جزوراً وأتصدق بلحمه فذبح مكانه سبع شياه جاز اهـ. وهو يدل على أن مرادهم بالواجب الفرض من قولهم وأن يكون من جنسه واجب لأن الأضحية واجبة وهو الذبح لا التصدق أنه النذر يصح بالذبح من غير تصريح بالتصدق بلحمه وقدمنا في باب الاعتكاف ما يجب فيه التتابع من المنذور، وكذا في أول كتاب الصوم. وفي الولوالجية : لو قال الله علي أن أتصدق بمائة درهم فأخذ إنسان فمه فلم يتم الكلام وهو يريد أن يقول إن فعلت كذا، فالاحتياط أن يتصدق، هذا فرق بين وبين اليمين بالطلاق فإن ثمة إذا وصل الشرط بعد ما رفع يده عن فمه لا يقع الطلاق، والفرق أن الطلاق محظور فيكلف لعدمه ما أمكن وقد أمكن بجعل هذا الانقطاع غير فاصل كما لو حصل الانقطاع بالعطاس، أما الصدقة عبادة فلا يكلف لعدمها. ولو قال إن دخلت الدار فلله علي أن أتصدق مثلاً فدخل لا يلزمه شيء لأن المثل بمنزلة التشبيه وليس في التشبيه إيجاب فلا يجب إلا أن يريد به الإيجاب ولو قال إن فعلت كذا فلله علي أن أكفن الميت أو أن أضحي لا يكون يميناً لأن تكفين الميت ليس بقربة مقصودة، وأما التضحية فلأن التضحية واجبة عليه، ولو قال الله علي ثلاثون حجة كان عليه بقدر عمره اهـ. وأشار بقوله وفى به إلى أنه معين مسمى فلو لم يكن مسمى كقوله إن فعلت كذا فعلي نذر، فإن نوى قربة من القرب التي يصح النذر بها نحو الحج والعمرة فعليه ما نوى لأنه يحتمله لفظه فجعل ما نوی کالمنطوق به وإن لم يكن له نية فعليه كفارة اليمين وكذا إن قال إن كلمت أبي فعلي نذر أو إن صليت الظهر؛ فإن نوى معيناً لزمه وإلا كفر. وفي الولوالجية : وإذا حلف بالنذر شيء) أقول : في البزازية إن عوفيت صمت كذا لم يجب ما لم يقل الله علي. وفي الاستحسان وإن لم يكن تعليقاً لا يجب قياساً أو استحساناً كما إذا قال أنا أحج فلا شيء عليه، ولو قال إن فعلت كذا فأنا أحج ففعل يجب عليه الحج، إن سلم ولدي أصوم ما عشت فهذا وعد . اهـ . فيحتمل أن يكون ما هنا مبنياً على القياس أو على أنه وعد لعدم الفاء الرابطة في قوله «علي شاة أذبحها» تأمل .
Ten [dirhams?] according to Abu Hanifa. 'By Allah, I must feed a poor person' - binding upon him is half a sa' of wheat, by istihsan. And if he says 'If I do such-and-such, then one thousand dirhams of my wealth is charity, one dirham for each poor person,' then he breaks his oath and gives it all to one poor person, it is permissible. And if he says 'By Allah, I must free this slave' while he owns it, he must fulfill it; if he does not, he sins but the judge does not compel him. In Majmu' al-Nawazil: if a sick person says 'If I recover from this illness, I will slaughter a sheep' or 'I owe a sheep to slaughter' and he recovers, nothing is binding; but if he says 'I owe a sheep to slaughter and give its meat in charity,' it is binding; and if he says 'By Allah, I must slaughter a camel and give its meat in charity,' and he instead slaughters seven sheep, it is permissible. This indicates that their meaning of 'obligatory' is 'fard' (obligatory) in their statement 'and that there be an obligatory of its kind' because the sacrifice is obligatory (wajib) and it is the slaughtering, not the charity; indeed, the vow is valid for slaughtering without mentioning charity with its meat. And we presented in the chapter of i'tikaf what requires continuity in what is vowed, and similarly in the beginning of the book of fasting. In al-Walwaljiyya: if he says 'By Allah, I must give charity of one hundred dirhams' and then a person puts his hand over his mouth and he does not complete the speech while intending to say 'if I do such-and-such,' then the precaution is to give charity. This distinguishes between it and an oath of divorce; for there, if the condition is made after he removes his hand from his mouth, divorce does not occur. The difference is that divorce is prohibited, so it is avoided as much as possible, and it is possible to consider this interruption as non-interrupting, just as an interruption by sneezing. But charity is an act of worship, so it is not compelled to avoid it. And if he says 'If I enter the house, then for Allah upon me I must give charity' and he enters, nothing is binding because 'such' is like a simile, and in a simile there is no obligation, so it is not binding unless he intends obligation. And if he says 'If I do such-and-such, then for Allah upon me I must shroud the deceased' or 'I must sacrifice,' it is not an oath because shrouding the deceased is not an intended act of worship; as for sacrifice, it is because it is already obligatory upon him. And if he says 'By Allah, I owe thirty pilgrimages,' he owes according to his age. And he pointed by his saying 'and he fulfills it' to it being specific and named. So if it is not named, like his saying 'If I do such-and-such, then upon me is a vow,' and he intends an act of worship among those that are valid for vows, such as pilgrimage or umrah, then he must do what he intended because his wording bears it, so what he intended is like what is uttered. And if he has no intention, then upon him is the expiation of an oath. Similarly, if he says 'If I speak to my father, then upon me is a vow' or 'If I pray zuhr,' then if he intends a specific thing, it is binding; otherwise, he expiates. In al-Walwaljiyya: and if he swears by a vow... I say: in al-Bazzaziyya, if he says 'If I recover, I will fast such-and-such,' it is not binding unless he says 'By Allah, upon me.' And in istihsan, even if it is not a conditional, it is not binding by qiyas or istihsan, just as if he says 'I will perform Hajj,' nothing is upon him; but if he says 'If I do such-and-such, then I will perform Hajj,' upon doing it, Hajj is binding upon him. 'If my child is safe, I will fast as long as I live' - this is a promise. So it is possible that what is here is based on qiyas or on it being a promise due to the absence of the connecting fa' in his statement 'upon me a sheep to slaughter' - reflect.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage presents a series of specific rulings on various formulations of vows (nadhr) in the Hanafi school. The rulings are derived by istihsan (juristic preference) or qiyas (analogy), and they illustrate how subtle differences in wording affect obligation. Key points: (1) Vowing 'I must feed a poor person' is binding with half a sa' of wheat by istihsan. (2) Vowing a specific number of dirhams for each poor person allows giving all to one poor person. (3) Vowing to free a slave you own must be fulfilled; failure is sinful but not enforceable by the judge. (4) A sick person's conditional vow of slaughter is not binding unless the wording includes giving meat in charity. (5) Vowing to slaughter a camel allows substituting seven sheep. (6) The phrase 'such-and-such' (mithl) does not create obligation unless intention is present. (7) Vowing to shroud the dead is invalid because it is not an independent act of worship. (8) Vowing a number of pilgrimages is binding according to one's ability over a lifetime. (9) An unspecified vow ('upon me is a vow') is interpreted by intention; without intention, expiation of an oath is required. (10) Conditional statements without the word 'Allah' may not be binding unless clearly a vow. The passage ends with a discussion of the importance of the connecting word 'fa' in conditional vows.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows departing from qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling, often based on a stronger evidence or custom.
كفارة اليمين (kaffarat al-yamin): The expiation for breaking an oath: feeding ten poor people, clothing them, or freeing a slave; if unable, fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person says 'If I do such-and-such, then one thousand dirhams of my wealth is charity, one dirham for each poor person' and then gives the entire sum to one poor person?
A) Invalid because each poor person must receive exactly one dirham.
B) Permissible because the total charity amount is given.
C) Binding upon him to redistribute to multiple poor people.
D) The vow is void due to uncertainty in distribution.

Key Takeaway
The binding nature of a vow depends on the precise wording and whether the vowed act is a valid act of worship, and certain vows, like freeing a slave, are binding but not enforceable by a judge.

Ambiguous Vows, Intention, and the Role of Wording in Vows
Detailed rulings on ambiguous vows and the role of intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an unspecified vow ('upon me is a vow') is interpreted by intention, and distinguish between vows that require expiation and those that do not.

Core Idea
When a vow is vague, the intention of the speaker determines the obligation; without intention, expiation of an oath is required.

This passage explores more nuanced aspects of vow law: how ambiguous phrases like 'upon me is a vow' are handled, the role of the connecting word 'fa', and the difference between a vow and a mere promise. The Hanafi school uses intention (niyyah) to resolve ambiguity and stresses that certain acts, like shrouding the dead, are not valid subjects of a vow because they are not independent acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous vow wording
Look for phrases like 'upon me is a vow' (علي نذر) without a specific act stated. Determine if the speaker had a specific intention.
The passage distinguishes between cases where a specific act is named (e.g., 'thirty pilgrimages') and where it is not.
2
Apply the rule of intention (niyyah)
If the speaker intended a specific valid act of worship (e.g., hajj, umrah), that intention is considered as if spoken. If no intention, the vow becomes like an oath, requiring kaffarat al-yamin.
This is a Hanafi principle that gives weight to internal intention over literal wording.
3
Analyze the effect of the connecting word 'fa'
The passage discusses the importance of the particle 'fa' (ف) in conditional vows. Without 'fa', the statement may be considered a mere promise, not a binding vow.
Example: 'If my child is safe, I will fast as long as I live' without 'fa' is treated as a promise (wa'd).
4
Identify vows that are invalid due to subject
Vowing to shroud a deceased or to perform an act that is already obligatory (like sacrifice upon oneself) is invalid. Shrouding is not a qurbah, and an obligatory act cannot be made obligatory again.
The ruling on vowing to sacrifice when sacrifice is already due shows that one cannot vow an already wajib act.

And if he says 'If I do such-and-such, then one thousand dirhams of my wealth is charity, one dirham for each poor person,' then he breaks his oath and gives it all to one poor person, it is permissible. And if he says 'By Allah, I must free this slave' while he owns it, he must fulfill it; if he does not, he sins but the judge does not compel him. In Majmu' al-Nawazil: if a sick person says 'If I recover from this illness, I will slaughter a sheep' or 'I owe a sheep to slaughter' and he recovers, nothing is binding; but if he says 'I owe a sheep to slaughter and give its meat in charity,' it is binding; and if he says 'By Allah, I must slaughter a camel and give its meat in charity,' and he instead slaughters seven sheep, it is permissible. This indicates that their meaning of 'obligatory' is 'fard' (obligatory) in their statement 'and that there be an obligatory of its kind' because the sacrifice is obligatory (wajib) and it is the slaughtering, not the charity; indeed, the vow is valid for slaughtering without mentioning charity with its meat. And we presented in the chapter of i'tikaf what requires continuity in what is vowed, and similarly in the beginning of the book of fasting. In al-Walwaljiyya: if he says 'By Allah, I must give charity of one hundred dirhams' and then a person puts his hand over his mouth and he does not complete the speech while intending to say 'if I do such-and-such,' then the precaution is to give charity. This distinguishes between it and an oath of divorce; for there, if the condition is made after he removes his hand from his mouth, divorce does not occur. The difference is that divorce is prohibited, so it is avoided as much as possible, and it is possible to consider this interruption as non-interrupting, just as an interruption by sneezing. But charity is an act of worship, so it is not compelled to avoid it. And if he says 'If I do such-and-such, then for Allah upon me I must shroud the deceased' or 'I must sacrifice,' it is not an oath because shrouding the deceased is not an intended act of worship; as for sacrifice, it is because it is already obligatory upon him. And if he says 'By Allah, I owe thirty pilgrimages,' he owes according to his age. And he pointed by his saying 'and he fulfills it' to it being specific and named. So if it is not named, like his saying 'If I do such-and-such, then upon me is a vow,' and he intends an act of worship among those that are valid for vows, such as pilgrimage or umrah, then he must do what he intended because his wording bears it, so what he intended is like what is uttered. And if he has no intention, then upon him is the expiation of an oath. Similarly, if he says 'If I speak to my father, then upon me is a vow' or 'If I pray zuhr,' then if he intends a specific thing, it is binding; otherwise, he expiates. 'If my child is safe, I will fast as long as I live' - this is a promise. So it is possible that what is here is based on qiyas or on it being a promise due to the absence of the connecting fa' in his statement 'upon me a sheep to slaughter' - reflect.

Commentary
This passage presents a series of specific rulings on various formulations of vows (nadhr) in the Hanafi school. The rulings are derived by istihsan (juristic preference) or qiyas (analogy), and they illustrate how subtle differences in wording affect obligation. Key points: (1) Vowing 'I must feed a poor person' is binding with half a sa' of wheat by istihsan. (2) Vowing a specific number of dirhams for each poor person allows giving all to one poor person. (3) Vowing to free a slave you own must be fulfilled; failure is sinful but not enforceable by the judge. (4) A sick person's conditional vow of slaughter is not binding unless the wording includes giving meat in charity. (5) Vowing to slaughter a camel allows substituting seven sheep. (6) The phrase 'such-and-such' (mithl) does not create obligation unless intention is present. (7) Vowing to shroud the dead is invalid because it is not an independent act of worship. (8) Vowing a number of pilgrimages is binding according to one's ability over a lifetime. (9) An unspecified vow ('upon me is a vow') is interpreted by intention; without intention, expiation of an oath is required. (10) Conditional statements without the word 'Allah' may not be binding unless clearly a vow. The passage ends with a discussion of the importance of the connecting word 'fa' in conditional vows.

Key Terms
كفارة اليمين (kaffarat al-yamin): The expiation for breaking an oath: feeding ten poor people, clothing them, or freeing a slave; if unable, fasting three days.
نية (niyyah): Intention; in vows, the speaker's internal intent determines the specific obligation when wording is ambiguous.

Hanafi Principle: Oaths Based on Custom
Ruling with proof: establishing and refining the custom principle for oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle for interpreting oaths, apply it to a concrete case, and evaluate the internal Hanafi dispute over its scope.

Core Idea
Oaths are interpreted by custom ('urf), not by literal dictionaries or intentions.

This passage establishes the foundational Hanafi rule that oaths are understood according to common custom. It explains why Hanafis reject the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali approaches and then reveals an internal debate: should custom apply absolutely or only when literal meaning fails? Understanding this principle is key to correctly applying oath rulings in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational principle
Locate the statement 'al-asl anna al-ayman mabniyyatun 'ala al-urf' and note that this is the default Hanafi position.
The principle is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Contrast with other schools
Identify the positions attributed to al-Shafi'i (linguistic reality), Malik (Qur'anic usage), and Ahmad (intention) and note the Hanafi critique.
This shows the distinct Hanafi approach.
3
Explore the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf
Find the two views: some apply custom absolutely (e.g., authors of al-Dhakhira and al-Marghinani), others restrict it when literal meaning is possible.
The commentator criticizes the restriction as undermining the principle.
4
Apply to the spider's house example
Consider the ruling: if one swears not to demolish a 'house' and destroys a spider's web, the absolute custom view says he is guilty; the restricted view might exempt him.
This illustrates the practical difference.
5
Understand the reconciliation
Note the quote from al-Hawi al-Hasiri ('words not purposes') and its reconciliation: it applies judicially, while custom applies in religious matters.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

قوله : (وفي الحاوي الحصيري والمعتبر في الأيمان الألفاظ دون الأغراض) هذا مخالف لما حققه في الفتح ووفق بينهما في الشرنبلالية بقوله ولعله قضاء وما قاله الكمال ديانة فلا مخالفة ا هـ
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths Concerning Entering and Exiting. Chapter on oaths concerning entering, exiting, dwelling, coming, and other things. If one swears not to enter a house, he is not guilty by entering the house, the mosque, the church, the synagogue, or the hallway. Chapter on oaths concerning entering, exiting, dwelling, coming, and other things. Begin: Explanation of the actions about which oaths are sworn. There is no way to enumerate them due to their abundance because they depend on the choice of the doer. So we mention what our scholars have mentioned in their books. What is mentioned is of two types: sensory actions and legal matters. He began with the most important, which is entering and the like, because the state of residing in a place is more binding on the body than eating and drinking. The author mentioned in this chapter five actions: entering, exiting, dwelling, coming, and riding. The principle is that oaths are based on custom according to us, not on linguistic reality as reported from al-Shafi‘i, nor on Qur’anic usage as from Malik, nor on intention as from Ahmad, because the speaker only speaks customary speech, i.e., words intended for their customary meanings. From words absolutely, as . about those placed in custom, just as the Arab, being a native speaker, only speaks linguistic realities. So it is necessary to interpret the speaker’s words according to what is customary as intended. Then among the mashayikh, some followed this absolute approach and judged the branch mentioned by the author of al-Dhakhira and al-Marghinani, which is that if one swears not to demolish a house and then demolishes a spider’s house, he is guilty, considering it a mistake. Some restricted applying speech to custom to cases where the literal meaning cannot be applied. It is obvious that this makes the linguistic reality the standard except for what has no linguistic meaning but was innovated by custom, and whatever has both linguistic and customary meaning is considered according to its linguistic meaning even if spoken by a customary speaker. This demolishes the principle of basing oaths on custom because nothing is considered except language except where impossible, and this is far-fetched, as there is no doubt that the speaker only speaks according to the custom of discourse, whether it is linguistic custom if he is a native speaker or other custom if he is not. Yes, what is used commonly between native speakers and custom is considered language as custom. As for the mentioned branch, the point is that if he intended it in the generality of “house,” he is guilty; if it did not occur to him, he should not be guilty because speech is directed to the customary meaning when the word “house” is used absolutely. It appears that our meaning by directing speech to custom is that if he has no intention, the import of speech is its customary meaning; and if he has an intention and the word can bear it, the oath is contracted accordingly. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Hawi al-Hasiri: “What is considered in oaths are the words, not the purposes.” And in al-Zahiriya from the third chapter of hibah: a man. Chapter on oaths concerning entering, exiting, dwelling, and other things. His statement: (And in al-Hawi al-Hasiri, “What is considered in oaths are the words, not the purposes”) contradicts what was verified in al-Fath. A reconciliation between them is given in al-Sharnaballaliyya by his statement: “Perhaps it is a judicial matter, and what al-Kamal said is a religious matter, so there is no contradiction.” End.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It opens the chapter on oaths concerning entering, exiting, etc., by stating the foundational Hanafi principle: oaths are interpreted according to common custom ('urf), not according to literal linguistic meanings (al-haqiqah al-lughawiyyah) as held by al-Shafi'i, nor according to Qur'anic usage (al-isti'mal al-Qur'ani) as held by Malik, nor according to the swearer's unexpressed intention (al-niyyah) as held by Ahmad. The reasoning is that ordinary speakers use words in their customary, not literal, senses. The text then presents an internal Hanafi dispute. Some Hanafi scholars, like the authors of al-Dhakhira and al-Marghinani, apply this principle absolutely. They rule that if someone swears not to demolish a 'house' and destroys a spider's web, he breaks his oath because 'house' in custom can include a spider's structure. Others restrict the customary interpretation to cases where the literal meaning is impossible. The commentator criticizes this restriction, arguing it undermines the entire custom-based approach. The correct view is to always apply custom, except when the swearer had a specific intention that the word can bear. The passage ends by quoting al-Hawi al-Hasiri: only the uttered words matter, not the secret purposes. A reconciliation is provided: al-Hawi's statement applies in judicial contexts (qada'), while Fath al-Qadir's reference to custom applies in religious matters (diyanah).

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Common usage and customary practice of the speech community; the basis for interpreting oaths in Hanafi law.
اليمين (al-yamin): A legally binding oath, typically invoking Allah's name or a solemn vow, subject to interpretation by 'urf.
الحقيقة اللغوية (al-haqiqah al-lughawiyyah): The literal, dictionary meaning of a word; in oaths, rejected by Hanafis as the default in favor of customary meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how are oaths primarily interpreted?
A) Based on literal linguistic meanings
B) Based on the swearer's unexpressed intention
C) Based on common custom ('urf)
D) Based on Qur'anic usage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted by custom ('urf), not by literal meanings, intentions, or Qur'anic usage.

Oaths on Price: Customary Interpretation for Buyer and Seller
Rulings on interpreting oaths in commercial negotiations according to Hanafi custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a buyer's and a seller's oath regarding price and apply the Hanafi rule of customary interpretation to determine breach.

Core Idea
A buyer's oath on price includes increases; a seller's oath is limited to the exact named price.

In commercial transactions, the Hanafi school interprets oaths according to the customary understanding of the parties. This passage from Mukhtasar al-Jami' and later commentaries illustrates how the same price term ('ten dirhams') can have different meanings for buyer and seller. The buyer intends 'ten or more' (absolute), while the seller intends 'exactly ten' (singular). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly determining when an oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the party swearing
Determine whether the oath is made by the buyer or the seller. The Hanafi ruling differs based on the party's customary intention.
The custom ('urf) of the market is the basis for interpretation.
2
Classify the price term in the oath
The price term (e.g., 'ten') is classified as 'mutlaqah' (absolute) for the buyer and 'mufradah' (singular) for the seller. This classification comes from customary usage.
For the buyer, 'ten' customarily includes any amount from ten upward; for the seller, it means exactly ten.
3
Compare the actual transaction to the oath
Check whether the actual price matches the customary interpretation. If the buyer buys at eleven, it falls within his 'absolute ten' (breach). If the seller sells at eleven, it exceeds his 'exact ten' (no breach).
A seller's purpose is to get more than ten, so selling at eleven fulfills his purpose and does not breach.
4
Apply the general rule: form vs. purpose
The passage states that breach occurs only when both the form (the named act) and the purpose (the intended result) are contravened. If only form or only purpose is contravened, there is no breach.
Example: swearing not to leave by the door and leaving by the roof does not breach because the form (leaving by door) is not contravened.
5
Derive the ruling for the case
Combine the customary interpretation with the form-purpose rule to determine whether the oath is broken.
For buyer: buying at eleven contravenes both form (ten included) and purpose (to not spend ten). For seller: selling at eleven does not contravene form (exact ten) and fulfills purpose (increase).

اغتاظ على غيره فقال إن اشتريت لك بفلس شيئاً فامرأته طالق فاشترى له بدرهم شيئاً لم يحنث في يمينه فدل على أن العبرة بعموم اللفظ اهـ . وذكر الإمام الخلاطي في مختصر الجامع فروعاً مبنية على ذلك فقال باب اليمين في المساومة : حلف لا يشتريه بعشرة حنث بإحدى عشرة، ولو حلف البائع لم يحنث به لأن مراد المشتري المطلقة ومراد البائع المفردة وهو العرف، ولو اشترى أو باع بتسعة لم يحنث لأن المشتري مستنقص والبائع وإن كان مستزيداً لكن لا يحنث بلا مسمى كمن حلف لا يخرج من الباب أولاً بضربه سوطاً أو لا يشتري بفلس أو ليغدينه اليوم بألف فخرج من السطح وضرب بعصا واشترى بدينار وغدى برغيف لم يحنث اهـ. وفي التنوير للإمام المسعودي شارحه والحاصل أنه إذا كان في اليمين ملفوظ به يجوز تعيين أحد محتمليه بالغرض، وأما الزيادة على الملفوظ فلا يجوز بالغرض ففي مسألة لا أبيعه بعشرة فباعه بتسعة إنما لا يحنث البائع وإن كان غرضه المنع عن النقصان لأن الناقص
وسيأتي قريباً توفيق آخر وهو أن حمله على الألفاظ هو القياس وحمله على الأغراض استحسان قوله : (وغدي برغيف لم يحنث) بقي من عبارة مختصر الجامع بقية وهي قوله وغدي برغيف مشتري بألف لم يحنث كذا بتسعة ودينار أو ثوب، وبالعرف يخص ولا يزاد حتى خص الرأس بما يكبس ولم يرد الملك في تعليق طلاق الأجنبية بالدخول. انتهت عبارة الجامع. وقد أوضح هذا المقام الإمام الفارسي في شرحه المسمى «تحفة الحريص شرح التلخيص فنذكره ملخصاً وهو أنه لو حلف المشتري لا يشتريه بعشرة فاشتراه بأحد عشر حنث لأنه اشتراه بعشرة وزيادة والزيادة على شرط لا تمنع الحنث كما لو حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار فدخلها ودخل داراً أخرى ولو حلف البائع لا يبيعه بعشرة فباعه بأحد عشر لم يحنث لحصول شرط بره لأن غرضه الزيادة على العشرة وقد وجد لأن البيع بالعشرة نوعان : بيع بعشرة مفردة وبيع بعشرة مقرونة بالزيادة ففي المشتري اللفظ مطلق لا دلالة فيه على تعيين أحد النوعين فكان مراده العشرة المطلقة، أما البائع فمراده البيع بعشرة مفردة بدلالة الحال إذ غرضه الزيادة عليها ولم يوجد شرط حنثه وهو البيع بعشرة مفردة فلا يحنث وهذا هو المتعارف بين الناس فيحمل اليمين على ما تعارفوه ولو اشتراه المشتري أو باعه البائع بتسعة لم يحنث واحد منهما، أما المشتري فلأنه مستنقص فكان شرط بره الشراء بأنقص من عشرة وقد وجد وأما البائع فإنه وإن كان مستزيداً للثمن على العشرة إلا أنه لا يحنث بفوات الغرض وحده بدون وجود الفعل المسمى وهو البيع بعشرة فلا يحنث. وهذا لأن الحنث إنما يثبت بما يناقض البر صورة وهو تحصيل ما هو شرط الحنث صورة وللحالف في الإقدام على اليمين ،غرض، فإذا وجد الفعل الذي هو شرط الحنث صورة وفات غرضه به فقد فات شرط البر من كل وجه فيحنث، أما إذا وجد صورة الفعل الذي هو شرط في الحنث بدون فوت الغرض أو بالعكس لا يكون حنثاً مطلقاً فلا يترتب عليه حكم الحنث فصار كمن حلف لا يخرج من الباب فخرج من جانب السطح أو لا يضرب عبده سوم رطاً فضربه بعصا أو لا يشتري لامرأته شيئاً بفلس فاشتري شيئاً بدينار أو ليغدين فلاناً اليوم بألف درهم فغداه برغيف مشتري بألف
He became angry with someone and said, “If I buy you something for a fils, my wife is divorced.” Then he bought him something for a dirham; he did not break his oath. This indicates that consideration is given to the generality of the word. End. Imam al-Khilati mentioned in Mukhtasar al-Jami‘ branches built on that. He said: Chapter on oaths concerning negotiation: If one swears not to buy it for ten, and he buys it for eleven, he breaks the oath. But if the seller swears, he does not break it because the buyer’s intent is the absolute (mujarrad) price and the seller’s intent is the single (mufrad) price, which is custom. If he buys or sells for nine, neither breaks the oath because the buyer is decreasing (mustanqis) and the seller, though desiring increase, does not break without the named amount. Like one who swears not to leave by the door and leaves by the roof, or strikes with a whip, or buys for a fils, or feeds today for a thousand; he leaves from the roof, strikes with a stick, buys for a dinar, and feeds with a loaf – he does not break the oath. End. In al-Tanwir of Imam al-Mas‘udi, his commentator: The summary is that if in the oath there is a pronounced (pronounced word), it is permissible to specify one of its probable meanings by purpose. But an increase beyond the pronounced word is not permissible by purpose. So in the issue of “I do not sell it for ten” and he sells it for nine, the seller does not break even though his purpose is to prevent deficiency, because the deficient ... And another reconciliation will come shortly: that interpreting by words is qiyas and interpreting by purposes is istihsan. His statement: (And he fed with a loaf and did not break) Remaining from the wording of Mukhtasar al-Jami‘ is his statement: “And he fed with a loaf bought for a thousand, he does not break; similarly with nine, a dinar, or a garment. And by custom, it is specified and not increased, just as the head is specified with what covers, and the king is not intended in suspending divorce of a foreign woman upon entering.” End of the wording of al-Jami‘. Imam al-Farisi has explained this place in his commentary titled Tuhfat al-Haris Sharh al-Talkhis, so we mention it in summary: That if the buyer swears not to buy it for ten, and he buys it for eleven, he breaks because he bought it for ten plus an increase, and increase on the condition does not prevent breaking, just as if he swore not to enter this house and he entered it and entered another house. But if the seller swore not to sell it for ten, and he sold it for eleven, he does not break because his condition of faithfulness is fulfilled; his purpose is increase over ten, and that has been achieved. Because sale for ten is of two types: a sale for ten alone (mufrada) and a sale for ten paired with an increase. In the buyer’s case, the word is absolute with no indication specifying one of the two types, so his intended meaning is the absolute ten. As for the seller, his intended meaning is sale for ten alone by indication of the situation, because his purpose is increase over it, and the condition of his breaking (sale for ten alone) was not found, so he does not break. This is the commonly understood practice among people, so the oath is interpreted according to their custom. And if the buyer buys or the seller sells for nine, neither breaks the oath. As for the buyer, because he has decreased, so the condition of his faithfulness (buying for less than ten) exists. As for the seller, even though he desired an increase in price over ten, he does not break by mere loss of purpose without the existence of the named act, which is sale for ten. This is because breaking is only established by what contradicts faithfulness in form, i.e., achieving what is the condition of breaking in form. The swearer has a purpose in entering the oath. So if the act that is the condition of breaking in form is found and his purpose is missed thereby, then the condition of faithfulness is completely violated, and he breaks. But if the form of the condition of breaking is found without loss of purpose, or conversely, there is no breaking at all, and the ruling of breaking is not attached to it. Thus it becomes like one who swore not to leave by the door and left from the side of the roof, or not to strike his slave with a whip and struck with a stick, or not to buy something for his wife for a fils and bought something for a dinar, or to feed so-and-so today a thousand dirhams and fed him with a loaf bought for a thousand.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a collection of ruling statements (from Mukhtasar al-Jami‘ and later commentaries) detailing how oaths in commercial contexts are interpreted according to the Hanafi school. The core principle: oaths are interpreted according to the custom of the traders, which distinguishes between the perspectives of buyer and seller. If a buyer swears not to buy something for ten, buying it for eleven breaks the oath because the increase is still a purchase at ten plus extra; the word 'ten' in custom includes 'ten and more' from the buyer's perspective. But if a seller swears not to sell for ten, selling for eleven does not break his oath because his customary purpose is to achieve a price above ten, and the phrase 'for ten' in a seller's oath means exactly ten alone (mufrada). The text analogizes to oaths about entering houses, striking, etc. The later part from Imam al-Farisi provides a systematic reasoning: the distinction hinges on whether the word 'ten' is considered as 'absolute ten' or 'ten alone' based on the party's customary usage. The buyer's oath is interpreted as 'ten or more' because that is the common understanding; the seller's oath is interpreted as 'exactly ten'. The passage also explores cases where the act matches the form but not the purpose (e.g., selling for nine), and concludes that no breach occurs unless both form and purpose are contravened. The last analogy (feeding with a loaf) illustrates that replacing the specified item with an equivalent value does not break the oath.

Key Terms
المشتري (al-mushtari): The buyer; in oath context, his intention is the absolute price including any increase over the named amount.
البائع (al-ba'i‘): The seller; in oath context, his intended meaning is the exact named price without increase.
المطلقة (al-mutlaqah): The absolute (price) – the named amount without qualification, but in buyer's custom includes additional sums.
المفردة (al-mufradah): The singular (price) – the exact named amount, without any increase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller swears not to sell his goods for ten dirhams, and then sells them for eleven dirhams, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because eleven is more than ten
B) He does not break his oath because his customary intent was to get more than ten
C) He breaks his oath because he sold the goods
D) The oath is invalid because the price changed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, oaths about price are interpreted according to custom: a buyer's price term is absolute (includes increases), a seller's is singular (exact amount only).

Oaths in Buying and Selling: Between Literal Wording and Intention
Explaining rulings on buyer's and seller's oaths and the role of custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that literal wording governs oaths unless custom specifies probabilities, and determine when an oath is broken based on violation of literal condition and loss of intent.

Core Idea
An oath is broken only when the literal condition is violated AND the intent is lost; literal wording is primary.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles the delicate balance between literal wording and intention in commercial oaths. Through vivid examples of buyer and seller oaths, the author demonstrates that Hanafi fiqh gives primacy to the literal expression, while custom can specify probabilities but cannot expand the scope. This lesson will walk you through the key rulings and the methodological dispute between qiyas and istihsan underlying them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the oath is about (buying or selling) and the specific condition (e.g., 'not buy for ten'). Then note the actual action taken (e.g., bought for eleven). The ruling is whether the oath is broken (hanth) or not.
The passage gives multiple examples: buyer vs seller, increase vs decrease.
2
Locate the underlying principles
Note that there are two approaches: qiyas (literal wording) and istihsan (purposes). The passage states that interpreting by words is qiyas and by purposes is istihsan. The mufta bihi considers both but gives primacy to literal wording.
Custom can specify probabilities but does not add to the wording.
3
Trace the examples
Apply the ruling to each example: buyer swears not to buy for ten → buys for eleven → breaks. Seller swears not to sell for ten → sells for eleven → does not break. Buyer/seller swears not to buy/sell for ten → does so for nine → neither breaks.
The key is the combination of literal violation and intent loss.
4
Understand the qiyas-istihsan debate
Identify that the preferred position (qiyas) insists on literal wording, while istihsan looks to the purpose. The resolution is that custom specifies the meaning but does not allow addition to the wording.
This is a valid intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Apply to analogous cases
Consider examples like swearing not to go out the door but leaving from the roof, or not to beat with a whip but beating with a stick. In each, the literal condition is not met, so no hanth even if intent is defeated.
This reinforces the primacy of literal wording.

بألف لم يحنث كذا بتسعة ودينار أو ثوب، وبالعرف يخص ولا يزاد حتى خص الرأس بما يكبس ولم يرد الملك في تعليق طلاق الأجنبية بالدخول. انتهت عبارة الجامع. وقد أوضح هذا المقام الإمام الفارسي في شرحه المسمى برغيف مشتري بألف
He became angry at someone and said, 'If I buy for you something for a fals, then my wife is divorced.' So he bought for him something for a dirham, and he did not break his oath. This indicates that the consideration is the general application of the wording. Imam al-Khilati mentioned in Mukhtasar al-Jami' branches based on this, saying: Chapter on oath in bargaining: If he swears not to buy it for ten, he breaks it with eleven. If the seller swears, he does not break it by it, because the buyer's intent is the absolute [ten] and the seller's intent is the singular [ten], which is the custom. If he buys or sells for nine, neither breaks it, because the buyer is decreasing and the seller, even if seeking increase, does not break it without a named thing, like one who swears not to go out of the door but leaves from the roof, or not to beat his slave with a whip but beats with a stick, or not to buy for a fals but buys for a dinar, or not to feed him today with a thousand but feeds him with a loaf bought for a thousand, he does not break. In al-Tanwir by Imam al-Mas'udi, his commentator: The summary: if in the oath there is a word, it is permissible to specify one of its probabilities by intent, but increase over the word is not permissible by intent. So in the issue of not selling it for ten and he sells it for nine, the seller only does not break even if his intent is to prevent deficiency because the deficient... And soon another reconciliation will come: that interpreting it by the words is qiyas and interpreting it by the purposes is istihsan. His words: 'and fed with a loaf, he does not break.' There remains from the expression of Mukhtasar al-Jami' a remainder: his words: 'and fed with a loaf bought for a thousand, he does not break; similarly with nine and a dinar or a garment, and by custom it is specified and not increased, until the head is specified by what is pressed, and ownership is not intended in suspending divorce of a non-wife by entry.' The expression of al-Jami' ends. Imam al-Farisi has explained this topic in his commentary called Tuhfat al-Haris Sharh al-Talkhis, so we will mention it summarized: that if the buyer swears not to buy it for ten, then buys it for eleven, he breaks because he bought it for ten and an increase, and increase on a condition does not prevent breaking, as if he swears not to enter this house, then enters it and enters another house. If the seller swears not to sell it for ten, then sells it for eleven, he does not break because the condition of his obedience is achieved, because his intent is increase over ten and it occurred. For sale by ten is of two types: sale by ten alone and sale by ten coupled with increase. So for the buyer, the word is absolute with no indication specifying one of the two types, so his intent is the absolute ten. As for the seller, his intent is sale by ten alone by indication of the situation since his intent is increase over it, and the condition of his breaking (sale by ten alone) did not occur, so he does not break. This is what is customary among people, so the oath is interpreted according to their custom. If the buyer buys or the seller sells for nine, neither of them breaks. As for the buyer, because he is decreasing, so the condition of his obedience (buying at less than ten) has occurred. As for the seller, even if he is seeking increase in price over ten, he does not break by mere loss of intent without the existence of the named act, which is sale for ten. This is because breaking is established by what contradicts obedience in form, i.e., achieving the condition of breaking in form. The one swearing has an intent in embarking on the oath. If he finds the act which is the condition of breaking in form and his intent is lost by it, then the condition of obedience is lost from every aspect, so he breaks. If he finds the act which is the condition of breaking in form without loss of intent, or vice versa, it is not breaking at all, so no ruling of breaking results. So it becomes like one who swears not to go out of the door but goes out from the side of the roof, or not to beat his slave with a whip but beats with a stick, or not to buy for his wife something for a fals but buys something for a dinar, or not to feed so-and-so today with a thousand dirhams but feeds him with a loaf bought for a thousand.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of rulings on oaths related to buying and selling. The author begins with an illustrative example: a man swears the divorce of his wife if he buys something for someone for a fals (a small coin), then buys it for a dirham (a larger coin). The ruling is that he does not break his oath, because the literal wording of 'fals' was not violated; the dirham is a different coin entirely. This establishes the principle that the literal wording is the primary consideration, not the intention or the value. Then the author quotes Imam al-Khilati, who lists branches based on this: If a buyer swears not to buy something for ten, and buys it for eleven, he breaks his oath because he bought it for ten and more. But if the seller swears not to sell for ten and sells for eleven, he does not break his oath, because his intention is to get more than ten. The custom is that the seller's 'ten' means exactly ten. If either buys or sells for nine, neither breaks the oath: the buyer is decreasing (his intent is to buy for less), and the seller, even though he wants more, does not break because the literal condition was not met. The author then cites examples to illustrate: going out from the roof instead of the door, beating with a stick instead of a whip, buying for a dinar instead of a fals, and feeding with a loaf instead of a thousand dirhams—in all these, the oath is not broken because the literal condition was not fulfilled. The passage then transitions to a discussion of the underlying methodology: interpreting oaths by the literal wording is qiyas (analogy), while interpreting by the purposes is istihsan (juristic preference). Imam al-Farisi's explanation from Tuhfat al-Haris is summarized to clarify that for the buyer, the word 'ten' is absolute and may refer to either ten alone or ten plus increase, but custom indicates the absolute ten for the buyer and the singular ten for the seller. The key distinction is that breaking an oath occurs only when the literal condition is violated and the intent is lost; if the literal condition is not violated, even if the intent is lost, there is no breach. This principle is supported by the examples of leaving from the roof instead of the door, etc. The passage concludes that custom specifies the meaning but does not add to the wording. This is a fundamental lesson in Hanafi fiqh: the primacy of literal wording in oaths, with custom playing a role only in interpretation, not in expanding the scope of the oath.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or other sacred things, creating a legal obligation to fulfill or expiate.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by violating its literal condition, requiring expiation (kaffarah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer swears not to buy an item for ten dirhams, but buys it for eleven dirhams, does he break his oath?
A) No, because he paid more than ten, which is better.
B) Yes, because he bought it for ten and more.
C) No, because the literal wording 'ten' was not exactly met.
D) Yes, because his intention was not to buy at ten or more.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, breaking an oath requires both violation of the literal wording and loss of the underlying intent; custom can clarify probabilities but not expand the scope.

Definition of 'Bayt' in Oaths: Customary Meaning
Ruling that 'bayt' in oaths is interpreted by custom, excluding non-dwellings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'bayt' in oaths and identify which structures are excluded based on custom.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'bayt' means a dwelling prepared for living, not any building—custom defines the word.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that the meaning of 'bayt' in oaths is not literal but based on custom. A person who swears not to enter a 'bayt' does not break the oath by entering a mosque, church, or entrance hall. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle that oaths are interpreted according to 'urf, but custom cannot add to the literal wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is swearing not to enter a 'bayt'. The ruling: does not break the oath by entering a mosque, church, synagogue, entrance hall, shade structure, or portico.
The ruling is negative: no hanth (oath is not broken).
2
Identify the legal principle
Oaths are built on custom ('urf). The meaning of words in oaths is determined by common usage, not literal definitions.
This is a core Hanafi principle in the chapter of ayman.
3
Define the key term
'Bayt' in custom means a dwelling (ma'add li al-baytutah), i.e., a place prepared for sleeping and living.
The source explicitly says: 'البيت في العرف ما أعد للبيتوتة'.
4
Apply the definition to examples
The passage lists structures not considered 'bayt': the Ka'bah, mosque, church, synagogue, entrance hall, shade structure, portico. None are primarily for dwelling.
Even though the Ka'bah is called 'Bayt Allah', custom does not treat it as a dwelling.
5
Note the limitation
Custom specifies the meaning of words but does not allow increasing the scope beyond what is spoken. For example, if one swears not to buy for a dirham, buying for a dinar does not break the oath because 'dirham' does not include 'dinar'.
This is from the response to a potential objection in the passage.

And the zullah (shade structure) and suffah (portico), and regarding a house that is ruined, entering it, and in this house he breaks his oath even if he builds another house afterwards... [missing text] ...away from ten, it is not in his wording nor does his wording bear it, so he is not bound by it. In al-Khilasa from the fifth type of oaths in purchase: If the seller is the one who swore and said, 'My slave is free if I sell this to you for ten,' and he sells it for ten dirhams and a dinar, or for eleven dirhams, he does not break. If he sells it for nine, he also does not break. This is the answer of qiyas. In istihsan, the opposite: custom among people is that whoever swears not to sell for ten means not to sell except for more than ten, so if he sells for nine, he breaks by istihsan. The summary: building the ruling on words is qiyas, and building on purposes is istihsan. It will follow whether custom is considered in speech or action. His words: (If he swears not to enter a house, he does not break by entering the house, the mosque, the church, the synagogue, the entrance hall, the shade structure, and the portico) because we have presented that oaths are built on custom, and 'house' in custom is what is prepared for dwelling, and these places were not built for that. He intended by 'house' the Ka'bah, but if he had expressed that, it would be clearer. Al-biyah (church) is the place of worship of Christians; al-kanisah (synagogue) is the place of worship of Jews; al-dihliz (entrance hall) is what is between the door and the house, a Persian word arabized as in al-Sihah; al-zullah is the canopy that is at the door of the house, a roof with beams, one end on the wall of the door and the other on the wall of the opposite neighbor. We only restrict it because if the zullah... he does not break in all of these, even if his intent in the first is dwelling in the house, in the second is refraining from hurting the slave, in the third is harming the woman and not being kind to her, in the fourth is that what he feeds him is of great value. Similarly, if the buyer buys or the seller sells for nine and a dinar or nine and a garment, he does not break. As for the buyer, because he did not commit to ten for the sold item, and even if he decreased the price from ten, that is an intent, and by intent he is obedient and does not break. As for the seller, due to the absence of the condition of breaking in form, which is selling for ten, with the presence of the condition of his obedience, which is increase over ten, since his intent is increase over it, and by intent obedience is achieved without breaking. His words: 'and by custom it is specified and not increased' is a response to a question: that the intent of the buyer from the oath by custom is decrease from ten, so if he buys it for nine and a dinar or nine and a garment, the decrease did not occur; rather there is increase in quantity and value, so breaking should occur. Likewise, the seller selling for nine alone should break because preventing removal of his ownership for ten is preventing removal for nine alone by custom. The response to the first: that the ruling is not established by mere intent; it is established by the wording. The wording of the buyer does not bear purchase for nine and a dinar or a garment, because the dirham does not bear the dinar or garment. It cannot be made a metonym for purchase at a value of ten by considering intent in custom, because increase is not allowed by custom over what is not in his wording. For this, if he swears not to buy it for a dirham and buys it for a dinar, he does not break. The response to the second: that the spoken word is 'ten' and the request for increase over it is not in the seller's wording, and his wording does not bear it. 'Ten' does not imply 'nine' to be specified by his intent. Increase over the wording by custom is not allowed, unlike purchase for nine, because 'ten' in the buyer's side... if [unclear].

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the definition of 'bayt' (house) in the context of oaths. The author begins by quoting a ruling from al-Khilasa regarding a seller's oath: if the seller swears not to sell for ten, selling for ten and a dinar or eleven does not break the oath according to qiyas, but by istihsan, if he sells for nine, he does break because custom dictates that 'not to sell for ten' means 'not to sell for less than ten'. The author then presents the main ruling: if someone swears not to enter a 'bayt', entering the Ka'bah (the House), a mosque, a church (bi'ah), a synagogue (kanisah), a dihliz (entrance hall), a zullah (shade structure), or a suffah (portico) does not break the oath. The reason is that oaths are built on custom, and 'bayt' in custom means a dwelling prepared for living, not places of worship or auxiliary structures. The author defines each term: dihliz is the passage between door and house, zullah is a canopy over the door, etc. The passage then returns to a discussion of intent and wording, clarifying that even if the swearer's intent is to avoid certain harms (like hurting a slave or being unkind to a woman), the literal wording is not violated. The conclusion is that custom specifies the meaning of words but does not allow increasing the scope beyond what is spoken. This lesson is crucial for understanding how Hanafi fiqh interprets oaths: the literal wording is the starting point, and custom refines its scope but does not override it.

Key Terms
بيت (bayt): In oaths, a house prepared for dwelling (بيتوتة) as per custom; does not include mosques, churches, etc.
عرف (urf): Custom or common usage that determines the meaning of terms in oaths.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath; requires expiation (kaffarah) or fulfillment of the conditional penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Yes, because his intention was not to enter any building.
D) No, because he entered with a valid purpose.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the meaning of 'bayt' in oaths is determined by custom, which restricts it to dwellings, excluding places of worship and auxiliary structures.

Defining 'House' in Entry Oaths: Ruins and Structures
Commentary on matn defining بيت and rulings on entering various places

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'bayt' (house) in the context of entry oaths according to Hanafi custom, distinguish between general and specific oaths regarding ruined houses, and apply the definition to related structures such as porticoes and entrance halls.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'house' is defined by custom—what is prepared for overnight stay; the plot (arsa) retains the name 'dar' even after demolition.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains how to determine whether entering a particular place breaks an oath not to enter a house. The key is the customary definition of 'bayt' as a place prepared for overnight stay (baytutah). The commentator then addresses the tricky case of ruined houses, distinguishing between general oaths ('I will not enter a house') and specific oaths ('I will not enter this house'). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly determining liability for expiation (kaffarah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms and their custom-based definitions
Read the passage and extract the definitions of bayt, dihliz, zulla, siffa, and kharibah. Note how the custom (urf) determines whether a place counts as a 'house' for oath purposes.
The definition pivots on the concept of baytutah (overnight stay).
2
Apply the definition to specific structures listed
Analyze why the mosque, church, synagogue, entrance hall, canopy, and portico do NOT break a general oath, unless they are suitable for overnight stay. Understand the reasoning based on their intended use.
The passage qualifies the ruling for large entrance halls and roofed porticos.
3
Distinguish between general and specific oaths for ruins
Focus on the section on kharibah (ruin). Note the difference: in a general oath ('la adkhulu daran'), entering a ruined plot does NOT break the oath because the term 'dar' customarily implies a building. In a specific oath ('la adkhulu hadhihi al-dar'), entering the same plot DOES break the oath because 'dar' also names the plot (arsa) itself.
The principle of description (wasf) being irrelevant in definite objects unless it motivated the oath is key.
4
Apply the principle of motivating description
Consider the example of swearing not to eat an unripe date and then eating it ripe. The description 'unripe' is adhered to because it motivated the oath. Similarly, for ruins, if the ruinous state was the reason for the oath, the oath may be restricted accordingly.
This principle helps in nuanced cases where the oath-taker had a specific intention.
5
Derive the practical ruling for common scenarios
Synthesize the rules: (1) General oath not to enter a house: broken only by entering a place that is customarily used for overnight stay. (2) Specific oath not to enter this house: broken by entering the plot even if ruined. (3) For structures like porticos, check if they have walls, a roof, and are used for sleeping.
The mufta bihi is that the plot (arsa) retains the name 'dar'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حلف لا يدخل بيتاً لا يحنث بدخول البيت والمسجد والبيعة والكنيسة والدهليز والظلة والصفة) لما قدمنا أن الأيمان مبنية على العرف والبيت في العرف ما أعد للبيتوتة وهذه البقاع ما بنيت لها. وأراد بالبيت الكعبة ولو عبر بها لكان أظهر والبيعة - بكسر الباء - معبد النصارى، والكنيسة معبد اليهود والدهليز بكسر الدال ما بين الباب والدار فارسي معرب كما في الصحاح والظلة الساباط الذي يكون على باب الدار من سقف له جذوع أطرافها على جدار الباب وأطرافها الأخرى على جدار الجار المقابل له وإنما قيدنا به لأن الظلة إذا لم يحنث في الكل... (ثم ينتقل إلى الكلام على الخربة) وأطلق المصنف في الدهليز والصفة وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يصلحا للبيتوتة، أما إذا كان الدهليز كبيراً بحيث يبات فيه فإنه يحنث بدخوله لأن مثله يعتاد بيتوتته للضيوف في بعض القرى وفي المدن يبيت فيه بعض الاتباع في بعض الأوقات فيحنث والحاصل أن كل موضع إذا أغلق الباب صار داخلاً لا يمكنه الخروج من الدار وله سعة تصلح للمبيت من سقف يحنث بدخوله، وعلى هذا يحنث بالصفة سواء كان لها أربع حوائط كما صفاف الكوفة، أو ثلاثة على ما صححه في الهداية بعد أن يكون مسقفاً كما هي صفاف ديارنا لأنه يبات فيه. غاية الأمر أن مفتحه واسع وسيأتي أن السقف ليس شرطاً في مسمى البيت فيحنث وإن لم يكن الدهليز مسقفاً؛ كذا في فتح القدير قوله: (وفي دار بدخولها خربة وفي هذه الدار يحنث وإن بنيت داراً أخرى بعد الانهدام أي في حلفه لا يدخل داراً لا يحنث بدخولها خربة، وفيما إذا حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار فإنه يحنث بدخولها خربة، وإن بنيت داراً أخرى بعد الانهدام لأن الدار اسم للعرصة عند العرب والعجم. يقال دار عامرة ودار غامرة أي خراب وقد شهدت أشعار العرب بذلك والبناء وصف فيها غير أن الوصف في الحاضر لغو والاسم باق بعد الانهدام. وفي الغائب تعتبر وأراد بالخربة الدار التي لم يبق فيها بناء أصلاً، فأما إذا زال بعض حيطانها وبقي البعض فهذه دار خربة فينبغي أن يحنث في المنكر إلا أن يكون له نية؛ كذا في فتح القدير. والأصل أن الوصف في المعين لغو إن لم يكن داعياً إلى اليمين وحاملاً عليها، وإن كان حاملاً عليها تقيدت به كمن حلف أن لا يأكل هذا البسر فأكله رطباً لم يحنث إلا إذا كانت الصفة مهجورة شرعاً فحينئذ لا يتقيد بها وإن كانت حاملة كمن حلف لا يكلم هذا الصبي لا يتقيد بصباه كما سيأتي
His statement: (He swore not to enter a house; he does not break his oath by entering the house, mosque, synagogue, church, entrance hall, canopy, or portico.) Because we have established that oaths are based on custom, and a house in custom is what is prepared for staying overnight, and these places were not built for that. He meant by 'house' the Ka'ba; if he had expressed it, it would be clearer. Al-Bi'a (with kasr of ba) is the place of worship of Christians; al-Kanisa is the place of worship of Jews; al-Dihliz (with kasr of dal) is the space between the door and the house, a Persianized word as in al-Sihah; al-Zulla is the canopy over the door of the house, a roof with beams whose ends are on the wall of the door and the other ends on the wall of the neighbor opposite. We qualified it because if the canopy... (then transitions to discussion on the ruin) The author made absolute in the entrance hall and portico, but it is restricted to when they are not suitable for staying overnight. If the entrance hall is large enough to stay overnight, then he breaks his oath by entering it, because such is customarily used for staying overnight for guests in some villages, and in cities some servants stay there at times, so he breaks his oath. The conclusion is that any place where, if the door is closed, the entrant cannot exit the house, and it has sufficient space with a roof for overnight stay, he breaks his oath by entering it. And on this, he breaks his oath for the portico, whether it has four walls like the porticos of Kufa, or three as corrected in al-Hidayah, provided it is roofed like the porticos of our lands, because one can stay overnight in it. The only thing is that its opening is wide, and it will come that a roof is not a condition for the name 'house,' so he breaks his oath even if the entrance hall is not roofed; so in Fath al-Qadir. His statement: (And in a house, by entering it as a ruin, and in this house, he breaks his oath, even if another house is built after demolition.) That is, in his oath not to enter a house, he does not break his oath by entering it as a ruin. But if he swears not to enter this specific house, he breaks his oath by entering it as a ruin, even if another house is built after demolition, because 'house' (dar) is a name for the plot (arsa) according to Arabs and non-Arabs. It is said 'a thriving house' and 'a desolate house' meaning ruined, and Arab poetry bears witness to that. The building is a description in it, but the description is irrelevant in the present case, and the name remains after demolition. In the absent case, it is considered. He meant by 'ruin' a house in which no building remains at all. As for if some of its walls have fallen and some remain, this is a ruined house; it should be that he breaks his oath in an indefinite oath, unless he has an intention; so in Fath al-Qadir. The principle is that the description in a definite object is irrelevant unless it motivated the oath and caused it; if it was a cause, it is adhered to, like one who swears not to eat this unripe date, then eats it ripe, he does not break his oath, unless the description is legally deprecated, in which case it is not adhered to even if it was a cause, like one who swears not to speak to this boy, he is not restricted by his youth, as will come.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's ruling on oaths regarding entering a house. The commentator begins by defining key terms: بيت (house) is customarily what is prepared for staying overnight; therefore, entering a mosque, church, etc., does not break the oath because they are not built for overnight stay. The Ka'ba is also excluded. The terms بيعة, كنيسة, دهليز, ظلة, and صفة are defined. The discussion then shifts to the ruling on entering a ruined house (خربة). If the oath is general ('la adkhulu daran'), entering a ruined house does not break it because 'dar' normally implies a built house. But if the oath specifies 'this house' (هذه الدار), entering it even as a ruin breaks the oath because the name 'dar' applies to the plot (عرصة) regardless of building. The commentator clarifies that if some walls remain, it is still a ruin, and the oath is broken in an indefinite case unless the swearer had a specific intention. The principle is that descriptions in definite objects are irrelevant unless they motivated the oath.

Key Terms
البيت (al-bayt): In the context of oaths, it is what is customarily prepared for staying overnight (بيتوتة).
الخربة (al-kharibah): A ruined house with no building remaining, but the plot still called 'dar'.
العرصة (al-arsah): The plot of land, the site of the house; it remains 'dar' even after demolition.
الدهليز (al-dihliz): The entrance hall, the space between the door and the house; it does not break a general oath unless large enough for overnight stay.
الظلة (al-zullah): A canopy or shed over a door; not considered a house for oaths.
الصفة (al-siffah): A portico or verandah, usually roofed; can be considered a house if used for sleeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not enter this house' and then enters it after it has been completely demolished (no walls remain), according to the passage, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because 'this house' refers to the plot, which remains.
B) No, because it is no longer a house.
C) It depends on his intention.
D) He breaks his oath only if he rebuilds it first.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the term 'dar' includes the plot (arsa) even after demolition, so a specific oath to 'this house' is broken by entering the empty plot, while a general oath to 'a house' is not.

Oaths Regarding Ruin or Transformation of a House
Commentary on rulings when an oath-bound place is ruined or transformed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath about entering a house or mosque is broken if the place is ruined or transformed, and explain the distinction between specific and general oaths.

Core Idea
An oath on a specific house remains binding on the plot even if the building is gone; a mosque remains a mosque in ruins.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a nuanced issue in the law of oaths: what happens when the place one swore about is physically changed? The author, drawing on earlier Hanafi authorities, explains that the key is whether the underlying identity of the place persists. Understanding this principle is essential for correctly applying oath rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon: entry into a place after it has been ruined or transformed. The ruling depends on whether the oath is specific (mu'ayyan) or general (nakira).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof used: for a specific house, the name 'dar' refers to the plot (arsa) as per Arabic usage; for a mosque, Abu Yusuf defines it linguistically as a place of prostration.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the dalil proves the ruling: the definition of 'dar' as plot means the building is an attribute that can be removed without changing the name; the definition of 'masjid' as place of prostration persists even without a building.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Observe the difference between specific and general oaths: for a specific house, the description (building) is inconsequential unless it motivated the oath; for a general house, the description matters. Also note the intra-Hanafi difference on a ruined mosque.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Identify the relied-upon position: For a specific house, entry into a ruin breaks the oath. For a mosque, the view of Abu Yusuf (that it remains a mosque forever) is mufta bihi.

Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths regarding Entering and Exiting. The demolition, and if it is made a garden, mosque, bath, or a house not like this house, then he demolishes or builds another... it means what is inside the house roofed, for he breaks the oath by entering because he sleeps there. And the author spoke generally about the entrance hall (dehliz) and the portico (suffah), but it is restricted to when they are not suitable for overnight stay, but if the entrance hall is large enough to sleep in, then he breaks the oath by entering... The summary: any place which, when the door is closed, becomes interior such that one cannot exit the house, and it has sufficient space suitable for sleeping with a roof, he breaks the oath by entering... And upon this, he breaks the oath by the portico... and it will come that the roof is not a condition for the name 'house', so he breaks the oath even if the entrance hall is not roofed. His statement: (And regarding a house, by entering it as a ruin, and in this specific house he breaks the oath even if another house is built after the demolition)... The origin is that the description in a specified (object) is inconsequential if it was not a motivator for the oath... And the author indicated that if he swore not to enter this mosque, and it was demolished and became a desert, then he entered it, he breaks the oath, and this is narrated from Abu Yusuf... His statement: (And if it is made a garden, mosque, bath, or a house not like this house, he demolishes or builds another)... It is an explanation of three issues: First, if he swore not to enter this house, and it became ruined and then made a garden, mosque, bath, or house, he does not break the oath by entering it... Second, if he swore not to enter this house, and he entered it after it was demolished, he does not break the oath... Third, if he swore not to enter this house, and it was demolished and another built, then he entered it, he does not break the oath... Then analogies with a pillar, wall, pen, scissors, etc. And the query about dividing the house...
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning oaths related to places that undergo physical change. The passage type is an expansion probe, providing detailed rulings and reasoning. The author first discusses the definition of a 'house' (bayt) and the conditions for breaking an oath when entering a place that is roofed and suitable for sleeping. He then distinguishes between a general oath not to enter 'a house' (nakira) and a specific oath not to enter 'this house' (mu'ayyan). For a specific house, entering it even in a ruined state breaks the oath because the name 'dar' remains, as the term refers to the plot of land (arsa) among Arabs, and the building is merely an attribute. For a general oath, the description (building) is considered, so entering a ruined house does not break the oath. The text also covers the case of a mosque: if it is demolished and becomes an empty plot, entering it still breaks the oath because a mosque is defined as a place of prostration, which persists even in ruins. This is the fatwa position from Abu Yusuf. The core of the passage is the 'three issues' (thalath masa'il) concerning a specified house: (1) if it is transformed into a garden, mosque, etc., the oath is not broken because the name changes; (2) if it is completely demolished, the oath is not broken for a house (bayt) because the name is lost, but for a house (dar), it depends on whether the walls remain and the roof falls; (3) if it is demolished and another building constructed on the site, the oath is not broken because the new building is a different entity. The author extends this principle to other objects like a pillar, wall, pen, and scissors: if the essential identity changes due to destruction or transformation, the oath is not broken. Finally, a query is addressed about a person who divides the house with someone else and gains a right of passage: he does not break the oath by entering because he does not enter the whole house. The commentary emphasizes that the ruling hinges on whether the oath is on the specific entity (ayn) or on the name/description, and whether the change is substantial or merely superficial.

Key Terms
خربة (kharibah): A ruined house with no remaining building, or with only partial walls, as distinguished from an intact house.
الوصف في المعين لغو (al-wasf fi al-muayyan laghw): The principle that a descriptive attribute in a specified object is inconsequential for the oath unless it was the motivator for the oath.
اليمين على العين (al-yamin 'ala al-'ayn): An oath taken on a specific physical entity (the 'ayn) rather than on its name or description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'By Allah, I will not enter this house,' and then the house's roof collapses but the walls remain standing, does he break his oath by entering the ruins?
A) Yes, because the name 'house' still applies since it remains a dwelling place.
B) No, because the roof is an essential part of a house and its removal changes the entity.
C) Yes, but only if the oath was general (nakira).
D) No, because any destruction invalidates the oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the key is whether the essential identity (name) of the place persists after change; for a specific house, the plot remains the same, but for a house (bayt), the building structure matters.
