Excuse for the Wife in Night Oaths
Ruling on oath made at night when leaving is impossible until morning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a wife's excuse for an oath made at night and distinguish it from the case of a man.

Core Idea
A wife is excused from an oath at night; a man is not, due to fear.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi oath law: when a man conditions divorce on his wife residing in a house and the oath is made at night. The passage clarifies that the wife is excused until morning because she fears going out at night, whereas a man is not excused. Understanding this distinction helps apply the principle of valid excuses (udhr) in oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is a husband's conditional oath of divorce: 'If you reside in this house, you are divorced,' made at night. The ruling is that the wife is excused (ma'dhurah) from leaving until morning.
The excuse prevents immediate divorce.
2
Note the Condition for the Excuse
The excuse applies specifically when the oath is made at night. The wife's fear of going out at night makes her akin to one coerced (mukrah).
Fear is a valid reason.
3
Understand the Reason
The reason for the excuse is that the wife fears harm or danger at night. This fear makes her stay permissible; she is not sinning by delaying fulfilling the oath.
Contrast with inability (lack of means) in al-Tabyin.
4
Compare with the Case of a Man
If the same statement is made to a man, he is not excused because he does not generally fear going out at night. The excuse is gender-specific based on custom.
This is the chosen view (al-mukhtar).
5
Reconcile with Al-Tabyin
There is no contradiction: al-Tabyin ruled on a person who cannot leave due to inability (e.g., locked door), while this case from al-Tajnis concerns fear. Both are valid excuses but distinct.
Each scenario has its own basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التجنيس : رجل قال لامرأته إن سكنت هذه الدار فأنت طالق وكانت اليمين بالليل فإنها معذورة حتى تصبح لأنها في معنى المكره في هذه السكنى لأنها تخاف الخروج ليلاً، ولو قال ذلك لرجل لم يكن معذوراً لأنه لا يخاف هذا هو المختار ا هـ ولا منافاة بينهما لأن ما في التبيين مفروض بأنه لا يمكنه الخروج وما في التجنيس فيما إذا كان لا يخاف.
And in al-Tajnis: A man said to his wife, 'If you reside in this house, you are divorced,' and the oath was at night, then she is excused until morning because she is like one coerced in this residence because she fears going out at night. But if he said that to a man, he is not excused because he does not fear. This is the chosen view. There is no contradiction between them because what is in al-Tabyin is assumed to be that he cannot go out, and what is in al-Tajnis is for the case where he does not fear.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a specific scenario within the section on oaths: when an oath is made at night. The source is al-Tajnis by al-Sarakhsi or similar. A man tells his wife, 'If you reside in this house, you are divorced,' and the oath is pronounced at night. The wife is excused from leaving immediately because she fears going out at night, and she is considered coerced. She is given time until morning. However, if the same statement was made to a man, he is not excused because he does not have the same fear. The author notes that there is no contradiction between this ruling and the earlier one from al-Tabyin (where a person who cannot leave until morning due to genuine inability is excused), because the earlier case dealt with inability, while this case deals with fear. So the excuse for a woman at night is based on a valid fear, not just inability.

Key Terms
معذورة (ma'dhurah): Excused; having a valid reason for not fulfilling the oath immediately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) She is excused until morning because she fears going out at night.
C) She is excused only if she cannot leave due to a locked door.
D) She is not excused because the oath was conditional.

Key Takeaway
A wife is excused from an oath made at night until morning due to fear of going out, but a man is not excused.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Transfer of Belongings in Oaths
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the extent of belongings transfer in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on what constitutes sufficient transfer of belongings to fulfill an oath to vacate a residence, and evaluate the tarjih among them.

Core Idea
The amount of belongings that must be moved to avoid breaking an oath divides the Hanafi school.

When a person swears to leave a residence, the oath is broken (hanth) if either family or belongings remain. While there is consensus on the definition of family, the scholars differ sharply on what suffices for belongings. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents three distinct views within the Hanafi school, along with their proofs and subsequent tarjih preferences. Understanding this dispute is essential for muftis who must balance legal precaution with practical convenience.

Guided Path
1
State the baseline consensus
Identify the agreed-upon rule: breaking the oath requires the transfer of both family and all belongings. Recognize that 'family' is defined as wife, children, and household staff.
This consensus is the starting point for the dispute.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement
List the three positions: Abu Hanifa (any remaining item, even a peg, causes hanth), Abu Yusuf (majority suffices), Muhammad (only items necessary for dwelling matter).
The dispute centers on the definition of 'residence' (sukna).
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's proof
Analyze his qiyas: residence is established by the whole, so it remains with any part. He supports this with analogies about grape juice (quality of stillness) and the apostate house (one Muslim prevents it from becoming Dar al-Harb).
He distinguishes parts (e.g., a peg) from individuals (people).
4
Analyze objections of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
State their practical concerns: full transfer is sometimes impossible (Abu Yusuf) and not all items are essential for dwelling (Muhammad). Note that they reject Abu Hanifa's strictness.
Their views are based on istihsan (juristic preference) and ease.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Identify the practical difference: liability for a deposit left behind (Abu Hanifa says no liability, the two say yes). Then examine the tarjih preferences among later Hanafi authorities (Abu al-Layth preferred Abu Hanifa, al-Hidayah preferred Muhammad, al-Muhit preferred Abu Yusuf).
The author suggests that fatwa according to Abu Hanifa is more precautionary (ahwat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحنث يحصل ببقاء أحدهما من غير توقف عليهما، فلو قال نويت التحول ببدني خاصة لم يصدق في القضاء ويدين كما في البدائع. وأفاد أنه لا بد من نقل جميع الأهل والمتاع وهو في الأصل بالإجماع. والمراد بالأهل زوجته وأولاده الذين معه وكل من كان يأويه لخدمته والقيام بأمره كما في البدائع. وأما في الأمتعة ففيه اختلاف فقال الإمام : المتاع كالأهل حتى لو بقي وتد حنث لأن السكنى تثبت بالكل فتبقى ببقاء شيء منه وقد صار هذا أصلاً للإمام حتى لو بقي صفة السكون في العصير يمنع من صيرورته خمراً وبقاء مسلم واحد في دار ارتد أهلها يمنع من صيرورتها دار حرب ولا يرد عليه أن الشيء ينتفي بانتفاء جزئه كالعشرة تنتفي بانتفاء الواحد لأن ذلك في الأجزاء، أما في الأفراد فلا كالرجال لا ينتفي بانتفاء واحد. والفرق بين الفرد والجزء أنه إن صدق اسم الكل على كل واحد فالآحاد أفراد وإلا فأجزاء كما عرف من بحث العام في الأصول. وقال أبو يوسف : يعتبر نقل الأكثر لتعذر نقل الكل في بعض الأوقات. وقال محمد : يعتبر نقل ما تقوم به السكنى لأن ما وراءه ليس من السكنى. وقد اختلف الترجيح فالفقيه أبو الليث في شرح الجامع الصغير رجح قول الإمام وأخذ به كما في غاية البيان، والمشايخ استثنوا منه ما لا تتأتى به السكني كقطعة ذكره في التبيين وغيره. ورجح في الهداية قول محمد بأنه أحسن وأرفق بالناس، ومنهم من صرح بأن الفتوى عليه كما في فتح القدير، وصرح كثير كصاحب المحيط والفوائد الظهيرية والكافي بأن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف فقد اختلف الترجيح كما ترى، والإفتاء بمذهب الإمام أولى لأنه أحوط وإن كان غيره أرفق. ويتفرق على كون السكني تبقى ببقاء اليسير من المتاع عنده أنه لو انتقل المودع وترك الوديعة لا غير في المنزل المنتقل عنه لا يضمن. وعندهما يضمن بكل حال؛ ذكره البزازي في فتاواه من كتاب الإجارة من فصل الخياط والنساج.
The breaking of the oath occurs by the remaining of one of them (family or belongings) without dependence on both. If he said, 'I intended to transfer only my body,' he is not believed in court but he is believed inwardly, as in al-Bada'i'. And he clarified that it is necessary to transfer all family and belongings, and that is in principle by consensus. And the meaning of 'family' is his wife and his children who are with him, and everyone whom he houses for his service and to manage his affairs, as in al-Bada'i'. As for belongings, there is disagreement. Imam Abu Hanifa said: Belongings are like family; if even a peg remains, he breaks the oath, because residence is established by the whole, so it remains by the remaining of some part of it. And this has become a principle for Imam: even if the quality of stillness remains in grape juice, it prevents it from becoming wine; and the remaining of one Muslim in a house whose inhabitants have apostatized prevents it from becoming a war territory. It is not objected that a thing is negated by the negation of its part, like ten is negated by the negation of one, because that is regarding parts; as for individuals, no, like men are not negated by the negation of one. The difference between an individual and a part is that if the name of the whole applies to each one, then the ones are individuals; otherwise, they are parts, as known from the discussion of the general in usul. Abu Yusuf said: Transfer of the majority is considered, because it is impossible to transfer everything at times. Muhammad said: Transfer of what is necessary for residence is considered, because what is beyond that is not part of residence. And there is disagreement on tarjih: al-Faqih Abu al-Layth in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir preferred the saying of Imam and acted upon it, as in Ghayat al-Bayan; and the mashayikh excluded from it what does not constitute residence, like a piece, as mentioned in al-Tabyin and others. In al-Hidayah, the saying of Muhammad was preferred as it is better and more convenient for people. Some declared that the fatwa is upon it, as in Fath al-Qadir. Many, like the author of al-Muhit, al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah, and al-Kafi, declared that the fatwa is upon the saying of Abu Yusuf. So the tarjih differs as you see. Issuing fatwa according to the madhhab of Imam is better because it is more precautionary, even if others are more convenient. There branches from the view that residence remains by the remaining of a little belonging: according to him (Abu Hanifa), if the depositor moves and leaves only the deposit in the house he moved from, he is not liable. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is liable in any case; mentioned by al-Bazzazi in his Fatawa from the book of Ijarah, chapter of the tailor and weaver.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of transferring belongings to fulfill an oath to leave a residence. The author first states that breaking the oath occurs by the remaining of either family or belongings—both must be moved entirely. However, there is a consensus on the definition of family (wife, children, servants), but a significant difference regarding belongings. Abu Hanifa held that even a small item like a peg remaining constitutes a breach, because residence is established by the totality. He supports this with analogies: the quality of stillness in grape juice prevents it from becoming wine (though not directly relevant), and the presence of a single Muslim in a house prevents it from being considered Dar al-Harb. He distinguishes between individuals and parts: a part (like a peg) is considered a part of the residence, so its presence sustains the whole. Abu Yusuf considered the majority of belongings to be sufficient due to practical difficulty in moving everything. Muhammad considered only what is necessary for dwelling (e.g., furniture for basic living) to be relevant; superfluous items are not part of residence. The tarjih (preference) among these views is debated. Abu al-Layth preferred Abu Hanifa's view, while al-Hidayah favored Muhammad's view as more practical. Many later authorities, such as the author of al-Muhit, adopted Abu Yusuf's view. The author finally suggests that fatwa according to Abu Hanifa is more precautionary (ahwat). A practical branch: according to Abu Hanifa, if a person moves and leaves a deposited item behind, he is not liable for it because the residence is considered to have been maintained; according to the two, he is liable.

Key Terms
المتاع (al-mata'): Belongings, household goods; includes furniture, utensils, etc.
الوتد (al-watad): A peg, tent peg, or small fixture; used as example of a minimal remaining item.
السكنى (al-sukna): Residence, dwelling; the legal concept of inhabiting a place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person swears to leave a house and moves all belongings except a single peg, does he break his oath?
A) No, because a peg is negligible.
B) Yes, because any remaining part of residence sustains it.
C) No, because only the majority needs to be moved.
D) Yes, but only if the peg is essential for dwelling.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers three distinct positions on what constitutes sufficient transfer of belongings in oaths, reflecting a balance between strictness and practicality.

Ruling on Oath Not to Reside in a Specific House
Ruling on oath regarding a specific house: occupation of any part breaks oath; moving out requires new residence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath not to reside in a house is broken and distinguish between an oath on 'this house' and an oath on 'the entire house'.

Core Idea
An oath on 'this house' breaks by any part; on 'the entire house' requires full occupation.

Oaths concerning residence can be tricky because the wording determines the precise legal obligation. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: if you swear not to live in a particular house, moving into a single room breaks the oath. But if you swear not to occupy 'the entire house', you must actually occupy every part. Moreover, simply moving your belongings out may not suffice; you must establish residence elsewhere. The following passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out these rules and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath's wording
Check whether the oath says 'this house' (which is understood as the whole dwelling) or 'the entire house' (which emphasises totality). The ruling differs accordingly.
If the wording is ambiguous, custom (urf) may clarify the intention.
2
Determine the action that constitutes residence
For 'this house', residing in any room or part breaks the oath. For 'the entire house', one must occupy all parts.
The literal meaning (haqiqah) is applied unless a specific intention is proven.
3
Assess the effect of moving out
If the oath is ongoing (e.g., not to reside ever), moving family and belongings away may not suffice unless the person takes up residence in a different house. Moving to the alley or mosque does not count.
Al-Hidayah requires moving to another house without delay; some later authorities rely on custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو حلف لا يسكن دار فلان هذه فسكن منزلاً منها حنث لأن الدار هكذا تسكن أن لا يسكنها كلها لا يحنث حتى يسكنها كلها لأن الدار حقيقة اسم للجميع فقد نوى الحقيقة، وظاهر كلام المصنف أنه لو نقل أهله ومتاعه منها فإنه يبرّ سواء سكن في منزل آخر أو لا، وفيه اختلاف ففي الهداية : وينبغي أن ينتقل إلى منزل آخر بلا تأخير حتى يبر فإن انتقل إلى السكة أو إلى المسجد قالوا لا يبر دليله في الزيادات أن من خرج بعياله من مصره فلم يتخذ وطناً آخر يبقى وطنه الأول في حق الصلاة كذا هذا ا هـ . وفي فتح القدير : قوله : (والمشايخ لستثنوا منه الخ) أقول : على هذا الاستثناء يتوافق قول الإمام مع قول محمد، وأما ما في النهر من أن هذا ليس قول واحد منهم فغير ظاهر تأمل قوله : ( والافتاء بقول الإمام أولى) قال في النهر : أنت خبير بأنه ليس المدار إلا على العرف في أنه ساكن أولاً ولا شك أن من خرج عادة، فإن عنى
And in al-Muhit: If he swore not to reside in this house of so-and-so, and he resided in one of its rooms, he breaks the oath because the house is thus resided in. But if he swore not to reside in its entirety, he does not break the oath until he resides in its entirety, because 'house' is literally a name for the whole; he intended the literal meaning. The apparent wording of the author (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) is that if he moved his family and belongings from it, he is freed from the oath, whether he resides in another house or not. There is disagreement on this. In al-Hidayah: He should move to another house without delay to be freed; if he moves to the alley or the mosque, they said he is not freed. Its proof is in al-Ziyadat: One who leaves his city with his family and does not take another abode, his first abode remains for the purpose of prayer. Similarly here. In Fath al-Qadir: His statement 'The mashayikh excluded from it...' I say: On this exclusion, the saying of Imam (Abu Hanifa) and that of Muhammad are compatible. As for what is in al-Nahr, that this is not the saying of any of them, it is not apparent. Reflect on his statement: 'Issuing fatwa according to the saying of Imam is better.' He said in al-Nahr: You know that the criterion is only custom in whether he is a resident or not, and there is no doubt that one who leaves customarily...
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a separate issue: an oath regarding a specific house. If someone swears not to reside in 'this house' and then lives in one room of that house, he breaks the oath because the house is known to be resided in by occupying parts. However, if the oath is 'not to reside in the entire house,' he must reside in all parts to break it, because the literal meaning of 'house' includes the whole. The author then notes that the apparent wording of Kanz al-Daqa'iq suggests that moving family and belongings away from the house suffices to free the swearer, regardless of where he later lives. But al-Hidayah adds a condition: he must move to another house without delay; moving to the alley or mosque does not suffice. This is supported by a ruling from al-Ziyadat: a person who leaves his city with his family but does not adopt another abode remains considered a resident of his first abode for certain rulings (like shortening prayer). Finally, the text touches on the earlier dispute, noting that the exclusion of non-essential items by the mashayikh brings Abu Hanifa and Muhammad into agreement. The author quotes from al-Nahr that the real criterion is custom (urf).

Key Terms
الدار (al-dar): A house; literally the entire dwelling, including all rooms and structures.
الوتن (al-watan): Abode, place of settlement; used in the context of determining residence for prayer and other rulings.
حنث (hanth): Break of an oath; being considered a violator of the swear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears not to reside in 'this house' and then moves his family and belongings to the same alley but does not move to another house, is he freed from his oath?
A) Yes, because he moved family and belongings.
B) No, because he did not move to a different house.
C) Yes, because the oath was on the house, not the alley.
D) No, because he must also change his name.

Key Takeaway
The wording of an oath determines the required action; moving out requires customarily establishing residence elsewhere.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Moving Belongings in Oaths of Dwelling
Intra-Hanafi dispute on moving belonging in dwelling oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions on what constitutes moving belongings in a dwelling oath and identify the mufta bihi in different contexts.

Core Idea
When swearing to leave a dwelling, the amount of belongings that must be moved is disputed among the Hanafi imams — a single peg may suffice to break the oath.

In oaths concerning dwelling, one must move both family and goods to avoid breaking the oath. While moving family is agreed upon, the required extent for goods is debated. This lesson unpacks the three positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with the differing tarjih among later Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue and Consensus
Determine the core scenario: a person swears to vacate a dwelling. The act of breaking the oath (hanth) occurs by remaining in the dwelling. By consensus, moving the entire family (wife, children, dependents) is required.
The definition of 'ahl' is broad, including servants.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's Position and Its Basis
For Abu Hanifa, goods are analogous to family: any remaining item, even a peg, causes hanth because dwelling is established by the whole and persists with any part. This is based on the principle that the whole is negated by negation of a part unless the part is an individual (as opposed to a true part).
He analogizes to grape juice stillness and a single Muslim preventing dar harb status.
3
Contrast Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's Positions
Abu Yusuf requires moving the majority of goods due to practical impossibility of moving everything. Muhammad requires only what is essential for dwelling; excess items are irrelevant because they are not integral to dwelling.
These positions relax the strictness of Abu Hanifa's view.
4
Analyze the Tarjih and Determine Mufta Bihi
Later scholars differed: Abu al-Layth preferred Abu Hanifa; al-Hidayah preferred Muhammad; many others (al-Muhit, al-Kafi) preferred Abu Yusuf. The author leans toward Abu Hanifa as more cautious (ahwat), but notes that fatwa is given on all three views.
Thus no single mufta bihi; context and ease may influence choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحنث يحصل ببقاء أحدهما من غير توقف عليهما، فلو قال نويت التحول ببدني خاصة لم يصدق في القضاء ويدين كما في البدائع. وأفاد أنه لا بد من نقل جميع الأهل والمتاع وهو في الأصل بالإجماع. والمراد بالأهل زوجته وأولاده الذين معه وكل من كان يأويه لخدمته والقيام بأمره كما في البدائع. وأما في الأمتعة ففيه اختلاف فقال الإمام: المتاع كالأهل حتى لو بقي وتد حنث لأن السكنى تثبت بالكل فتبقى ببقاء شيء منه وقد صار هذا أصلاً للإمام حتى لو بقي صفة السكون في العصير يمنع من صيرورته خمراً وبقاء مسلم واحد في دار ارتد أهلها يمنع من صيرورتها دار حرب. ولا يرد عليه أن الشيء ينتفي بانتفاء جزئه كالعشرة تنتفي بانتفاء الواحد لأن ذلك في الأجزاء، أما في الأفراد فلا كالرجال لا ينتفي بانتفاء واحد. والفرق بين الفرد والجزء أنه إن صدق اسم الكل على كل واحد فالآحاد أفراد وإلا فأجزاء كما عرف من بحث العام في الأصول. وقال أبو يوسف: يعتبر نقل الأكثر لتعذر نقل الكل في بعض الأوقات. وقال محمد: يعتبر نقل ما تقوم به السكنى لأن ما وراءه ليس من السكنى. وقد اختلف الترجيح فالفقيه أبو الليث في شرح الجامع الصغير رجح قول الإمام وأخذ به كما في غاية البيان، والمشايخ استثنوا منه ما لا تتأتى به السكنى كقطعة [؟] ذكره في التبيين وغيره. ورجح في الهداية قول محمد بأنه أحسن وأرفق بالناس، ومنهم من صرح بأن الفتوى عليه كما في فتح القدير، وصرح كثير كصاحب المحيط والفوائد الظهيرية والكافي بأن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف، فقد اختلف الترجيح كما ترى، والإفتاء بمذهب الإمام أولى لأنه أحوط وإن كان غيره أرفق.
Hanth occurs by the remaining of one of them without being dependent on both. If he said 'I intended to move with my body only', he is not believed in the judiciary but is believed between himself and Allah, as in al-Bada'i. It is indicated that it is necessary to move all the family and goods, and this is originally by consensus. The intended meaning of family are his wife and children who are with him, and everyone whom he houses for his service and management, as in al-Bada'i. As for goods, there is disagreement. Imam [Abu Hanifa] said: Goods are like family, so much so that if a peg remains, he is guilty of perjury because dwelling is established by the whole, so it remains by the remaining of any part of it. This has become a principle for the Imam, such that if a state of stillness remains in grape juice, it prevents it from becoming wine, and if one Muslim remains in a house whose inhabitants have apostatized, it prevents it from becoming a war territory. It is not objected to him that something is negated by the negation of its part, like the number ten is negated by the negation of one, because that is about parts, not individuals. An individual like men is not negated by the negation of one. The difference between an individual and a part is that if the name of the whole is true for each one, then the ones are individuals; otherwise they are parts, as known from the discussion of generality in usul. Abu Yusuf said: Moving the majority is considered, because moving the whole is sometimes impossible. Muhammad said: Moving what establishes dwelling is considered, because what is beyond that is not part of dwelling. The tarjih has differed: Faqih Abu al-Layth in Sharh al-Jami al-Saghir preferred the Imam's view and adopted it, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. The mashayikh exempted from it what does not enable dwelling, like a piece [؟], as mentioned in al-Tabyin and others. Al-Hidayah preferred Muhammad's view, saying it is better and more convenient for people. Some explicitly stated that the fatwa is upon it, as in Fath al-Qadir. Many, such as the author of al-Muhit, al-Fawaid al-Zahirah, and al-Kafi, stated that the fatwa is upon Abu Yusuf's view. So the tarjih has differed as you see. Giving fatwa according to the Imam's madhhab is better because it is more cautious, even if others are more convenient.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the intra-Hanafi dispute concerning oaths to leave a dwelling. The author first states a ruling: breaking the oath occurs by remaining of either the family or the goods, without waiting for both. If the swearer claims he intended only bodily movement, he is not believed in court but his claim is accepted between him and Allah. The core issue is: what constitutes sufficient removal? By consensus, all family must be moved, defined as wife, children, and dependents. For goods, there is a three-way ikhtilaf. Imam Abu Hanifa treats goods like family: even a single peg remaining would cause perjury, because dwelling is established by the whole and persists with any part. This is supported by analogies: residual stillness in juice prevents its becoming wine; one Muslim in a house of apostates prevents it from becoming dar harb. Abu Yusuf considers moving the majority sufficient, due to practical difficulty. Muhammad requires moving what is necessary for dwelling; surplus items are irrelevant. The commentators then present tarjih: Abu al-Layth prefers Abu Hanifa; al-Hidayah prefers Muhammad as more convenient; many later works prefer Abu Yusuf for fatwa. The author notes the difference of tarjih but opines that following Abu Hanifa is more cautious (ahwat), though the others are more convenient for people.

Key Terms
الأهل (al-ahl): Family including wife, children, and dependents living with him for service and care.
المتاع (al-mataa): Household goods and furniture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Hanafi position requires moving only what is essential for dwelling?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) All three agree

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers three valid views on moving goods in dwelling oaths, with tarjih varying by authority, and the author recommends the cautious view of Abu Hanifa but notes others are more convenient.

Depositor Liability and Moving in Dwelling Oaths
Derived ruling on depositor liability and moving family/goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on depositor liability in dwelling oaths and apply the distinction between moving family/goods versus leaving a deposit.

Core Idea
Dwelling ends when family and goods are moved; a deposit alone does not maintain it.

In the previous lesson, we learned that dwelling remains by the persistence of even a little of one's goods. Now we explore a corollary: if the depositor moves out but leaves only a deposit, is he still considered a resident? The Hanafi imams differ, and the answer affects whether he breaks his oath not to dwell. This lesson focuses on that ikhtilaf and the broader rule about moving family and goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ikhtilaf on liability
The text states that according to Abu Hanifa, if the depositor moves and leaves only the deposit, he is not liable. According to the Sahibayn, he is liable in all cases. Understand the practical consequence: liability here refers to breaking the oath (hanth).
This builds on the earlier rule that dwelling remains by a little of one's goods.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa's view is that dwelling is established by the entirety of one's family and goods. A deposit (wadi'a) is not considered part of one's dwelling goods because it is entrusted property. Therefore, its presence alone does not maintain the status of dwelling.
The Sahibayn consider any remaining item, including a deposit, as sufficient to maintain dwelling.
3
Apply to the case of moving family and goods
If the oath is not to reside in a specific house, and the person moves his family and goods entirely, he is free from the oath. However, al-Hidayah requires moving to another place without delay. Moving to the street or mosque is insufficient because the original home remains his 'watan' in terms of prayer.
Faqih Abu al-Layth distinguishes: if he does not hand over a rented house, he breaks the oath; if he does, he does not.
4
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The text mentions that giving fatwa according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) is better (ahwat). However, later authorities like al-Burhan prefer Muhammad's view. The practical tarjih depends on custom and intention.
This shows the dynamic nature of tarjih within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والنساج. وفي المحيط: لو حلف لا يسكن دار فلان هذه فسكن منزلاً منها حنث لأن الدار هكذا تسكن أن لا يسكنها كلها لا يحنث حتى يسكنها كلها لأن الدار حقيقة اسم للجميع فقد نوى الحقيقة، وظاهر كلام المصنف أنه لو نقل أهله ومتاعه منها فإنه يبر سواء سكن في منزل آخر أو لا، وفيه اختلاف ففي الهداية: وينبغي أن ينتقل إلى منزل آخر بلا تأخير حتى يبر فإن انتقل إلى السكة أو إلى المسجد قالوا لا يبر دليله في الزيادات أن من النهر من أن هذا ليس قول واحد منهم فغير ظاهر تأمل قوله: ( والافتاء بقول الإمام أولى) قال في النهر: أنت خبير بأنه ليس المدار إلا على العرف في أنه ساكن أولاً ولا شك أن من خرج عادة، فإن عنى وإطلاق عدم الحنث أوجه وكون وطنه باقياً في حق إتمام الصلاة ما لم يستوطن غيره لا يستلزم تسميته ساكناً عرفاً بذلك المكان بل يقطع من العرف فيمن نقل أهله وأمتعته وخرج مسافراً أنه لا يقال فيه إنه ساكن ا.هـ. وفصل نذكرها تتميماً للفائدة. ففي البدائع: لو حلف لا يسكن هذه الدار ولم يكن ساكناً فيها فالسكنى فيها أن يسكنها بنفسه وينقل إليها من متاعه ما يبات فيه ويستعمله في منزله، فإذا فعل ذلك فهو حانث، وأما المساكنة فإذا كان رجل ساكناً مع رجل في دار فحلف أحدهما أن لا يساكن صاحبه فإن أخذ في النقلة وهي ممكنة برّ وإلا حنث والنقلة على الخلاف المتقدم فإن لم ينتقل للحال حنث لأن البقاء على المساكنة مساكنة وهو أن يجمعهما منزل واحد، فإن وهب متاعه للمحلوف عليه أو أودعه أو أعاره ثم خرج في طلب منزل فلم يجد منزلاً أياماً ولم يأت الدار التي فيها صاحبه قال محمد: إن كان وهب له المتاع وقبضه منه وخرج منه ساعته وليس من رأيه العود فليس بمساكن، وكذلك إن أودعه المتاع ثم خرج لا يريد العود إلى ذلك المنزل، وكذا العارية، ولو كان له في الدار زوجة فراودها الخروج فأبت ولم يقدر على إخراجها فإنه لا يحنث ببقائها، وإذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً فساكنه في عرصة دار أو بيت أو غرفة حنث، فإن ساكنه دار هذا في ا في حجرة وهذا في حجرة أو هذا في منزل وهذا في منزل حنث إلا أن تكون داراً كبيرة. قال أبو يوسف: مثل دار الرقيق ودار الوليد بالكوفة، وكذا كل دار عظيمة فيها مقاصير ومنازل، وعن محمد: إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً ولم يسم داراً فسكن هذا في حجرة وهذا في حجرة لم يحنث إلا أن يساكنه في حجرة واحدة فإن سكن هذا في بيت من دار وهذا في بيت آخر وقد حلف لا يساكنه ولم يسم داراً حنث في قولهم لأن بيوت الدار الواحدة كالبيت الواحد وقال أبو يوسف: فإن ساكنه في حانوت في سوق يعملان فيه عملاً أو يبيعان تجارة فإنه لا يحنث إلا بالنية أو يكون بينهما كلام يدل عليها. قالوا: إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً بالكوفة ولا نية له فسكن أحدهما في دار والآخر في دار أخرى في على نية ترك المكان وعدم العودة إليه ونقل من أمتعته فيه ما يقوم به أمر سكناه وهو على نية نقل الباقي يقال ليس ساكناً في هذا المكان بل انتقل منه وسكن في المكان الفلاني وبهذا يترجح قول محمد اهـ. وهذا الترجيح بالوجه المذكور مأخوذ من الفتح. وفي الشرنبلالية عن البرهان أن قول محمد أصح ما يفتى به من التصحيحين . قوله: (وعن محمد إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً الخ قال الرملي: إذا حلف لا يساكنه فساكنه في بيت واحد أو مقصورة من غير أهل ومتاع لا يحنث كما في التتارخانية نقلاً عن الظهيرية، وقد قدم
It branches from the fact that dwelling remains by the persistence of a little of the goods, according to him [Abu Hanifa], that if the depositor moves and leaves only the deposit in the former home, he is not liable. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], he is liable in all cases; al-Bazzazi mentioned this in his Fatawa from the book of Ijarah. In al-Muhit: If one swears not to reside in this house of so-and-so, then resides in one of its rooms, he breaks his oath. Because a house is resided in that way. If one swears not to reside in it entirely, he does not break the oath until he resides in it entirely, because 'house' is a name for the whole, so he intended the literal meaning. The apparent meaning of the author's words is that if he moves his family and goods from it, he is free from the oath, whether he resides in another place or not. There is disagreement. In al-Hidayah: He should move to another place without delay to be free. If he moves to the street or mosque, they said he is not free. The evidence is in al-Ziyadat that one who leaves his town with his family without adopting another home, his first home remains his home in terms of prayer, and likewise here. In Fath al-Qadir: His statement: 'the mashayikh exempted from it etc.' I say: With this exception, the Imam's statement aligns with Muhammad's. What is in al-Nahr that this is not the view of any of them is not apparent. Consider his statement: 'Giving fatwa according to the Imam's view is better.' Al-Nahr says: You are aware that it depends solely on custom as to whether he is a resident or not. Undoubtedly, one who leaves customarily... and the absolute absence of guilt is more appropriate. The remaining of his home for the purpose of completing prayer as long as he does not adopt another does not entail calling him a resident customarily. Rather, it is cut off from custom that someone who moves his family and goods and travels is not said to be a resident. End. Faqih Abu al-Layth gave a good distinction: If he does not hand over his rented house to its owners, he breaks his oath; if he does, he does not. In al-Zahirah: The correct view is that he breaks his oath unless he takes another dwelling. The author may complete the issues of oaths on dwelling, so we mention them for completeness. In al-Bada'i: If one swears not to reside in this house and is not a resident in it, then 'residing' in it means he resides himself and moves into it his goods for sleeping and use. If he does that, he is guilty. As for cohabitation (musakana): If a man is living with another in a house, and one swears not to cohabit with his companion, then if he begins the move and it is possible, he is free; otherwise he breaks his oath. The move is according to the earlier disagreement. If he does not move immediately, he breaks his oath because remaining in cohabitation is cohabitation, meaning they share one home. If he gives his goods as a gift to the one he swore about, or deposits or lends them, then goes out seeking a home and does not find one for days and does not come to the house of his companion, Muhammad said: If he gave the goods as a gift and the other took possession, and he left immediately and does not intend to return, then he is not a cohabitant. Similarly if he deposited the goods and left not intending to return, and likewise a loan. If he has a wife in the house and asks her to leave but she refuses and he cannot force her out, then he does not break his oath by her remaining. If one swears not to cohabit with so-and-so and then cohabits with him in a courtyard, room, or chamber, he breaks his oath. If they cohabit in a house, one in a room, the other in a room; or one in an apartment, the other in an apartment, he breaks his oath, unless it is a large house. Abu Yusuf said: Like Dar al-Raqiq and Dar al-Walid in Kufa, and likewise any large house with separate quarters and apartments. From Muhammad: If one swears not to cohabit with so-and-so and does not specify a house, and each lives in a separate chamber, he does not break the oath unless they live in a single chamber. If one lives in a room of a house and the other in another room, and he swore not to cohabit without specifying a house, he breaks his oath according to their view because the rooms of one house are like one room. Abu Yusuf said: If he cohabits with him in a shop in a market where they work or sell goods, he does not break his oath unless he intended or there is speech indicating it. They said: If one swears not to cohabit with so-and-so in Kufa without intention, and one lives in one house and the other in another house... on the intention of leaving the place and not returning, and moves his goods that establish his dwelling, intending to move the rest, he is said not to be a resident of this place but has moved from it and resides in such-and-such place. Thus Muhammad's view is preferred. This tarjih by this method is taken from al-Fath. In al-Sharnablaliyyah from al-Burhan that Muhammad's view is the most correct for giving fatwa among the two corrections. His statement: 'And from Muhammad: If one swears not to cohabit...' al-Ramli said: If he swears not to cohabit with him, then cohabits with him in one room or a compartment without family and goods, he does not break his oath, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah quoting al-Zahirah, and it has been presented.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion from the previous one, deriving practical consequences (furu') from the ikhtilaf among the Hanafi imams. First, it addresses the liability of a depositor: according to Abu Hanifa, if the depositor moves out leaving only the deposit, he is not liable because the deposit's presence does not constitute his dwelling; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he is liable in all cases. Next, it discusses an oath not to reside in a specific house: if he resides in one room, he breaks the oath; but if he swears not to reside in it entirely (i.e., the whole house), he does not break the oath until he resides in the entire house, because 'house' is a name for the whole. The author of al-Hidayah requires moving to another place to be free of the oath; moving to the street or mosque is insufficient. Fath al-Qadir notes that with the mashayikh's exception, Abu Hanifa's view aligns with Muhammad's. Faqih Abu al-Layth distinguishes between handing over a rented house (if not handed over, hanth; if handed over, no hanth). The passage then presents supplementary issues from al-Bada'i', covering oaths not to reside (where residing means entering and using goods for sleep) and oaths not to cohabit (musakana). For cohabitation, the ruling depends on whether they share a dwelling. Details include scenarios of giving away goods, depositing, lending, wife's refusal to leave, and large houses. Abu Yusuf gives examples of large houses in Kufa. Muhammad's view is preferred in various sources, especially in matters of intention to leave permanently. The passage concludes with al-Ramli's note that cohabiting in a single room or compartment without family and goods does not break the oath.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'a): Deposit: an item left for safekeeping by one party with another.
السكنى (al-sukna): Dwelling: the act of residing in a place with one's family and goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when does a depositor who moves out become free from an oath not to reside?
A) Only when he takes all his possessions, including deposits.
B) When he moves his family and his own goods, even if a deposit remains.
C) When he hands over the house to someone else.
D) When he moves to another city.

Key Takeaway
In dwelling oaths, the presence of a deposit does not maintain dwelling according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn disagree; the ruling hinges on whether any item remains.

Supplementary Rulings on Cohabitation Oaths (Musakana)
Supplementary rulings on cohabitation oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that constitute cohabitation (musakana) in Hanafi fiqh and apply the rulings to scenarios such as gift, deposit, loan, and wife's refusal.

Core Idea
Cohabitation requires shared living space; the oath breaks when one fully departs with intention not to return.

Having covered oaths on dwelling (sukna), we now turn to oaths on cohabitation (musakana). The Hanafi imams detail what it means to 'cohabit' with someone and how one can break the oath. The key is whether the parties share a single dwelling unit and whether the person making the oath has genuinely moved out. This lesson explores these nuances from the text of al-Bada'i' and other sources.

Guided Path
1
Define musakana
Musakana means two or more people living in the same home, sharing living space. The text states that if one swears not to cohabit with someone, remaining in cohabitation (i.e., sharing a home) is itself a violation. The move must be complete to break the oath.
Contrast with sukna (residing alone or with family).
2
Identify acts that terminate musakana
Muhammad states that giving goods as a gift, depositing them, or lending them to the cohabitant, followed by immediate departure without intention to return, terminates musakana. The key is the intention not to return. If the person leaves but intends to come back, he remains a cohabitant.
The goods transfer analogy shows that removal of one's possessions ends the shared dwelling.
3
Exception: wife's refusal to leave
If a man swears not to cohabit with his wife, but she refuses to leave and he cannot force her, he does not break his oath by her remaining. This is because he is not voluntarily sharing the home.
This shows that cohabitation requires volitional sharing.
4
Impact of large houses and shops
Abu Yusuf says that in a large house (like Dar al-Raqiq in Kufa), separate apartments do not constitute cohabitation. Muhammad holds that any room in the same house counts as cohabitation unless the house is very large and has separate quarters. For shops, no hanth unless intention or verbal indication.
Custom and house size are factors.
5
Intention and custom as determiners
The text concludes that if the person leaves with intention not to return and moves the goods that establish his dwelling, he is no longer a cohabitant. Muhammad's view is preferred because it aligns with custom.
This explains the tarjih in later sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتفرع على كون السكنى تبقى ببقاء اليسير من المتاع عنده أنه لو انتقل المودع وترك الوديعة لا غير في المنزل المنتقل عنه لا يضمن. وعندهما يضمن بكل حال؛ ذكره البزازي في فتاواه من كتاب الإجارة من فصل الخياط والنساج. وفي المحيط: لو حلف لا يسكن دار فلان هذه فسكن منزلاً منها حنث لأن الدار هكذا تسكن أن لا يسكنها كلها لا يحنث حتى يسكنها كلها لأن الدار حقيقة اسم للجميع فقد نوى الحقيقة، وظاهر كلام المصنف أنه لو نقل أهله ومتاعه منها فإنه يبر سواء سكن في منزل آخر أو لا، وفيه اختلاف ففي الهداية: وينبغي أن ينتقل إلى منزل آخر بلا تأخير حتى يبر فإن انتقل إلى السكة أو إلى المسجد قالوا لا يبر دليله في الزيادات أن من خرج بعياله من مصره فلم يتخذ وطناً آخر يبقى وطنه الأول في حق الصلاة كذا هذا ا هـ. وفي فتح القدير: قوله: (والمشايخ لستثنوا منه الخ) أقول: على هذا الاستثناء يتوافق قول الإمام مع قول محمد، وأما ما في النهر من أن هذا ليس قول واحد منهم فغير ظاهر تأمل قوله: ( والافتاء بقول الإمام أولى) قال في النهر: أنت خبير بأنه ليس المدار إلا على العرف في أنه ساكن أولاً ولا شك أن من خرج عادة، فإن عنى وإطلاق عدم الحنث أوجه وكون وطنه باقياً في حق إتمام الصلاة ما لم يستوطن غيره لا يستلزم تسميته ساكناً عرفاً بذلك المكان بل يقطع من العرف فيمن نقل أهله وأمتعته وخرج مسافراً أنه لا يقال فيه إنه ساكن ا.هـ. وفصل الفقيه أبو الليث تفصيلاً حسناً فقال: إن لم يسلم داره المستأجرة إلى أهلها حنث وإن سلمها لا. وفي الظهيرية: والصحيح أنه يحنث ما لم يتخذ مسكناً آخر. ولو يستوف المصنف رحمه الله مسائل اليمين على السكني فنحن نذكرها تتميماً للفائدة. ففي البدائع: لو حلف لا يسكن هذه الدار ولم يكن ساكناً فيها فالسكنى فيها أن يسكنها بنفسه وينقل إليها من متاعه ما يبات فيه ويستعمله في منزله، فإذا فعل ذلك فهو حانث، وأما المساكنة فإذا كان رجل ساكناً مع رجل في دار فحلف أحدهما أن لا يساكن صاحبه فإن أخذ في النقلة وهي ممكنة برّ وإلا حنث والنقلة على الخلاف المتقدم فإن لم ينتقل للحال حنث لأن البقاء على المساكنة مساكنة وهو أن يجمعهما منزل واحد، فإن وهب متاعه للمحلوف عليه أو أودعه أو أعاره ثم خرج في طلب منزل فلم يجد منزلاً أياماً ولم يأت الدار التي فيها صاحبه قال محمد: إن كان وهب له المتاع وقبضه منه وخرج منه ساعته وليس من رأيه العود فليس بمساكن، وكذلك إن أودعه المتاع ثم خرج لا يريد العود إلى ذلك المنزل، وكذا العارية، ولو كان له في الدار زوجة فراودها الخروج فأبت ولم يقدر على إخراجها فإنه لا يحنث ببقائها، وإذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً فساكنه في عرصة دار أو بيت أو غرفة حنث، فإن ساكنه دار هذا في ا في حجرة وهذا في حجرة أو هذا في منزل وهذا في منزل حنث إلا أن تكون داراً كبيرة. قال أبو يوسف: مثل دار الرقيق ودار الوليد بالكوفة، وكذا كل دار عظيمة فيها مقاصير ومنازل، وعن محمد: إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً ولم يسم داراً فسكن هذا في حجرة وهذا في حجرة لم يحنث إلا أن يساكنه في حجرة واحدة فإن سكن هذا في بيت من دار وهذا في بيت آخر وقد حلف لا يساكنه ولم يسم داراً حنث في قولهم لأن بيوت الدار الواحدة كالبيت الواحد وقال أبو يوسف: فإن ساكنه في حانوت في سوق يعملان فيه عملاً أو يبيعان تجارة فإنه لا يحنث إلا بالنية أو يكون بينهما كلام يدل عليها. قالوا: إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً بالكوفة ولا نية له فسكن أحدهما في دار والآخر في دار أخرى في على نية ترك المكان وعدم العودة إليه ونقل من أمتعته فيه ما يقوم به أمر سكناه وهو على نية نقل الباقي يقال ليس ساكناً في هذا المكان بل انتقل منه وسكن في المكان الفلاني وبهذا يترجح قول محمد اهـ. وهذا الترجيح بالوجه المذكور مأخوذ من الفتح. وفي الشرنبلالية عن البرهان أن قول محمد أصح ما يفتى به من التصحيحين . قوله: (وعن محمد إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً الخ قال الرملي: إذا حلف لا يساكنه فساكنه في بيت واحد أو مقصورة من غير أهل ومتاع لا يحنث كما في التتارخانية نقلاً عن الظهيرية، وقد قدم
It branches from the fact that dwelling remains by the persistence of a little of the goods, according to him [Abu Hanifa], that if the depositor moves and leaves only the deposit in the former home, he is not liable. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], he is liable in all cases; al-Bazzazi mentioned this in his Fatawa from the book of Ijarah. In al-Muhit: If one swears not to reside in this house of so-and-so, then resides in one of its rooms, he breaks his oath. Because a house is resided in that way. If one swears not to reside in it entirely, he does not break the oath until he resides in it entirely, because 'house' is a name for the whole, so he intended the literal meaning. The apparent meaning of the author's words is that if he moves his family and goods from it, he is free from the oath, whether he resides in another place or not. There is disagreement. In al-Hidayah: He should move to another place without delay to be free. If he moves to the street or mosque, they said he is not free. The evidence is in al-Ziyadat that one who leaves his town with his family without adopting another home, his first home remains his home in terms of prayer, and likewise here. In Fath al-Qadir: His statement: 'the mashayikh exempted from it etc.' I say: With this exception, the Imam's statement aligns with Muhammad's. What is in al-Nahr that this is not the view of any of them is not apparent. Consider his statement: 'Giving fatwa according to the Imam's view is better.' Al-Nahr says: You are aware that it depends solely on custom as to whether he is a resident or not. Undoubtedly, one who leaves customarily... and the absolute absence of guilt is more appropriate. The remaining of his home for the purpose of completing prayer as long as he does not adopt another does not entail calling him a resident customarily. Rather, it is cut off from custom that someone who moves his family and goods and travels is not said to be a resident. End. Faqih Abu al-Layth gave a good distinction: If he does not hand over his rented house to its owners, he breaks his oath; if he does, he does not. In al-Zahirah: The correct view is that he breaks his oath unless he takes another dwelling. The author may complete the issues of oaths on dwelling, so we mention them for completeness. In al-Bada'i: If one swears not to reside in this house and is not a resident in it, then 'residing' in it means he resides himself and moves into it his goods for sleeping and use. If he does that, he is guilty. As for cohabitation (musakana): If a man is living with another in a house, and one swears not to cohabit with his companion, then if he begins the move and it is possible, he is free; otherwise he breaks his oath. The move is according to the earlier disagreement. If he does not move immediately, he breaks his oath because remaining in cohabitation is cohabitation, meaning they share one home. If he gives his goods as a gift to the one he swore about, or deposits or lends them, then goes out seeking a home and does not find one for days and does not come to the house of his companion, Muhammad said: If he gave the goods as a gift and the other took possession, and he left immediately and does not intend to return, then he is not a cohabitant. Similarly if he deposited the goods and left not intending to return, and likewise a loan. If he has a wife in the house and asks her to leave but she refuses and he cannot force her out, then he does not break his oath by her remaining. If one swears not to cohabit with so-and-so and then cohabits with him in a courtyard, room, or chamber, he breaks his oath. If they cohabit in a house, one in a room, the other in a room; or one in an apartment, the other in an apartment, he breaks his oath, unless it is a large house. Abu Yusuf said: Like Dar al-Raqiq and Dar al-Walid in Kufa, and likewise any large house with separate quarters and apartments. From Muhammad: If one swears not to cohabit with so-and-so and does not specify a house, and each lives in a separate chamber, he does not break the oath unless they live in a single chamber. If one lives in a room of a house and the other in another room, and he swore not to cohabit without specifying a house, he breaks his oath according to their view because the rooms of one house are like one room. Abu Yusuf said: If he cohabits with him in a shop in a market where they work or sell goods, he does not break his oath unless he intended or there is speech indicating it. They said: If one swears not to cohabit with so-and-so in Kufa without intention, and one lives in one house and the other in another house... on the intention of leaving the place and not returning, and moves his goods that establish his dwelling, intending to move the rest, he is said not to be a resident of this place but has moved from it and resides in such-and-such place. Thus Muhammad's view is preferred. This tarjih by this method is taken from al-Fath. His statement: 'And from Muhammad: If one swears not to cohabit...' al-Ramli said: If he swears not to cohabit with him, then cohabits with him in one room or a compartment without family and goods, he does not break his oath, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah quoting al-Zahirah, and it has been presented.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion from the previous one, deriving practical consequences (furu') from the ikhtilaf among the Hanafi imams. First, it addresses the liability of a depositor: according to Abu Hanifa, if the depositor moves out leaving only the deposit, he is not liable because the deposit's presence does not constitute his dwelling; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he is liable in all cases. Next, it discusses an oath not to reside in a specific house: if he resides in one room, he breaks the oath; but if he swears not to reside in it entirely (i.e., the whole house), he does not break the oath until he resides in the entire house, because 'house' is a name for the whole. The author of al-Hidayah requires moving to another place to be free of the oath; moving to the street or mosque is insufficient. Fath al-Qadir notes that with the mashayikh's exception, Abu Hanifa's view aligns with Muhammad's. Faqih Abu al-Layth distinguishes between handing over a rented house (if not handed over, hanth; if handed over, no hanth). The passage then presents supplementary issues from al-Bada'i', covering oaths not to reside (where residing means entering and using goods for sleep) and oaths not to cohabit (musakana). For cohabitation, the ruling depends on whether they share a dwelling. Details include scenarios of giving away goods, depositing, lending, wife's refusal to leave, and large houses. Abu Yusuf gives examples of large houses in Kufa. Muhammad's view is preferred in various sources, especially in matters of intention to leave permanently. The passage concludes with al-Ramli's note that cohabiting in a single room or compartment without family and goods does not break the oath.

Key Terms
المساكنة (al-musakana): Cohabitation: two or more people living in the same home sharing living space.
الوديعة (al-wadi'a): Deposit: an item left for safekeeping by one party with another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad, which of the following conditions is NOT required for cohabitation to end when goods are given as a gift?
A) The goods must be handed over and possession transferred.
B) The person must leave immediately.
C) The person must intend never to return.
D) The gift must be of significant value.

Key Takeaway
Cohabitation oaths are broken only by a complete departure with intention not to return; gift, deposit, or loan of goods is a means, not an end.

Definition of Sakin and Ruling on Transfer
Defines legal residence and effect of transferring household while traveling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'sakin' and apply it to determine whether an oath is broken when a person moves his household while traveling.

Core Idea
A person is not called a resident customarily unless he has moved his family and belongings to that place.

In Hanafi fiqh, the term 'sakin' is defined not by a legal formula but by custom ('urf). This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains how the transfer of one's household while traveling affects the classification of residence for oath purposes. Understanding this ruling helps distinguish between the legal continuity of a homeland (watan) for prayer and the customary residence for oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling Context
The passage addresses an oath concerning residence. Determine what legal question is being answered.
The oath is not to reside in a certain place.
2
Locate the Definition of 'Sakin'
The text defines 'sakin' by custom: having one's family and belongings in a place.
Custom ('urf) is the basis.
3
Distinguish from Other Legal Effects
Note that the continuity of homeland for prayer purposes does not imply residence for oaths.
Different legal objectives.
4
Apply the Definition
Apply to someone who moves his family and belongings and travels: he is no longer considered a resident.
Thus, the oath is not broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإطلاق عدم الحنث أوجه وكون وطنه باقياً في حق إتمام الصلاة ما لم يستوطن غيره لا يستلزم تسميته ساكناً عرفاً بذلك المكان بل يقطع من العرف فيمن نقل أهله وأمتعته وخرج مسافراً أنه لا يقال فيه إنه ساكن ا.هـ.
And declaring that he is not a breaker of the oath is more appropriate. And the fact that his homeland remains for the purpose of completing prayer as long as he does not settle elsewhere does not necessitate calling him a resident in that place customarily. Rather, it is cut off from custom that regarding one who moves his family and belongings and goes out traveling, it is not said of him that he is a resident. End.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that clarifies the definition of 'sakin' (resident) in the context of oaths. The author argues that even if a person's original homeland is considered for prayer shortening, when he moves his family and belongings and departs as a traveler, custom does not consider him a resident of that place. Thus, if he swore not to dwell there, he does not break the oath. This reasoning uses 'urf (custom) to determine the legal status.

Key Terms
ساكن (sākin): One who resides in a place with his family and belongings, as per custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is required for someone to be called a 'sakin' in a place?
A) To have a house there
B) To have his family and belongings there
C) To intend to stay permanently
D) To have completed a prayer there

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the definition of 'sakin' relies on custom: one is a resident only when his family and belongings are present.

Oath on Rented House: Return vs. New Residence
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath-breaking with rented house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking an oath regarding a rented house and distinguish between the positions of Abu al-Layth and Al-Zahiriyyah.

Core Idea
An oath not to dwell in a rented house is broken unless the house is returned AND another residence is taken.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores a nuanced ruling concerning oaths and rented dwellings. Abu al-Layth offered a distinction based on returning the house, but al-Zahiriyyah refined it, requiring the taking of a new residence to avoid breaking the oath. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to apply the principles of oaths (al-ayman) in contemporary rental situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The person swore not to dwell in a rented house. Clarify the exact wording and condition.
The oath is negative: 'I will not dwell in this house.'
2
State Abu al-Layth's position and his condition
Abu al-Layth says if he does not hand over the house to its owners, he breaks the oath; if he does hand over, he does not.
The key condition is returning possession (taslim).
3
State Al-Zahiriyyah's refinement
Al-Zahiriyyah holds that the correct view is that he breaks the oath unless he takes another residence.
This adds a second condition: taking a new dwelling.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and its reasoning
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Al-Zahiriyyah's, as indicated by 'as-sahih' (the correct view).
The rationale is that mere return of the house does not fully sever the connection; a new residence shows true compliance.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Test the ruling with a concrete case, e.g., a tenant who moves out and returns keys but does not lease elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفصل الفقيه أبو الليث تفصيلاً حسناً فقال : إن لم يسلم داره المستأجرة إلى أهلها حنث وإن سلمها لا . وفي الظهيرية : والصحيح أنه يحنث ما لم يتخذ مسكناً آخر.
And the jurist Abu al-Layth made a good distinction. He said: If he does not hand over his rented house to its owners, he breaks the oath; if he does hand it over, he does not. And in Al-Zahiriyyah: The correct view is that he breaks the oath as long as he does not take another residence.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This ruling addresses a person who swore not to dwell in a rented house. Abu al-Layth distinguishes based on whether he returns the house to its owners. If he returns it, he no longer controls it, so he does not break the oath. However, the later text from Al-Zahiriyyah holds that he breaks the oath unless he takes a new residence, implying that merely handing over the keys may not suffice. This reflects a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
حَنِث (ḥanitha): To break an oath, incurring legal consequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Layth, when does a person not break an oath not to dwell in a rented house?
A) When he takes another residence
B) When he hands over the house to its owners
C) When he moves out temporarily
D) When he changes the lock

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that an oath not to dwell in a rented house is broken unless the house is returned AND another residence is taken.

Defining Dwelling (Suknā) for Oaths in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines dwelling for oath purposes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of suknā (dwelling) for oath purposes and apply its two conditions to determine if an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Dwelling requires both physical presence and transfer of essential belongings; neither alone suffices.

When someone swears not to dwell in a house, what exactly counts as 'dwelling'? The Hanafi jurists, as recorded in Al-Badā’i, established a precise definition: mere entry or ownership is not enough. This lesson unpacks that definition and its practical implications for oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
What is at stake? The definition of 'suknā' determines when an oath not to dwell is broken. The text limits dwelling to a specific act.
This is a definitional ruling (ḥukm ta‛rīfī), not a ruling on permissibility.
2
Extract the First Condition: Personal Residence
The text requires 'that he himself lives in it' (يسكنها بنفسه). This means physical inhabitation, not mere ownership or having a key.
Temporary presence like visiting does not count.
3
Extract the Second Condition: Moving In Essential Belongings
He must transfer items used for sleeping (ما يبات فيه) and daily use (ويستعمله في منزله). This indicates material relocation of household goods.
The minimum is items for sleeping and basic living.
4
Understand the Consequence: Breach of Oath
Once both conditions are met, he becomes a breaker (ḥānith). Neither alone triggers breach.
If he sleeps there without belongings, he does not break the oath.
5
Apply the Definition
Consider a scenario: A man swears not to dwell in a house, then moves in his mattress and sleeps there but keeps his other belongings elsewhere. According to the text, he has fulfilled both conditions and broken his oath.
The ruling is based on the custom of dwelling; the text sets a clear standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي البدائع : لو حلف لا يسكن هذه الدار ولم يكن ساكناً فيها فالسكنى فيها أن يسكنها بنفسه وينقل إليها من متاعه ما يبات فيه ويستعمله في منزله، فإذا فعل ذلك فهو حانث
In Al-Bada'i: If he swears not to dwell in this house and he was not a resident in it, then dwelling in it means that he himself lives in it and moves into it from his belongings what he uses to sleep in and uses in his home. So when he does that, he is a breaker.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bada'i provides a clear definition of 'sukna' (dwelling) for oath purposes. It requires both personal presence and transfer of at least sleeping items and daily use items. Once these are moved in, he becomes a breaker of the oath.

Key Terms
سكنى (suknā): The act of dwelling, defined as living in a place and moving in essential belongings for oath purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bada'i, what are the two requirements for dwelling (sukna) in an oath?
A) Sleeping and eating
B) Living there and moving in belongings
C) Buying the house and locking doors
D) Inviting guests and cooking

Key Takeaway
Dwelling for oath purposes requires both personal residence and transfer of essential belongings; one without the other does not break an oath.

The Oath of Musakanah: Fulfillment by Transfer and Intention
Rulings on oaths of musakanah and effect of moving out

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Imam Muhammad's conditions for fulfilling an oath of cohabitation by analyzing scenarios of transferring belongings and intent.

Core Idea
The oath of cohabitation is fulfilled only by immediate physical separation with the intent not to return.

This passage from Al-Bada'i explains the criteria for fulfilling an oath not to cohabit (musakanah) with someone. While the general rule is that one must begin moving out when possible, Imam Muhammad provides specific scenarios where giving, depositing, or lending belongings to the person sworn against, combined with immediate departure and no intention to return, is sufficient. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath and its Condition
The oath is 'not to cohabit with someone.' The key condition for fulfillment is ceasing to share a residence.
Sharing a single house (manzil wahid) defines cohabitation.
2
Understand the General Rule
If the swearer begins to move out (akhadha fi al-nuqlah) and it is possible (mumkinah), he is a fulfiller (birr). Otherwise, he breaks the oath (hanth).
Immediate action is required, not merely intention.
3
Analyze the Gift/Deposit/Loan Scenario
If he gives his belongings to the person sworn about (mahluf 'alayhi), deposits them, or lends them, then leaves the house in search of a new residence, the normal rule applies unless specific conditions are met.
4
Apply Imam Muhammad's Condition
According to Imam Muhammad, if he gives the belongings as a gift, the other takes possession, he leaves that very moment (sa'atahu), and he does not intend to return (laysa min ra'yihi al-'awd), then he is not a cohabitant (fahuwa ghir musakin). The same applies to deposit and lending.
The key is immediate departure and no intention to return.
5
Distinguish from Temporary Absence
If he does not leave immediately or intends to return, even if he has transferred belongings, he remains a cohabitant and the oath is broken.
Intent (niyyah) is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المساكنة فإذا كان رجل ساكناً مع رجل في دار فخلف أحدهما أن لا يساكن صاحبه فإن أخذ في النقلة وهي ممكنة برّ وإلا حنث والنقلة على الخلاف المتقدم فإن لم ينتقل للحال حنث لأن البقاء على المساكنة مساكنة وهو أن يجمعهما منزل واحد، فإن وهب متاعه للمحلوف عليه أو أودعه أو أعاره ثم خرج في طلب منزل فلم يجد منزلاً أياماً ولم يأت الدار التي فيها صاحبه قال محمد : إن كان وهب له المتاع وقبضه منه وخرج منه ساعته وليس من رأيه العود فليس بمساكن، وكذلك إن أودعه المتاع ثم خرج لا يريد العود إلى ذلك المنزل، وكذا العارية
As for cohabitation, if a man is living with another man in a house and one of them swears not to cohabit with his companion, then if he begins to move out and it is possible, he is a fulfiller; otherwise he is a breaker. And the transfer [ruling] follows the previous difference of opinion. If he does not move out immediately, he breaks the oath because remaining in cohabitation is cohabitation, which is that one house brings them together. If he gives his belongings as a gift to the one sworn about, or deposits them, or lends them, then goes out in search of a house and does not find a house for days and does not come to the house where his companion is, Muhammad said: If he gave the belongings as a gift and the other took possession and he left that very moment and does not intend to return, then he is not a cohabitant. Similarly, if he deposits the belongings and then leaves not intending to return to that house, and likewise for lending.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bada'i explains the ruling on an oath not to cohabit (musakanah). The key is actual removal of oneself and one's belongings. If he begins moving when possible, he is a fulfiller. The text also discusses scenarios where he gives away his belongings, deposits them, or lends them, then leaves. According to Imam Muhammad, if he does so with no intention to return, he is not a cohabitant even if he delays finding a new home. This shows that intention and transfer of belongings are crucial.

Key Terms
مساكنة (musākanah): Cohabitation, sharing a residence with someone.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath; oath violation.
برّ (birr): Fulfilling an oath; being true to one's word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, when does giving belongings as a gift avoid breaking an oath of cohabitation?
A) When the gift is given but the giver stays overnight
B) When the gift is given and the giver leaves immediately intending not to return
C) When the gift is given but the recipient is absent
D) When the gift is not accepted

Key Takeaway
To fulfill an oath of non-cohabitation under Imam Muhammad's view, one must transfer belongings and leave immediately with no intention of returning.

Oath of Cohabitation: Spaces Within a House
Rulings on oaths about cohabiting in different areas of a property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths of musakanah when parties reside in different areas of the same property, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
An oath of cohabitation breaks when sharing the same house, unless the house is exceptionally large with separate apartments.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the nuances of oaths involving cohabitation (musakanah). The general rule is that living in the same house, even in different rooms or apartments, breaks the oath. However, an exception is made for very large houses that have distinct, separate living quarters, such as Dar al-Raqiq in Kufa. Within the Hanafi school, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree on the scope of this exception, and the ruling hinges on custom ('urf) and the physical structure of the dwelling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and general ruling
The act is swearing not to cohabit (musakanah) with someone. The general ruling is that cohabitation in any part of the same property—courtyard, house, or room—breaks the oath (hanath).
This applies even if they live in separate rooms or apartments within the same dwelling.
2
Note the exception for large houses
The text states that if the house is 'large' (like Dar al-Raqiq or Dar al-Waleed in Kufa), the oath is not broken even if they live in separate apartments (maqasir). This exception is based on custom and the house's structure.
The exception only applies when the house has clearly distinct, independent living quarters.
3
Analyze the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf focuses on the size of the house: if it is large with separate apartments, it is not considered cohabitation. Muhammad distinguishes between separate houses and separate rooms within one house: if they are in different rooms of the same house, he still considers it cohabitation unless they are in entirely separate houses.
Their disagreement results in different practical outcomes for moderately large houses.
4
Understand the basis of the ruling
The proof is based on custom ('urf) and the analogy (qiyas) that rooms of a single house are like one room for cohabitation purposes, unless the house is exceptionally large.
The ruling is not purely textual but relies on the Hanafi method of considering 'urf and ma'na.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a specific scenario (e.g., a large mansion with separate apartments), determine whether the oath is broken according to each imam's view.
The practical application requires knowing the size and layout of the dwelling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً فساكنه في عرصة دار أو بيت أو غرفة حنث، فإن ساكنه دار هذا في ا في حجرة وهذا في حجرة أو هذا في منزل وهذا في منزل حنث إلا أن تكون داراً كبيرة. قال أبو يوسف مثل دار الرقيق ودار الوليد بالكوفة، وكذا كل دار عظيمة فيها مقاصير ومنازل، وعن محمد: إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً ولم يسم داراً فسكن هذا في حجرة وهذا في حجرة لم يحنث إلا أن يساكنه في حجرة واحدة فإن سكن هذا في بيت من دار وهذا في بيت آخر وقد حلف لا يساكنه ولم يسم داراً حنث في قولهم لأن بيوت الدار الواحدة كالبيت الواحد
And if he swears not to cohabit with someone, and he cohabits with him in the courtyard of a house, or a house, or a room, he breaks the oath. If he cohabits with him in a house, one in a room and the other in a room, or one in an apartment and the other in an apartment, he breaks the oath unless it is a large house. Abu Yusuf said: Like the house of slaves and the house of al-Waleed in Kufa, and likewise every grand house that has many chambers and apartments. And from Muhammad: If he swears not to cohabit with someone and does not name a house, and one lives in a room and the other in a room, he does not break the oath unless they cohabit in one room. If one lives in a room of a house and the other in another room, and he swore not to cohabit with him and did not name a house, he breaks the oath in their opinion because the rooms of one house are like one room.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage deals with how different divisions of a property affect an oath of musakanah. The general rule is that being in the same house, even in different rooms, breaks the oath unless the house is exceptionally large like the mentioned examples. There is a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf considers large houses with separate apartments as not constituting cohabitation. Muhammad distinguishes based on whether the entire house is one unit or not. The ruling depends on custom and the specific structure.

Key Terms
عرصة (ʿarṣah): Courtyard of a house.
مقصورة (maqṣūrah): Private chamber or apartment within a large house.
مساكنة (musākanah): Cohabitation; living together in the same dwelling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a large house that does NOT make cohabitation (musakanah) when living in separate apartments?
A) A small one-story house
B) Dar al-Raqiq in Kufa
C) A two-room house
D) A rented apartment in a building

Key Takeaway
In oaths of cohabitation, the physical structure of the dwelling determines whether the oath is broken; large houses with separate apartments may not constitute cohabitation, according to Abu Yusuf, while Muhammad requires separate houses.

When an Oath of Cohabitation is Broken
Ruling on oath specifying a location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which an oath not to cohabit in a specific location is broken according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Cohabitation (musakanah) requires living in the same house; same area does not break the oath.

This lesson examines a precise Hanafi ruling on oaths tied to a specific location. The key is the definition of 'cohabitation' – it is not mere vicinity but actual shared dwelling. Understanding this protects against unwitting oath-breaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and condition
The person swears not to cohabit with someone 'in Kufa'. The ruling hinges on what constitutes cohabitation.
No intention is mentioned, so the literal meaning applies.
2
Define cohabitation
Cohabitation (musakanah) is defined as mixing (mukhalatah) within a single dwelling. Being in the same tribe, neighborhood, or lane does not count.
This is a linguistic and customary definition.
3
Apply the rule
Therefore, the oath is only broken when both parties live under one roof. If they live in separate houses even on the same street, the oath remains intact.
The location 'Kufa' does not change this requirement; it merely specifies where the cohabitation is forbidden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : إذا حلف لا يساكن فلاناً بالكوفة ولا نية له فسكن أحدهما في دار والآخر في دار أخرى في قبيلة واحدة أو محلة واحدة أو درب واحد فإنه لا يحنث حتى يجمعهما السكنى في دار لأن المساكنة المخالطة
They said: If he swears not to cohabit with someone in Kufa and has no intention, and one lives in one house and the other in another house in the same tribe or neighborhood or lane, then he does not break the oath until the dwelling brings them together in one house, because cohabitation means mixing.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that when an oath specifies a location (Kufa), merely being in the same area or neighborhood does not constitute cohabitation. The key is living in the same house. The term 'musakanah' implies close proximity and mixing (mukhalatah).

Key Terms
المخالطة (al-mukhālaṭah): Mixing, intimate association required for cohabitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When they live in the same neighborhood
B) When they live in the same lane
C) When they live in the same house
D) When they live in the same city

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath not to cohabit in a specific location is only broken when the two parties share the same dwelling, due to the requirement of mixing (mukhalatah).
