Oath of Cohabitation in Ships and Tents
Rulings on cohabitation oaths in ships and tents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on when an oath not to cohabit is broken, specifically in non‑standard dwellings like ships and tents.

Core Idea
The determining factor for cohabitation is sharing a single dwelling unit, not mere proximity.

This lesson examines two unusual living situations—a sailor living aboard a ship and Bedouins in tents. The Hanafi school clarifies that the oath of non‑cohabitation (yamīn ʿalā ʿadam al‑musākanah) is broken only when the parties literally occupy the same structure as their home. Proximity alone, even if very close, does not constitute cohabitation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
In each scenario, determine whether the oath is broken (hanth) or not. For the sailor who makes a ship his permanent home with family and belongings, the oath is broken. For Bedouins, if they share one tent, the oath is broken; if tents are separate, it is not.
2
Distinguish the key condition
The decisive factor is whether both parties share the same physical dwelling as a residence. The ship, when used as a home, becomes a dwelling. A single tent is a dwelling; separate tents are separate dwellings.
Proximity is irrelevant; only unity of the shelter matters.
3
Apply the analogy
Extend the principle: any structure that serves as a permanent home can trigger the oath if both parties reside in it. Temporary or non‑residential use (e.g., a ship for travel) does not break the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلف الملاح أن لا يساكن فلاناً في سفينة فنزل مع كل أهله ومتاعه واتخذها منزله حنث، وكذلك أهل البادية إذا جمعتهم خيمة وإن تفرقت الخيام لم يحنث وإن تقاربت
And if a sailor swears not to cohabit with someone in a ship, and he goes down with all his family and belongings and makes it his home, he breaks the oath. Similarly, the people of the desert, if a tent gathers them together (they break the oath), and if the tents are separate, they do not break the oath even if they are close.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage applies the same principle to unconventional homes. A ship used as a residence with all household goods breaks the oath. For Bedouins, sharing a single tent constitutes cohabitation, but separate tents do not, even if adjacent. The ruling is based on the sharing of a single dwelling unit.

Key Terms
خيمة (khaymah): Tent, a portable dwelling used by Bedouins.
سفينة (safinah): Ship, vessel.
مساكنة (musakanah): Cohabitation, living together in the same dwelling.

Definitions of Iwa and Mabit in Oaths
Defines iwa and mabit and their effect on oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between iwa and mabit and apply their definitions to determine if an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Iwa is any stay; mabit requires most of the night.

When someone swears not to lodge (iwa) or not to spend the night (mabit) in a place, the precise meaning of these terms determines whether the oath is broken. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi definitions, focusing on duration and the role of sleep.

Guided Path
1
Define iwa
Read the definition: iwa means being present in a place for any duration, day or night.
No minimum time required.
2
Define mabit
Mabit requires spending more than half of the night at the location. Sleep is not required.
If less than half, no breach.
3
Analyze the time threshold
Consider a scenario where most of the night has already passed before arrival. According to Muhammad, if two-thirds are gone, the remaining stay does not constitute mabit.
Oath may be invalid if impossible.
4
Consider intention
For iwa, if the swearer intends a longer stay (e.g., 'overnight'), his intention controls. For mabit, no intention is mentioned; the objective definition applies.
Intention can restrict the general term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا حلف أنه لا يأوي مع فلان أو لا يأوي في مكان أو دار أو بيت فالإيواء الكون ماكثاً في مكان قليلاً كان المكث أو كثيراً، ليلاً كان أو نهاراً، فإن نوى أكثر من ذلك فهو على ما نوى، فإذا حلف لا يبيت مع فلان أو لا يبيت في مكان كذا فالمبيت بالليل حتى يكون منه أكثر لم نصف الليل، وإن كان أقل لم يحنث، وسواء نام في الموضع أو لم ينم. فلو حلف لا يبيت الليلة في هذه الدار وقد ذهب ثلثا الليل ثم بات بقية ليلته قال محمد: لا يحنث لأن البيتوتة إذا كانت تقع على أكثر الليل فقد حلف على ما يتصور فلم تنعقد يمينه ا هـ
And if he swears that he will not lodge with someone or not lodge in a place or house or room, then lodging (iwa) is being staying in a place, whether the stay is little or much, night or day. If he intends more than that, it is according to his intention. If he swears not to spend the night (mabit) with someone or not to spend the night in a certain place, then spending the night is at night so that most of the night is spent (more than half). If it is less, he does not break the oath, whether he sleeps in the place or not. So if he swears not to spend this night in this house and two-thirds of the night have passed and he then stays the remainder of the night, Muhammad said: He does not break the oath because spending the night is when it covers most of the night; he has sworn about something that is not possible, so his oath is not valid (did not take effect). End.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between iwa (lodging) and mabit (overnight stay). Iwa is any stay, regardless of duration. Mabit requires spending more than half the night, and it does not require sleep. If most of the night has already passed before he enters, he does not break the oath because his action does not constitute mabit. Imam Muhammad's opinion on the invalid oath is noted.

Key Terms
إيواء (īwāʾ): Lodging; staying in a place for any period.
مبيت (mabīt): Overnight stay; spending most of the night.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is required for mabit (overnight stay) to break an oath?
A) Sleeping in the place
B) Spending most of the night there
C) Being there at midnight
D) Spending the entire night

Key Takeaway
Iwa breaks an oath with any stay; mabit requires spending more than half the night, regardless of sleep.

Duration Required for Musakanah (Cohabitation) – 15-Day Rule
States minimum duration (15 days) for cohabitation to be established

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum duration for cohabitation in Hanafi fiqh and apply the rule to scenarios involving oaths of non-cohabitation.

Core Idea
Cohabitation requires 15 days; less is not residence for oaths.

This passage from Al-Waqi'at tackles a practical question: if someone swears not to cohabit with another, what constitutes cohabitation? The Hanafi school sets a clear threshold of 15 days. We'll examine the ruling, its analogical basis, and an intra-Hanafi difference regarding a traveling spouse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing not to cohabit (musakanah). The ruling: the oath is not broken unless cohabitation is established.
Cohabitation here means living together in the same dwelling.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is analogical (qiyas) to the ruling on intending to stay in a city as a traveler.
The analogy is explicit: 'This is comparable to if he swore not to dwell in Kufa...'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is the duration of stay: 15 days determines residence. Just as 15 days' intention in a city breaks an oath, so does 15 days' cohabitation.
The analogy is clear (qiyas jaliy).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the swearer travels while his family stays with the other, Abu Hanifa says the oath breaks; Abu Yusuf says it does not.
Abu Yusuf's argument: the swearer did not actually cohabit.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text states 'wa 'alayhi al-fatwa' – the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view.
This is a relied-upon position in later Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يساكن فلاناً فنزل منزله فمكث فيه يوماً أو يومين لا يحنث لأنه لا يكون ساكناً معه حتى يقيم معه في منزله خمسة عشر يوماً، وهذا بمنزلة ما لو حلف لا يسكن الكوفة فمر بها مسافراً فنوى أربعة عشر يوماً لا يحنث، فإن نوى خمسة عشر يوماً يحنث . ولو سافر الحالف فسكن فلان أهله ; قال أبو حنيفة يحنث، وقال أبو يوسف لا وعليه الفتوى لأن الحالف لم يساكنه حقيقة ا هـ . وفي الظهيرية : لو حلف لا يساكن فلاناً فدخل فلان دار الحالف غصباً فأقام الحالف معه حنث، علم الحالف بذلك أو لم يعلم. وإن الحالف بأهله وأخذ بالنقل حين نزل الغاصب لم يحنث
And in Al-Waqi'at: He swore not to cohabit with someone, and he dismounted at his house and stayed there for a day or two – he does not break the oath because he is not a resident with him until he stays with him in his house for fifteen days. This is comparable to if he swore not to dwell in Kufa and he passed by it as a traveler and intended to stay fourteen days – he does not break the oath; if he intended fifteen days, he breaks it. And if the swearer traveled and his family stayed (with the other), Abu Hanifa said: he breaks the oath; Abu Yusuf said: no, and the fatwa is upon his view because the swearer did not actually cohabit. End. And in Al-Zahiriyyah: If he swore not to cohabit with someone, and that person entered the swearer's house forcibly, and the swearer remained with him, he breaks the oath, whether the swearer knew or not. If the swearer immediately moved out with his family when the usurper settled, he does not break the oath.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage from Al-Waqi'at establishes that cohabitation (musakanah) requires a minimum duration of 15 consecutive days. This is analogized to the ruling on intending to stay in a city as a traveler. It also discusses a scenario where the other party forcibly enters the swearer's house; if the swearer does not leave, he breaks the oath, but if he moves out immediately, he does not. There is an intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the case where the swearer travels while his family remains; Abu Yusuf's view is taken for fatwa.

Key Terms
خمسة عشر يوماً (khamsata ʿashara yawman): Fifteen days, the threshold for being considered a resident for cohabitation purposes.
يساكن (yusākinu): He cohabits, lives together with someone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How long must one stay in another's house to be considered a cohabitant for an oath?
A) Three days
B) Seven days
C) Fifteen days
D) One month

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, cohabitation (musakanah) is only established after a continuous stay of 15 days, and lesser stays do not break an oath of non-cohabitation.

Defining Cohabitation (Musakanah) in Oaths
Defines when an oath not to cohabit is broken

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of cohabitation (musakanah) and apply it to determine when an oath not to cohabit is broken.

Core Idea
Cohabitation requires shared dwelling; mere proximity or affiliation does not suffice.

When someone swears not to cohabit with another, what exactly constitutes cohabitation? The Hanafi school defines it strictly: actual shared residence under one roof. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that common tribe, locality, or even alley does not break such an oath. The ruling turns on the linguistic and customary meaning of 'musakanah' as mixing (mukhalatah) in a single dwelling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and ruling
The act is an oath (yamin) not to cohabit. The ruling (hukm) is the consequence: the oath is broken (hanth) when cohabitation actually occurs.
This is a conditional ruling: if X happens, then Y.
2
Extract the definition of musakanah
The passage defines musakanah as 'shared residence in a house' (جمعهما السكنى في دار). The rationale is that musakanah implies mixing (mukhalatah). Thus, mere belonging to the same tribe, locality, or alley is insufficient.
The definition is based on linguistic derivation (ish-tiqaq) from suknah (dwelling).
3
Apply the definition to specific cases
The text provides three applications: (1) Boatman: if he and his family take a ship as their home, that constitutes cohabitation. (2) Bedouins: a single shared tent breaks the oath; separate tents do not, even if close. (3) City specification: mentioning a specific city (e.g., Kufa) limits the oath to that city.
Each case tests the boundary of 'shared dwelling'.
4
Analyze the proof (dalil) for the definition
The proof is qiyas (analogy) based on the linguistic meaning of musakanah. The term itself indicates dwelling; thus, cohabitation must involve a shared physical dwelling. This is a clear example of talil (ratio legis) derived from language.
The dalil is zanni (probable) in transmission and meaning, but sufficient for fiqh rulings.
5
Consider cross-madhhab implications
The text acknowledges potential disagreement: other schools may define cohabitation more broadly (e.g., proximity in a locality). However, the Hanafi position is firmly rooted in the linguistic and customary understanding.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences in oath enforcement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبيلة واحدة أو محلة واحدة أو درب واحد فإنه لا يحنث حتى يجمعهما السكنى في دار لأن المساكنة المخالطة، وذكر الكوفة لتخصيص اليمين بها حتى لا يحنث بمساكنته في غيرها. ولو حلف الملاح أن لا يساكن فلاناً في سفينة فنزل مع كل أهله ومتاعه واتخذها منزله حنث، وكذلك أهل البادية إذا جمعتهم خيمة وإن تفرقت الخيام لم يحنث وإن تقاربت.
One tribe or one locality or one alley; he does not break his oath until they share residence in a house, because cohabitation is mixing. And the mention of Kufa is to specify the oath to it so that he does not break by cohabiting elsewhere. And if a boatman swore not to cohabit with someone in a ship, and he disembarks with all his family and belongings and takes it as his home, he breaks. Similarly, desert dwellers if a tent gathers them, but if the tents are separate he does not break even if they are close.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on oaths regarding cohabitation (musakanah). The author states that the key to breaking such an oath is actual shared dwelling in a single house. Merely belonging to the same tribe, locality, or alley does not constitute cohabitation. The rationale is that cohabitation implies mixing (mukhalatah). Specifying a city like Kufa limits the oath to that city. The example of the boatman shows that if he takes the ship as his home with his family and belongings, it counts as a dwelling. For bedouins, a single tent shared breaks the oath, but separate tents do not, even if close.

Key Terms
المساكنة (al-musakanah): Cohabitation, sharing a dwelling with someone
محلة (mahallah): Locality or neighborhood

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to cohabit with a person, and they live in the same tribe but different houses, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because they are in the same tribe.
B) Yes, if they are in the same locality.
C) No, not until they share the same house.
D) No, never.

Key Takeaway
Musakanah (cohabitation) is defined by shared dwelling; oaths not to cohabit are only broken when actual shared residence occurs.

Fifteen-day Rule for Cohabitation in Oaths
States that cohabitation requires a minimum stay of 15 days.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fifteen-day threshold for cohabitation in Hanafi oaths and apply it to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
A temporary stay under fifteen days does not break an oath not to cohabit.

This passage from al-Waqi'at, cited in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, establishes a key quantitative rule for oaths related to cohabitation. It clarifies the minimum period required for a person to be considered a cohabitant (musakin) and thus break an oath. The reasoning is based on custom ('urf) and analogy to oaths regarding residence in a city.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is an oath not to cohabit with someone. The ruling: staying one or two days does not break the oath; the minimum to break it is fifteen days.
2
Analyze the analogy
The passage compares this to an oath not to reside in Kufa. Passing through with intent of fourteen days does not break the oath, but fifteen days does. The analogy shows that the same threshold applies.
3
Understand the basis in custom
The fifteen-day period is not arbitrary; it is derived from common understanding of what constitutes cohabitation or residence. Hanafis use 'urf as a source of law in such matters.
Cross-madhhab: other schools may adopt different thresholds based on their own customs or textual interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يساكن فلاناً فنزل منزله فمكث فيه يوماً أو يومين لا يحنث لأنه لا يكون ساكناً معه حتى يقيم معه في منزله خمسة عشر يوماً، وهذا بمنزلة ما لو حلف لا يسكن الكوفة فمر بها مسافراً فنوى أربعة عشر يوماً لا يحنث، فإن نوى خمسة عشر يوماً يحنث.
And in al-Waqi'at: He swore not to cohabit with so-and-so, then alighted at his house and stayed a day or two; he does not break the oath because he is not considered a cohabitant with him until he stays with him in his house for fifteen days. This is like the case where he swore not to reside in Kufa, and he passed through it as a traveler intending fourteen days; he does not break. If he intended fifteen days, he breaks.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage from al-Waqi'at, cited in the commentary, establishes a quantitative criterion for cohabitation: a stay of fifteen days or more. A shorter stay, even if overnight, does not transform one into a 'sakin' (resident). The analogy to an oath not to reside in a city clarifies: passing through with an intention of less than fifteen days does not break the oath, but fifteen days or more does. This ruling is based on custom ('urf) and the literal meaning of 'sukna' (residence).

Key Terms
المساكنة (al-musakanah): Cohabitation requiring a minimum fifteen-day stay
السكنى (al-sukna): Residence; dwelling in a place

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears not to cohabit with his brother. He stays at his brother's house for fourteen days. According to the Hanafi school, has he broken his oath?
A) Yes, because he stayed for two weeks.
B) No, because the stay was less than fifteen days.
C) Yes, because he intended to stay more than a week.
D) No, because he did not stay for a month.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath not to cohabit is broken only after fifteen continuous days of residence, based on custom and analogy.
